THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN; A POEM, IN ELEVEN BOOKS.

BY TIMOTHY DWIGHT.

Fired, at first sight, with what the Muse imparts,
In fearless youth we tempt the height of arts.
POPE. HARTFORD: PRINTED BY ELISHA BABCOCK. M, DCC, LXXXV.

To his EXCELLENCY, GEORGE WASHINGTON, ESQUIRE, Commander in chief of the
American Armies, The Savior of his Country, The Supporter of Freedom, And the
Benefactor of Mankind;
This Poem is inscribed, with the highest respect for his character, the most
ardent wishes for his happiness, and the most grateful sense of the blessings,
secured, by his generous efforts, to the United States of North America,

by his most humble, and most obedient servant, TIMOTHY DWIGHT.
Greenfield, in Connecticut, March 1, 1785.
AS this poem is the first of the kind, which has been published in this
country, the writer begs leave to introduce it with several observations, which
that circumstance alone may perhaps render necessary.

He has taken to himself the liberty of altering the real order of the two last
battles, because he imagined the illustrious events, which attended the battle
of Gibeon, would make it appear to be the catastrophe of the poem, wherever
inserted.

He has varied the story of the embassy from Gibeon, for reasons, which he
thinks will be obvious to every reader, and which he hopes will be esteemed his
sufficient justification.

To give entire Unity to the Action, he has made Jabin the Canaanitish hero
through the whole poem; and has transferred the scene of the battle, between
Hazor and Israel, from the shores of the lake Merom to the neighborhood of Ai.

In the Manners, he has studied a medium between absolute barbarism and modern
refinement. In the best characters, he has endeavored to represent such
manners, as are removed from the peculiarities of any age, or country, and
might belong to the amiable and virtuous, of every age: such as are elevated
without design, refined without ceremony, elegant without fashion, and
agreeable, because they are ornamented with sincerity, dignity, and religion,
not because they are polished by art and education. Of such manners, he hopes
he may observe, without impropriety, that they possess the highest advantages
for universal application.

He has made use of Rhyme, because he believed it would be more generally
relished than blank verse, even amongst those who are esteemed persons of tas^e.

It may perhaps be thought the result of inattention or ignorance, that he
chose a subject, in which his countrymen had no national interest. But he
remarked that the Iliad and Eneid were as agreeable to modern nations, as to
the Greeks and Romans. The reason he suppo^ed to be obvious -- the subjects of
those poem^ furnish the (...) opportunities of exhibiting the agreeable* the
novel* the moral, the pathetic, and the sublime. If he i^ not deceived, the
subject he has chosen possesses, in a degree, the same advantages.

It will be observed that he has introduced some new words, and annexed to some
old ones, a new signification. This liberty, allowed to others, he hopes will
not be refused to him: especially as from this source the copiousness and
refinement of language have been principally derived.

That he wishes to please he frankly confesses. If he fails in the design, it
will be a satisfaction that he shall have i^jured no person but himself. As the
poem is uniform^y friendly to delicacy, and virtue, he hopes his countrymen
will so far regard him with candor, as not to impute it to him as a fault,
that he has endeavored to please them, and has thrown in his mite, for the
advancement of the refined arts, on this side of the Atlantic.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK I.

ARGUMENT.
Subject proposed. Invocation. After the battle, mentioned in the beginning of
the seventh chapter of Joshua, the Israelites, in correspondence with the
sacred history* are represented in circumstances of extreme distress. With this
event the poem opens, in the evening. Morning. Scene of war. Story of Zimri,
and Aram. Zimri returns to the assembly of Israel, and brings an account of the
death of Aram, and of an army, sent by Jabin, king of Hazor, to assist Ai.
Distress of the Israelites. Character and oration of Hanniel. After a pathetic
address, and rehearsal of their miseries, he attempts to prove the
impossibility of succeeding in their present design, because of the strength,
skill, and numerous allies of their enemies; foretells their approaching ruin,
asserts that GOD is opposed to them, that they were led out of Egypt to silence
their murmurs, and, the end being accomplished, ought to return. Panegyric on
that country; obviates objections to a return, and informs them that, if they
should conquer Canaan, they will be ruined, during the war, by the necessary
neglect of arts and agriculture, difficulty of dividing the land, of settling a
form of government, and of avoiding tyranny; and concludes with a new
exhortation to return to Egypt. Applause. Joshua replies, and beginning to
explain the dispensations of Providence, is interrupted by Hanniel, who first
obliquely, and then openly accuses him of aiming at the usurpation of kingly
authority; and asserts the return to be easy. Joshua vindicates his innocence
with severity upon Hanniel; and allowing they can return, paints to them the
miseri^s, they will experience from the Egyptian king, lords, people, and
manners, and from providential dispensations terminating in their ruin. He
appeals to them to judge of the fal^ehood of Hanniel's ideas of the purposes of
Heaven, in leading them out of Egypt; and declares the certainty of their
success from their union, with a few exceptions, their previous prosperity, and
the ^avour and revealed ^esigns of Heaven, and exalts in their future glory.
App^^use. Preparation for war. Caleb opposes immediate war, and advises a fast
of two days. Jos^ua approves of it.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK I.
THE Chief, whose arm to Israel's chosen band
Gave the fair empire of the promised land,
Ordained by Heaven to hold the sacred sway,
Demands my voice and animates the lay.
O thou, whose Love, high throned above all height,
Illumes the immense, and ^unn^ the world of light;
Whose distant beam the human mind inspires,
With wisdom brightens, and with virtue sires;
Unfold how pious realms to glory rise,
And impious nations find avenging skies:
May thy own deeds exalt the humble line,
And not a stain obscure the theme divine.
When now from western hills the sun was driven,
And night expanding filled the bounds of heaven,
Over I^rael's camp ten thousand fires appeared
And solemn cries from distant guards were heard,
Her tribes, escaped from Ai's unhappy plain,
With shame and anguish mourned their heroes slain,
Line 1.) Wherever Chief, Hero* Leader, etc. with a capit^l, respect the
Israelitish army, Joshua is intended; when th^^ respect ^he (...) army, J^bin
is intended. The (...), wi^^ a (...).
Pierced with deep wounds the groaning warriors stood;
Their bosoms heaved, their tears incessant flowed;
Their sons unburied on the hostile plain,
Their brothers captived, and their parents slain.
The tender father clasped his lovely child,
That thoughtless-sporting innocently smiled,
To his fond arms with soft endearments leapt,
Gazed on his tears, and wondered why he wept.
Her woes with his the trembling mother joined,
Edged all his fears, and sunk his drooping mind,
Arrayed in tenfold gloom the approaching light,
And gathered foes unnumber'd to the fight.
Thus trembling, sad, of every hope forlorn,
The hapless thousands watched the coming morn.
In Joshua's ear their sad complaints resound,
As slow, unseen, he traced the camp around.
Wherever shrill cries, or groans distinguished flowed
Propp'd on his lance, the Hero listening stood:
For often the secret hour of night he chose,
To hush their tumults, and to learn their woes;
Each tear, each cry his feeling mind oppressed,
And schemes of pity filled his laboring breast.
And now bright Phosphor waked the dawning day,
The tents all whitening in the expanded ray;
The sun's broad beam the scene of war displayed,
A wide extent, with distant groves overspread;
A tall, dark forest gloom'd the northern round,
And eastern hills over hills the horizon bound:
Far south, a plain in vivid green (...),
And one un^^ried level filled the view;
Beyond, Ai's grandeur proudly rose on high,
And azure mountains pierced the western sky.
Around their Leader's tent, the unnumber'd train
Thronged from the camp, and gathered on the plain*
When Zimri slow approached; of Asher's race
The first in merit, as the first in place.
Him, not a chief, that dared the battling field,
In swiftness equaled, or in strength excelled;
Save Joshua's arm, that still unconquered shone;
From every rival every prize he won.
In night^s last gloom (so Joshua's will ordained)
To find what hopes the cautious foe remained,
Or what new strength, allied, increased their force,
To Ai's high walls the hero bent his course.
Aram, his friend, unknowing vile dismay,
With willing footsteps shared the dangerous way.
In virtue joined, one soul to both was given;
Each steered his path, and led his friend to heaven.
Over earth's dim verge as dawned the cheerful day,
Near slumbering Ai they cours'd their fearless way;
Unseen, in twining shrubs, a heathen sat,
Marked their still path, and boded Aram's fate;
Swift hurled, his javelin sought the hero's side,
Pierced to the heart, he groaned, and gasp'd, and died.
The heathen flew, fierce Zimri clave his breast,
But Aram's eyes were closed in endless rest.
Thus, while fond Virtue wished in vain to save,
Hale, bright and generous, found a hapless grave.
With genius' living flame his bosom glowed,
And science charmed him to her sweet abode:
In worth's fair path his feet adventured far;
The pride of peace, the rising grace of war;
In duty firm, in danger calm as even,
To friends unchanging, and sincere to heaven.
75.) While, amid, and among, are used throughout this poem, euph. great.
instead of whilst, amidst, and amongst.
76. Hale bright) The comparisons of this kind were all written in the early
stages of the late war, and annexed to the poem to indulge the Author's own
emotions of regard to the persons named in them. As it was impossible to pay
this little tribute of respect to all the deserving characters, who have fallen
in defense of American liberty, the Author determined to desist, after the
first attempt. The lines on Major Andre are an exception to the above remark,
as are those on General Mercer.
How short his course, the prize how early won!
While weeping friendship mourns her favorite gone.
With soul too noble for so base a cause,
Thus Andre bowed to war's barbarian laws.
In morn's fair light the opening blossom warmed,
Its beauty smiled, its growing fragrance charmed;
Fierce roared the untimely blast around its head;
The beauty vanished, and the fragrance fled;
Soon sunk his graces in the wintry tomb,
And sad Columbia wept his hapless doom.
As now over eastern hills the morning burned,
Alone brave Zimri to the camp returned;
Pale in his front despair and anguish sat,
And each kind bosom feared for Aram's fate,
When thus, the Leader -- Say, exalted chief.
What dire misfortune clouds thy mind with grief?
O best of men, he cried, my tears deplore
The hero's sat, brave Aram is no more.
Weep, weep, my friends: his worthy life demands
This last, poor tribute from your grateful hands,
Nor weep for him alone: dread scenes of grief
Surround our steps, and Heaven denies relief.
The insulting wretch, that sealed the hero's fate,
In death proclaimed what terrors round you wait.
I die, he cried, but know, thou culprit, know,
To the dark tomb thy harbinger I go.
Over Israel's race ascend, from realms afar,
The clouds of ruin, and the storms of war.
The hosts, that bow to Jabin's great control
From Hazor's rocky hills, in thunder roll;
Hosts, that never knew the tender tear to sh^d,
Born in the field, beneath the standard bred;
That raptured fly, where shrilling trumpets call,
Plunge on the pointed spear, and climb the kindled wall.
These dauntless bands (to Ai the message came)
Shall sink in night thy nation's hated name;
Even now brave Oran, Jabin's martial boast,
Speeds his glad course and moves a countless host:
Raptured I see thy camp in flames arise,
And Israel's ashes cloud the angry skies.
He spoke. Astonished at the impending doom,
Round the pale thousands breathed a solemn gloom*
Rent were their martial vestments, torn their hair,
And every eye spoke pangs of keen despair.
Mid the sad throng, in mournful robes arrayed
Vile dust besprinkled over his down-cast head,
Pale Hanniel rose, and with dissembled woe,
Clouded his front, and urged the tear to flow.
Of princely blood* his haughty ^ire, of yore
Proud Pharaoh's favorite on the Egyptian shore,
Over Israel's race was sceptered to preside,
To rule their tributes and their toils to guide.
In the son's mind again the parent lived,
His pride rekindled, and his art revived.
Wherever pride called, his changing soul would turn;
Grieve with the sad, and with the envious burn;
Vaunt with the (...) with the wise,
And cheat the pio^^ with uplifted eyes;
In Youth's fond sports with seeming zeal engage,
Or list, delighted, to the tales of Age.
When Joshua's hand the sacred rule adorned,
With pangs he saw, but still in secret mourned:
His close revenge the Hero's fate decreed,
And smooth, sure slander taught his name to bleed.
With friendly grasp he squeezed each warrior's hand;
With jests familiar pleased the vulgar band;
In (...), sh^ewd hints the Leader's faults disclosed;
Praised his whole sway, but single acts opposed;
Admired how law so stern a face could wear;
Styled combat rashness, and named caution fear:
With angels the^ his fame and virtue joined,
To tempt coarse scandal from each envious mind:
Blessed his own peaceful lot, and smiled, that Heaven,
To minds, that prized them, empire's toils had given.
Yet base-born fear his vigorous soul disdained;
Each danger shared and every toil sustained;
Joy'd, in terrific fields, the foe to dare,
And claimed the honors of the fiercest war.
Now the blessed period, long in vain desired,
His fond hope flattered, and his bosom fired;
To end his rival's sway, his own secure,
Resolved, his fancy deemed the triumph sure.
In seeming anguish often his hands he wrung,
And words imperfect murmured on his tongue;
At length, with feeble voice, he thus began,
While round the tribes a mute attention ran.
Friends! brethren! sires! or by what tenderer name
Shall I address the heirs of Jacob's fame?
Dear to my soul, as those red drops, that flow,
Through my warm veins, and bid my bosom glow,
If chilled by grief's cold hand, the vital flood
Still pours its warmth, nor yet forsakes the road!
Long has this heart with deep compassion viewed
Your generous tribes, by countless ills subdued;
Ills, these pained eyes foreboding, long beheld,
And this sad warning voice in vain revealed.
Those counsels, now by sure experience proved,
That voice, alone by Israel's welfare moved,
Once more attend. Ye guardian powers, be near,
Enlarge their minds and give them hearts to hear!
Let base-born prejudice no more control
The native candor of each generous soul;
Assert yourselves; your future conduct scan;
Reason's the noblest privilege of man.
Long have our feet with restless error roved,
And the sad waste with all its miseries proved;
That waste, by Heaven's unerring sentence cursed
With ceaseless hunger, and eternal thirst,
The tyger's rage, the lion's fearful path,
Bestrew'd with bones, and red with recent death,
The sun's keen fury, midnight's gloomy dread,
And all the horrors of the impoison'd shade.
How often those eyes the hapless child have viewed,
By hunger famished and by pain subdued,
While the fond parent over his beauties hung,
And looked distress, that froze his faltering tongue,
Distress, to hear the young, the piercing cry,
That claimed relief, when no relief was nigh;
To see the babe, its face with death overspread,
Stretch forth its little hands, and sue for bread:
While friends, all impotent, rolled down the tear,
Rocks learned to feel, and forests bent to hear.
When pale Disease assumed her fatal reign,
Chased the warm glow, and racked the joint^ with pain,
Often have these failing eyes the chief beheld,
In counsel famed, and glorious in the field,
Condemned the pangs of sickness to endure,
Far from relief, and hopeless of a cure;
No downy couch to rest his drooping head,
The skies his covering, and the earth his bed;
No softening plant his stiffen'd wounds to heal,
Soothe his racked nerves, and learn them not to feel;
Nor sweet, embowering shade to drive away
Night's baleful damps, and summer's scorching ray.
But who the various ills can number over.
Or tell the sands that form the sea-beat shore?
Even now by slow degrees our thousands fall,
Till one wide, common grave involve us all.
For see what woes surround our daring course,
That tempts the terrors of unmeasured force;
Safe in high walls, insulting foes deride,
Our boastful impotence, and banner'd pride;
On boundless wealth, with careless ease, rely,
And hosts unnumber'd never taught to fly;
Proud of the dreadful steed, the wasting car,
And all the strength, and all the art, of war.
These foes to aid, what countless throngs will join!
What peopled realms against our arms combine!
From Gibeon's walls, and where tremendous powers
Surround imperial Hazor's hundred towers,
Or where proud shores the western main behold,
Or orient Gihon's haughty tides are rolled,
I see to fearful combat millions rise,
Chiefs mount the car, and point the fated prize;
See in the vanguard haughty Conquest ride
Lo, murdered thousands pour the ruddy tide!
Over Israel's camp the clouds of vengeance lower,
Fear wings our flight, and flames our race devour.
At that dread season, chained in bonds forlorn,
Of men the proverb, and of Heaven the scorn,
Hissed by ^ile slaves, our tribes the rack shall feel,
Or gasp, (...) ^appier, on the griding steel:
Slow round the ^orm the fires of Molock burn;
Chiefs mount the pile, and babes to ashes turn:
Impaled with anguish, bleeding sires behold,
Their wives polluted and their virgins sold;
Their sons, sweet solace of declining age,
In sport tran^fix'd, or cleft in causeless rage;
While threats, while insults rend with sore dismay,
And hungry hounds stand gaping for their prey.
But cease my faltering tongue; ere these befall,
Oh Heaven, let Hanniel's blood bedew yon impious wall.
And will no happier hand direct the road,
And tell, where Quiet builds her sweet abode?
Where is the sage, on whose ^^gelic tongue
Bright wisdom dwelt, and soft persuasion hung?
Does no kind breast with patriot virtue glow,
And claim an interest in hi^ country's woe?
Here then* ye h^ir^ of Jacob's name, behold
A f^iend, whose bosom terror never controlled;
Whose voice, though envious thousands dare oppose,
Shall pour the balm, and heal his country's woes.
How long, brave heroes, shall your feet pursue
Such keen distress, as nations never knew?
How long your host the chains of slavery own,
And millions die, to swell the pride of one?
Against Heaven's decree let folly cease to rise,
And tempt no more the vengeance of the skies.
To other lords that firm decree ordains
The expected mountains, and the promised plains.
Our every path unnumber'd woes surround;
Our blood in streams bedews polluted ground;
No glad success arrays our steps in light,
And smiling Victory triumphs in our flight.
Search ancient years; through time's long course return,
When earth first wantoned in the beams of morn;
Success unchanged attends, when GOD approves,
And Peace propitious smooths the path he loves.
Base flight, and dire amaze, and creeping shame,
Man lost in guilt, and alien'd skies, proclaim.
If still your fettered minds, by folly swayed.
Doubts wavering toss, and leaden fears invade,
To yon bright dome your eyes convicted turn;
Say why forgets the guiding flame to burn?
Why round its point forgets the cloud to roll,
Sublime pavilion of the all-moving soul?
The dreaded truth must Hanniel singly own?
Fled is the smile of Heaven, the Guardian gone.
But Virtue asks, Why, led by GOD's command*
Roved this brave host through many a weary land?
Each hour, with pains replete, each field replies,
And with dread language, loud as clarions, cries,
In Egypt's realms, where every pleasure smiled,
And, far from famine, laborers lightly toiled,
Wanton with feasts, our thankless hearts repined,
And tainted prayers provoked the all-ruling Mind;
Tired by long scenes of woe, the ungrateful host,
Learned humbler thoughts, and prized the good they lost:
Reclaimed, each spotless mind adores his ways,
And every blessing wakes the voice of praise.
The end thus gained, his terrors lifted high
Bid his warned sons the unblessed purpose fly.
See, swiftly borne, the storm of vengeance rise!
Cloud after cloud invades the angry skies;
Even now over earth, fierce peals commencing roar,
And round the concave flames vindictive pour;
Hark, with what din the distant whirlwinds roll!
How the floods threaten from the thundering pole!
Rise, nimbly rise, burst every dead delay,
And fly, ere fury sweep our race away.
But where, oh where shall hapless Israel fly?
Where find a covert, when the ruin's nigh?
Will no kind land the wished recess disclose!
No friendly refuge soothe our long, long woes?
Yes, the fair, fruitful land, with rapture crowned,
Where once our sires a sweet retirement found,
That land, our refuge Heaven's high will ordains,
Pleased with our prayers, and piteous of our pains.
Hail favored realms, where no rude tempest blows!
Serene retreats, and shades of kind repose!
Ordained, the union'd bliss of life to prove,
The wreaths of glory, and the bowers of love!
There the great prince, with awful splendour crowned,
From foes shall guard us, and with peace surround,
In no rude combat ^ated to engage,
Nor fired by clarions to vindictive rage.
There cates divine shall yield the sweet repast,
Charm the palled eye, and lure the loathing taste;
With die refulgent crimson vestures glow,
And robes of kings succeed this garb of woe:
Our tribes, in spicy groves, at ease recline,
Press the swelled fig, and pluck the clustered vine;
Her floods of boundless wealth the river roll,
And spring, with autumn joined, beam temperate round the pole.
For these blessed joys, what mind, so left to shame,
Can grudge the tribute, regal glories claim?
Return, how due! Devoid of decent snow,
How soon would Power to trampled weakness grow?
How soon base minds the feeble judge deride,
And beggared rulers quake at wealthy pride?
Nor the just doom can Avarice' self deny,
Who share the blessing must the tax supply.
No danger now even timid minds can fear,
Lest stern Oppression lift her rod severe:
Unlike our fires, who raised impatient cries,
A fairer doom awaits us from the skies.
Taught by our hated flight, the nation knows
How, joined with ours, their vast dominion grows;
Disjoined, how swift the weakened tribes decay,
To foes a triumph, and to schisms a prey.
Even now with friendly joy their bosoms burn,
And with fond prescience hail our wished return;
Bid our own hands the grateful covenant frame,
Prepared to give, what avarice scarce can claim;
Our sons invite their boundless wealth to share,
Garlands of fame, and sweet repose of care.
Here, warriors, here the dreaded miseries flow,
Scenes of dire scorn, and seats of thickening woe.
For blessed as hope can paint, over all our toil
Let conquest flourish, and let glory smile;
Still in long train, what ceaseless ills await!
The waste of war, and frowns of adverse fate!
While sheathed in arms, the conquered realms we (...),
End of long pains, and patience' wished reward,
Those realms what culturing hand shall ^each to (...)
Or bid bright vesture purple over the loom*
Unfed, unclothed, our tribes shall (...)
Our lands grow wild, and every art (...)
Whose wisdom then shall equal lots divine,
And round each province lead the bordering line?
Will none, for fancied wrongs, the falchion draw,
His arm the umpire, and his will the law,
Over his friend's prize with rude irruption pour,
Burst nature's bonds, and bathe in kindred gore?
Whose chosen hand the scepter then shall sway?
What system'd rule the union'd tribes obey?
To my pained eyes what hideous prospects spread,
When impious Faction rears her snaky head!
Arrayed in savage pomp, Destruction reigns
Over flaming cities, and over crimson plains;
Friends, against friends, that knew but one fond heart,
Aim the dark knife, and lift the secret dart;
In brother's blood unseeling brothers wade,
And parent's bosoms sheath the filial blade.
Let Pity round the scene extend her veil,
And thrilling virtue shun the dreadful tale!
Or shall one arm the state forever sway?
And, sunk to stocks, our torpid race obey?
One voice, through ages, Jacob's pride control,
Ourselves the clay, and he the all-moving soul*
Perish the thought! to oppose a tyrant's reign,
One patriot life shall flow from every vein;
In Israel's cause shall burst this fearless voice,
And this bold arm avenge the freeborn choice.
Rise* warriors, rise! desert this dreary plain.
These fields of slaughter, and these haunts of pain!
To scenes of brighter name, to happier skies,
To other Edens lift your raptured eyes!
The world's fair Empress chides our dull delay,
Spreads her fond arms, and bids us haste away,
To bliss, to glory; seize the ^^spicious road,
And claim your interest in the blessed abode!
The hero spoke. As when, in distant skies*
Slow-roll'd, the darkening storm begins to rise,
Through the deep grove, and through the sounding vale,
Roar the long murmurs of the sweeping gale:
So round the throng a hoarse applause was heard,
And growing joy in every face appeared.
On a tall rock, whose top o'erlook'd the plain,
The Leader rose, and hushed the reverent train.
By Hanniel warmed, with airy visions fired,
He saw gay hope their glowing minds inspired,
In prospect bright, at hand fair Egypt lay,
Divine the pleasure, and secure the way;
With calm, frank aspect, that serenely smiled*
His port all-winning, and his accent mild;
Too wise, to thwar^ at once the general choice,
Or hope to sway alone by reason's voice,
He thus began. Ye heirs of Jacob's name,
Let Joshua's voice your generous candor claim.
In Israel's sacred cause my toils ye know,
My midnight watchings, and my morning woe.
Your long, lone path my wakeful eye surveyed,
Charmed the sad wild, and cheered the languid head;
Soothed drooping sickness, banished fear's alarms
And clasped the orphan with delighted arms:
Against fierce invasions raised a guardian shield,
The first to seek, the last to leave, the field;
For all your tribes a parent's fondness proved,
Fulfilled each wish, and even your wanderings loved.
In those sad scenes, when pity owns applause,
Not Hanniel's tears adorn a fairer cause.
For Israel's woe does Hanniel singly feel?
Are these eyes blind? or is this bosom steel?
When ceased these hands from toil? or what strange sun
Saw Joshua's feet the haunts of danger shun?
Your eyes have seen, these honest scars proclaim
How often this breast has poured the vital stream:
Still be it poured. A nation's cause to save,
Life's a poor price; the field an envied grave.
Whatever voice your welfare shall divine,
My heart shall welcome and my hand shall join:
But, calmly weighed, let Truth our counsels guide*
And Reason's choice the destined course decide.
So prone the mind in error's path to rove,
TO explore is wisdom, and it is bliss to prove.
Charmed, at first fight, when pleasures rise to view,
Each painted scene our ventrous thoughts pursue;
In airy visions far-seen Edens rise,
And isles of pleasure tempt enamored eyes.
On the calm tide, to aromatic gales,
Our fearless hands exalt impatient sails;
Through sapphire floods the bark foresees its way,
While wanton billows smoothly round it play,
Nor heeds the angry storm, that with dread power,
Climbs dark behind the hill, and hopes the avenging hour,
Warned by my voice, such hidden dangers fly,
And each gay prospect scan with searching eye.
In realms far distant spreads the expected shore,
Hills rise between, and boiling oceans roar:
Two tiresome ways invite our wearied bands,
Through trackless deserts, or through hostile lands.
Say, shall our steps again the waste pervade,
Dare the fierce heat, and tempt the impoison'd shade?
Consult yon chief; his voice again shall tell
Those dreary scenes, he painted now so well.
Or shall our feet, its dangers hid from view,
Through peopled realms, a nearer path pursue?
I blush, when falsehood leads the chosen tribes,
Where folly dictates, and where fear prescribes.
One foe to shun, shall fiercer foes be tried?
Death their delight, and war their earliest pride.
Lo the fierce wrath, at Taberah's plain that burned,
And Korah's host to instant corses turned,
Roused to more dreadful flames, our guilt to spy,
And see our feet to hated Egypt fly,
Shall wing Philistia's host to death and war,
And bid fierce Midian whirl the thundering car;
Full on her prey avenging Amalek fall,
And guilt and terror every heart appall;
Our wives, our sons, to savage wrath be given,
Feast famished wolves, and glut the hawks of heaven.
No fancied doom my b^ding words declare:
Truth, fixed as mountains, fills your startled ear.
To every beast the lamb presents a prey,
And coward bands invite the world to slay.
But will ye tremble for one shameful fall?
Shall one lost combat Abraham's race appall?
Is Aram dead! to rapid vengeance fly;
By me his orphan babes for vengeance cry;
Fired by his fate, your nerves let ardour string,
Exalt the standard, and to combat spring.
Even Zimri fears, by strong affections led,
While his fond bosom mourns his Aram dead.
Of all the sympathy, that woes impart
To the soft texture of the good man's heart,
Departed friendship claims the largest share,
And sorrow in excess is virtue there.
But timid passion! Grief, with startled eye
Spies fancied ills, and quakes, ere danger's nigh*
Yon chief demands, why fled the guardian fire?
What unknown folly bade the cloud retire?
That base distrust, which glorious fight delays,
That smooth, close fraud which tempts to dangerous ways,
These claim the scourge of Heaven: be these atoned,
Each fear shall vanish, and each hope be crowned.
While thus the Chief their bosoms warmed anew,
And every ear, and heart, to virtue drew;
Their kindling zeal impatient Hanniel eyed,
Shook for his cause, and frowned with startled pride:
When those, he cried, whose choice our warriors lost,
Of truth expatiate, and of wisdom boast,
With just disdain my rising spirits burn,
And my pained heart, at times, forgets to mourn*
To shame, to flight, does sacred Wisdom lead?
Does sacred Truth command our sons to bleed?
Rouse then to arms; lo Ai impatient stands,
And yields the doom, our eager wish demands;
In wisdom's cause with active zeal engage,
And fall, a splendid triumph to their rage.
Far happier lot, to meet the falchion's sway!
Than, one by one* thus lingering, waste away.
Far other end yon Chief ambitious eyes;
Concealed by virtue's mask the danger lies.
Unbrib'd, unawed, the honest task I claim,
To burst the veil, and ward the impending shame.
Long versed in wiles, the lust of power his guide,
He lulls our caution, and inflates our pride;
With sense, that darts through man a searching view,
With pride, that rest, or limits never knew,
To deep designs mistrustless hearts he draws,
With freedom soothes, and cheats with flattered laws;
A crown to seize, the patriot's fire can claim,
And mock with seeming zeal the fearful Name.
Full well he knows that, worn by slow delay,
Our generous tribes shall fall an easy prey;
That long-felt influence, great by habit grown,
Climbs to firm sway, and swells into a throne.
Be warned, be warned; the threatening evils fly,
And seek repose beneath a kinder sky.
Short is the toil, the well-known path secure,
The pleasure endless, and the triumph sure.
Rejoiced, each land will open the destined road,
And smiling guide us to the wished abode.
Freed from the fearful storm that round them spread,
Their hearts shall hail us, and their hands shall aid.
No giant chief in terror there shall rise;
No dreadful Jabin spring to seize the prize:
From Madon's hills, to fierce vindictive war
No frowning Jobab roll his iron car:
From death's alarms the potent king shall guard,
And bowers of transport yield the bright reward.
He spoke. Like Angels dressed in glory's prime,
With conscious worth, and dignity sublime,
While the still thousand^ gazed with glad surprise,
His great soul living in his piercing eyes,
The Chief returned. By wild ambition tossed,
To shame impervious, and to virtue lost,
Here bend thine eye, thy front unblushing rear;
Let frozen Conscience point no sting severe;
Then tell, if falsehood lends thee power to tell,
Thy mind believes one scene, thy lips reveal;
One black aspersion, formed to blot my name;
Or one vain prospect, raised for Israel's shame.
Disclose what dreaded toil this arm has fled;
On what dire plain this bosom failed to bleed:
Tell, if thou can, when, lured by interest's call,
One nerve, one wish forgot the bliss of all.
In virtue armed, while Conscience gayly smiles,
I mock thy fraud, and triumph over thy wiles:
Thy darts impoison'd peace and glory bring;
It Is guilt alone gives slander strength to sting.
Blush, Hanniel, blush; to yonder tent depart;
Let humbler wishes rule thy envious heart;
Calm the wide lust of power; contract thy pride;
Repent those black designs, thou can not hide;
Once more to Heaven thy long-lost prayers revive,
And know, the mind that counsels can forgive.
Can I, as GOD, unfailing bliss assure,
Foil with a wish, and peace at choice secure?
What nature can* this arm unbroke shall bear,
Whatever man dared, this breast unshaken dare
Canaan's host, those eyes with pain shall view
My falchion vanquish, and my feet pursue;
On Israel's faithful sons this hand bestow
The bl^ss of quiet, and the balm of woe.
Should then these thoughts* to base ambition grown,
With impious madness bui^d the envied throne,
To wing my doom let rapid lightnings ^ly,
And pampered hounds the peaceful grave deny.
Mine be the bliss, the bliss supreme to see
My long-lov'd nation blessed, and blessed by me:
Let others rule; compared with this pure joy,
A throne's a bubble, and the world a toy.
In reason's face let all thy wishes speed;
Let foes befriend thee, and let Heaven succeed:
Then count thy gains; the mighty prize survey;
And straws, and bubbles, shall those gains outweigh.
Wrought in gay looms, thy golden robes shall glare;
Rich banquets tempt, and luscious wines ensnare:
But to vile show shall Men their bliss confine?
Or sink to brutes, and only live to dine?
On these poor joys what dreadful ills attend?
Fears ever rising! miseries never to end!
Though whelmed in floods one impious tyrant lies,
In the throned son shall all the father rise;
The same black heart; the same beclouded mind;
To pity marbled, and to reason blind.
Search ancient times: the annal'd page (...) over;
With curious eye the sun's long course explore;
Scarce can each age a single king confes^,
Who knew to govern, or who wished to bless:
The rest, of earth the terror, or the scorn,
By knaves exalted, and by coward^ borne*
To lords like these shall Israel's millions bow?
Bend the false knee, and force the perjured vow?
A few short years, our wealth content to shore,
The rest their greedy hand to toil may (...):
But soon, fu^l soon, their ^nvious mi^d^ (...) know
Our growth their ruin, and our peace their woe.
Then all the plagues, from jealous power that spring*
And death, the tender mercy of a king,
Your breasts shall feel; and, racked with anguish, mourn
The day, when madness counseled to return.
Can I forget, how, from the dunghill raised,
Villains who bowed, and sycophants who praised,
Over Jacob's heirs were sceptered to preside,
Their tributes gather, and their labors guide?
From them, each cruel pang your heart shall rive,
That coward minds, or offic'd slaves, can give:
Their daring hands profane the spotless charms,
That yield soft transport to your melting arms;
Each generous thought the brandished scourge control
And Insult rend the agonizing soul.
Then too shall Egypt, fired with wrath, recall
The plagues they felt, their king's, their nation's fall;
Against your race, while Vengeance spreads the wing,
With fury arm them, and to torture spring;
Your sacred dome shall burn; your altars rend;
Your priests destroy; your hated worship end.
In that dread period, what auspicious shore
Shall banished Virtue's lifted wings explore?
In what new realm, when, crushed, her vo^aries fail,
Build the bright dome, and spread the hallowed veil;
Her priests inspire; her altars teach to rise,
And waft her morning incense to the skies?
Her final ^^ight your hearts in vain shall mourn;
In vain, with anguish, call her wished return;
In vain the hour extatic sigh to find,
And the sweet sabbath of a guiltless mind.
To Egypt's crimes our sons shall fall a prey,
And learn her manners, while they own her sway:
From many a bower obscene the poison gli^e,
Taint the young soul, and freeze the vital ^ide;
The sacred Law our rising hope forsake*
And lisp out curses, ere they kn^w to sp^^k:
Sad Conscience bow beneath an iron rod,
And torpid Reason own a reptile God.
Then, roused to wrath, shall Heaven refuse to hear;
Mock all your pangs, and hiss your bitter prayer:
In poisoned gales, its wasting curses rise;
The plague empurpled taint the sickly skies:
The fields all wither, famine rend the breast,
And babes, sad victims! yield the dire repast.
Then from Sabean climes, with hideous sound,
Swift cars shall roll, and savage war resound;
To blood, to vengeance, chiefs their hosts inspire,
Spread boundless death, and wrap the world in fire:
Our sons, blessed refuge of the waning year!
Charm of sad toil, and sweet repose of care!
Against their own hapless sires with foes combine,
And with new anguish point the dart divine.
Thus over our race shall matchless misery ro^l,
And death, and bondage blast the rising soul;
Till the last dregs of vengeance Heaven expend,
Blot out our race, and Israel's glory end;
In final darkness set our sun's pale beam,
And black oblivion shroud our hapless name.
For this dire end, were such bright scenes bestowed?
For this, the eternal covenant sealed by God?
For this did ocean's trembling waves divide,
And over pale Egypt roll their whelming tide?
For this, the seraph lead our sacred bands?
For this loud thunder speak the dread commands?
From the hard rock refreshing waters rise?
The food of angels shower from balmy skies?
The sun-bright waste its flaming heats allay,
And Jordan's parting billows yield our way?
But Hanniel cries, These wondr^^^ signs were given,
To scourge our guilt, and bend our hearts to Heaven.
Were this the end, fierce famine had annoyed;
The plague had wasted, or the sword destroyed.
To fairer bliss he led the chosen train
Through the dark wave, and over the howling plain,
Ordained, when yon proud towers in dust are hurled,
To found an empire, and to rule a world;
Over earth's far realms bid truth and virtue shine,
And spread to nature's bounds the Name divine.
What though a few base minds the course oppose,
Slaves of poor pride, and Israel's bitter foes;
For pomp, for banquets would their race destroy,
And smile, to sell a nation for a toy;
What though of lifeless mold, a feeble race
With souls of maids the shape of men disgrace;
Think life no life, unblessed with torpid ease,
Shrink from a shield, and shiver at a breeze:

And hissing Vengeance blast their impious name:

And to their kindr^^ nothing fleet away,
Far other mind our true-born race inspires;
Keen bravery prompts, and Abraham's virtue fires:
I see to combat ardent heroes rise;
I see bright glory flash from sparkling eyes;
Hark a glad cry! that every danger braves,
"Perish the day, ere Israel's sons be slaves;"
Swift pour new transports through my thrilling veins;
Heaven's voice in thunder calls to hostile plains:
Mark, mark the sound divine! cease every care;
Gird on your arms, and wake to manly war:
To bright possessions glory points the way,
And calls her sons, her heroes, to the prey.
By friendship's ties, religion's bands combined,
By birth united, and by interest joined,
In the same view our every wish conspires,
One spirit actuates, and one genius fires;
Plain, generous manners vigorous limbs confess,
And vigorous minds to freedom ardent press;
In danger's path our eyes serenely smile,
And well-strung sinews hail accustomed toil.
Against hosts like these what foe with hope can arm?
What numbers daunt them? or what fears alarm?
To reeds before them deadly spears shall turn,
Swords blunt their edge, and flames forget to burn;
To the s^ight mound descend the heaven-topp'd wall,
The floods grow dry, and hills and mountains fall.
Rise then to war; awake to bright alarms;
Hail the glad trump, and seize your eager arms!
Behold, my sons, behold with raptured eyes,
How slight the toil, how vast the glorious prize!
These golden robes the fate of Sihon tell,
How Midian yielded, and how Amalek fell;
How sunk proud Jericho's invaded wall,
And wide Canaan trembled at her fall;
How through each region rings the dreadful cry,
And their wild eye balls see destruction nigh.
That faith, that arm of steel, that dauntless soul
That bade over Bashan's walls destruction roll,
Over fields, over towers, shall Israel's standard bear,
Turn realms to flight, and wrest the prize of war;
Fill life with glory; Heaven's complacence gain,
And call fair Peace to cheer the crimson plain.
Then over wide lands, as blissful Eden bright,
Type of the skies, and seats of pure delight,
Our sons, with prosperous course, shall stretch their sway,
And claim an empire, spread from sea to sea:
In one great whole the harmonious tribes combine;
Trace Justice' path, and choose their chiefs divine;
On Freedom's base e^ect the heavenly plan;
Teach laws to reign, and save the rights of man.
Th^n (...) Art shall wrap the fields in bloom,
Fine (...), and guide the useful loom;
(...) towers in golden pomp arise;
Then (...) cities meet auspicious skies:
The soul on Wisdom's wing sublimely soar,
New virtues cherish, and new truths explore:
Through time's long tract our name celestial run,
Climb in the east, and circle with the sun;
And smiling Glory stretch triumphant wings
Over hosts of heroes, and over tribes of kings.
The Leader spoke; and deep in every breast
A thrilling joy his cheerful voice impressed.
Round the wide train, late drowned in sad dismay,
His eyes refulgent cast a living ray:
Soul caught from soul the quick, enlivening charm;
Each parent's visage bade his children arm;
In every heart the undaunted wish began;
Over the glad field a pleasing murmur ran;
On Ai's high walls they cast a longing eye,
Resolved to conquer, or prepared to die.
So, when the northeast pours a deepening storm,
Night shades the world, and clouds the heaven deform,
Loud on some ship descends the driving rain,
And winds imperious toss the surging main;
Dissolved in terror, sailors eye the wave,
Lift ardent prayers, and wait the gaping grave:
Is chance in beauty's bloom the morn arise,
Still the rough roar, and charm the troubled skies,
Serenely opening, far the billows over,
The blue-seen mountains, and the native shore;
Raptured the newborn day with shouts they hail,
And stretch their canvas to the joyous gale.
When sickening Hanniel saw their bosoms glow,
Their fierce eyes burn, and tears of transport stow,
The loved, the fond design, his changeful mind,
With secret pangs, to happier hours consigned.
High over the rest his shouts distinguished rose;
With well feigned smiles his artful visage glows,
And thus his voice -- When pierced with Israel's grief
I strove in vain to lend the wished relief,
Perhaps this heart, by nature prone to know
The good man's interest in his country's woe,
Of peace, and prosperous arms too soon despaired,
Unreal ills foresaw, and fancied dangers feared.
Yet still those views a kind indulgence claim,
Your fame their glory, and your bliss their aim.
Should this bold course be doomed to woe severe,
Pure is my warning voice, my conscience clear;
On destined fight should friendly Conquest smile,
With joy, my soul shall welcome every toil;
In Israel's cause, to scenes of danger driven,
To war is transport, and to die is heaven.
The hero ceased: a faint applause was heard,
And half-form'd smiles around the plain appeared,
With startling sound the trump's deep voice began;
To seize their arms the raptured thousands ran;
When Caleb, reverend chief, all white with age,
Serenely rose, and hushed the tumult's rage.
Deep thought fate musing on his furrowed face;
Calm wisdom round him cast an awful grace;
With smiles just Heaven surveyed his constant truth,
Innerv'd his limbs, and lengthened out his youth:
Even now his arm rejoiced the sword to wield,
To lead the contest, and to sweep the field.
Near the great Chief, in purple robes he stood;
Sense, from his tongue, and sweet persuasion flowed;
Round the wide plain attentive silence hung,
And thus sage counsels swayed the listening throng.
My voice impels to arms; but let the sky
Lead on our host, and bid the heathen s^y.
Were Israel spotless in the ETERNAL'S sight,
Ai had not boasted a victorious fight.
When Virtue dressed us in divine array,
Joy cheered each hour, and smoothed the rugged way:
To scenes of fame each warrior ardent ran,
And claimed the glories of the dreadful van.
But when black Vice our breasts with poison stained,
We shook for dangers timorous Fancy feigned;
Each shameful field beheld our squadrons sly,
And heroes a^m'd for bat^le but to die.
And ^ow some sin, some solly, not bemoaned,
Rebellion bold, or injury unaton'd,

Else round the heavenly dome the cloud had spread,
And sacr^d fires illum'd the rightly shade.
Let the whole race to GOD submissive bend;
Let ceaseless prayer to Mercy's throne ascend;
'Till the third morn, the pious fast endure;
Each d^^d be holy, and each bosom pure;
Then over our path wi^h joy shall HEAVEN preside,
Our guilt discover, and our counsels guide.
Then, nor 'till then, to war let trumpets call;

But should our course, this day, to fight be driven,
Should arms be brandished in the face of HEAVEN,
Look round your steps; survey the dreadful road;
Think if the sword and shield can war with GOD.
Thus spoke the sage. Blessed man! the Chief replied,
The war's first honor* and the council's pride!
Thine is the voice of GOD: the inspiring ray
Shines through thy breast, and gives the brightest day.
Two days shall combat cease. The camp around,
Let the sad fast in every ten^ be found:
Two days to HEAVEN be raised by pious fear
The grateful tribute of a humble prayer.
So shall we wipe away the crimson stain,
And Israel's glory gild the conquering plain.
He spoke. Each warrior with delight obeyed;
Each cheerful face the obsequious mind displayed.
The host dispersed; and prayers, and reverent sighs
Rose in soft incense to the approving skies.
THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK II.

ARGUMENT.
Morning. Gibeonites assemble for the worship of the Sun. Mina refuses to join
them in this worship; the king enquires the reason. She mentions her adoration
of the true GOD. The king being anxious to know more of the matter, after an
apology for speaking in such an assembly, she gives a general account of the
Deity, and his dispensations. As he is still further inquisitive, she gives him
a more minute account of the Divine works, in a history of the creation -- our
first parents -- the fall -- general succeeding apostasy -- deluge -- second
apostasy -- calling of Abraham -- Israelites journeying into Egypt --
oppression and plagues of Egypt -- Israelites' deliverance -- journey through
the wilderness -- promulgation of the divine law -- destruction of Sihon, and
Og -- last prophecy, death, and burial of Moses, and the commission of Joshua.
The Gibeonites being much afflicted at the prospect of their destruction, Mina
proposes an emb^ssy to Joshua, to solicit peace. The king approves the
proposal. Conclusion.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK II.
BEYOND those western hills, whose haughty brow,
To heaven exalted, scorned the world below,
A plain outspread, with growing verdure bright,
And stole, extensive, from the aching sight.
Here, in proud pomp, adorned with countless spires,
That mocked the glories of the solar fires,
Gibeon's imperial towers sublimely rose,
And spurned the terrors of surrounding foes.
Now over the hills red streams began to burn,
And bursting splendors ushered in the morn;
With living dies the flowers all-beauteous glowed;
Over the glad fields etherial odours flowed;
The forest echoed with a boundless song,
And rising breezes poured the strains along.
Adorned with green, before the palace lay
A spacious square, and smiled upon the day.
Here, ere the dawn the kin^^ing skies illum'd,
Or opening flowers the frag^ant gales perfumed,
Of every age, a v^st, assembled train
Poured from the lo^ty (...) and filled the plain.
High in the midst two sacred al^ars shone,
Adorned with honors to their God, the Sun.
This, decked with art, and (...) in royal pride,
With sable go^e the quivering victim died:
On that gay flowers in rich profusion lay,
And gales of Eden bore their sweets away.
Here, white with age, in snowy vesture dressed,
Aradon stood, their monarch, and their priest;
Red in his hand a torch refulgent shone,
And his fixed countenance watched the rising sun.
When first the flaming Orb, with glorious rays,
Rolled over the hills, and poured a boundless blaze;
Charmed at the sight, the monarch stretched his hand,
And touched the tributes with the sacred brand;
Through freshen'd air perfumes began to rise,
And curling volumes mounted to the skies.
Thrice to the earth the raptured s^ppliants bowed,
Then struck the lyre, and hymn'd the rising God.
O thou, whose bursting beams in glory rise,
And fa^l, and brighten, through unbounded skies!
The world's great Parent! heaven's exalted King?
Sole Source of good! and life's eternal Spring!
All hail, while clothed in beauty's endless ray,
Thy face unclouded gives the newborn day!
Above all scenes is placed thy heavenly throne;
Ere time began, thy spotless splendour shone:
Sublime from east to west thy chariot rolls,
Chears the wide earth, and warms the distant poles;
Commands the vegetable race to grow,
The fruit to redden, and the flower to blow.
This world was born to change: the hand of Time
Makes, and unmakes the scenes of every clime.
The insect millions scarce the mo^n survive;
One transient day the ^lowery nations live:
A few short years complete the h^man doom;
Then pale Death summons to the narrow tomb.

Las^'d by the flood, the hard rocks wear away;
Worn by the storm, the lessening hills decay;
Unchanged alone is thine exalted flame,
From endless years to endless years the same;
Thy splendors with immortal beauty shine,
Roll round the eternal heavens, and speak thy name divine*
When thy bright throne, beyond old ocean's bound,
Through nether skies pursues its destined round,
Lost in the ascending darkness, beauty fades;
Through the blank field, and through the woodland, spreads
A melancholy silence. Over the plain
Dread lions roam, and savage ^errors reign.
And when sad Autumn sees thy face retire,
And happier regions hail thy orient fire.
High in the storm imperious Winter flies,
And desolation saddens all the skies.
But when once more thy beam the north ascends,
Thy light invigorates, and thy warmth extends;
The fields rejoice, the groves with ^ransport ring,
And boundless nature hails the sky-born spring.
Nor even in winter's gloom, or night's sad reign,
Darts the warm influence of thy beams in vain.
Beyond the main some fairer region lies,
Some brighter isles beneath the southern skies,
Where crimson War never bade the clarion roar,
Nor sanguine billows died the vernal shore:
No thundering storm the day's bright face conceals,
No summer scorches, and no frost congeals;
No sickness wastes, no grief provokes the tear,
Nor tainted vapors blast the clement year.
Round the glad day star endless beauties burn*
And crowned with rainbows, opes the imperial morn;
A clear unbounded light the skies display,
And purple luster leads the changing day.
Over conscious shades, and bowers of soft repose.
Young breezes spring, and balmy fragrance blows;
The fields all wanton in serenest beams,
Wake fairer flowers, and roll diviner streams;
Through the long vales aerial music roves,
And nobler fruitage dies the bending groves.
Through spotless nations as the realm refined,
Thine influence there sublimes the immortal mind;
Its active pinions swift through nature roam,
Lose the low world, and claim a nobler home.
Their limbs, of endless life, with glory crowned,
New youth improves, and growing charms surround:
On the blessed shore thy splendors love to shine,
And raise thy sons each hou^, to raptures more divine.
Thus ceased the sound: the harp's melodious strain
Joined the glad hymn, and charmed the listening train;
A sparkling joy each (...) face displayed,
While light exp^nding lesse^'d every shade.
Fair as the lucid s^ar, that up the sky
Leads the gay morn, and bids the darkness fly,
Beside the king a lovely Virgin st^^d,
Nor joined the (...), no^ with the assembly bowed.
A sweet disple^sure tinged her melting eye.
And her sad bosom heaved the o^pressive sigh.
Her soft dist^^ss the watchfu^ king su^^ey'd,
And th^s, with friendly smile, addressed the maid.
Say, loveliest fair one, whence the meaning gloom,
That damps our joys, and clouds thy rosy bloom!
Why does thy soul the reverence due deny
To yon bright orb, that gilds the orient sky?
Far other God, replied the fair, demands
My vocal transpo^ts, and my suppliant hands;
A GOD, whose power raised (...) yon azure round,
Formed the wide ea^^h, and fixed the ocean's bound;
Who more the (...) transcends, ^han his gay gla^e
The transient glimmerings of some half-seen star.
Strange seenes, the monarch crie^, the voice declares*
And breathes sweet music through our raptured ears.
But can thou, unconvinc'd, yon orb behold,
Over earth, over heaven, in endless triumph rolled?
What boundless joy his gladsome course attends!
What glory brightens! and what good descends!
Round the blue void his beams unchanging shine,
And speak his nature, and his name, divine.
Yet still my curious thoughts the tale demand,
And ask improvement at thy lovely hand.
Say then, O fair, what all-exalted Power
Thy wishes reverence, and thy hands adore.
With down-cast eye, and cheek of crimson bright
That sweetly mingled with the spotless white,
Replied the virtuous maid. To bolder tongues
Of man's bold sex, the arduous task belongs.
But thy fond cares, that saved my life, demand
Toils far superior from my grateful hand.
Thy bliss, thy endless bliss, my voice shall bribe
To pass the bounds, the maiden's laws prescribe.
Far, very far beyond this lower sky,
Beyond the sun, beyond the flames on high,
Dwells in pure light, in heaven's serene abode,
The Source of life, the Spring of endless good;
All scenes, all heights above, sublimely reigns;
All worlds created, and all worlds sustains.
Yon orb, whose brightness wakes thy raptured praise,
Is but a beam of his unbounded blaze.
His breath illum'd, his hand exalted high,
And rolled him flaming through the expanded sky.
His bounteous influence, through all nature driven*
Warms the wide earth, and cheers the wider heaven.
All scenes, all beings his pure fight surveys,
Where morn begins, and where pale eve decays;
Where hell's dark shores the glooms of night display;
Or heaven's broad palace glows in lasting day;
Through worlds of endless youth, where angels shine,
And unknown nations rove in light divine.
He moves, informs, directs, and rules the whole;
Their cause, their end, their guardian, and their soul.
He wakes the beauties of the vernal morn;
He bids the flames of sultry summer burn;
He showers the autumnal wealth; and his dread power
Sounds in the wintry storm, and bids the wild waves roar.
In these vast regions countless beings move,
Live in his smiles, and wanton in his love:
In all, his power, and boundless wisdom, shine,
The works, the glories of a hand divine.
Throned in high heaven, in starry mansions reign,
Of purest intellect, the angelic train
All sense, all soul, all love, eternal power
Their thoughts contemplate, and their songs adore.
Through earth's wide realms unnumber'd tribes we find,
Of different ranks, for different ends designed.
On every leaf the insect millions swarm,
Hum round the flower, or in the sunbeam warm;
The birds, on painted pinions, gayly fly
Through the wide regions of the sapphire sky;
Beasts climb the cliff, or walk the savage wood;
And fishes sport around the foamy flood.
These, with the reptile race, to time a prey,
Of dust were fashioned, and to dust decay.
To man of nobler rank, two parts were given,
This formed of earth, and that inspired by heaven.
Such as the texture, such the allotted doom;
His body moulders in the narrow tomb:
But the winged soul, when earth in dust is hurled,
Shall spring, immortal, from the sinking world;
Ordained, if crimes its earthly course distain,
To bathe in fire, and waste with endless pain;
If cleans'd from guilt, with active joy to rise
To the pure transports of angelic skies;
But man, unmindful of his nobler birth,
In vain seek^ pleasure from surrounding earth.
Far different, far, the scenes by Heaven designed
To fill the wishes of the active mind.
This bounded point is but our being's morn;
To endless life the etherial Soul was born.
Upward with nimble flight her thoughts should soar,
And, winged by virtue, brighter worlds explore*
Earth's grovelling joys disdain with conscious pride,
Like angels fashioned, and to heaven allied.
For this fair train our nature to prepare,
And the pure fragrance of immortal air,
To raise the downward heart from earthly toys,
And mould our wishes to sublimer joys,
Through earth's wide realms, afflictions first began,
The noblest blessings HEAVEN bestows on man.
Toil, disappointment, hunger, thirst, and pain,
A long, long, dismal, melancholy train,
Cleanse the dim eye, dissolve the powerful lust,
And loose the chains, that bind our hearts to dust.
From sorrow's fire, like silver well refined,
Freed from vile earth, shall rise the undrossy mind,
Each hour, with beams of clearer beauty shine,
And ceaseless claim an image more divine.
At length, when sickness brings the expected doom,
Its powers shall rise triumphant over the tomb,
Forward to nobler scenes with rapture spring,
And hail the message of the undreaded king;
While life's long stream its farthest shore shall lave,
And seek the bosom of the eternal wave.
Then shall we see diviner winds arise,
The main grow calm, and smiles invest the skies:
Then shall our happy hands exalt the sail,
Launch on the deep, and call the etherial gale;
With joy, our spirits leave the fading shore,
And hear the less^ning storms at distance roar.
Inwrapp'd in beams of uncreated light,
All heaven, disclosed, shall burst upon the sight;
Streams of immortal bliss in vision roll,
And hosts of angels hail the kindred soul.
With rosy smiles, thus spoke the lovely maid,
While over the plain a boundless silence spread.
Like the tuned lyre, the music of her tongue
Poured soft persuasion on the truths she sung:
Pleased, her sweet grace, and sparkling eye, they view,
And the frank mein, that Falsehood never knew.
To all, Aradon bent a yielding ear;
For Heaven inspired his honest heart to hear.
Mid savage realms, fair Gibeon's sons inclined
To manners gentler, worship more refined:
Each social art adorned the generous door;
The stranger welcomed, and relieved the poor;
And hence they lived. From nature's bounteous Lord,
Even virtue's semblance finds a sure reward.
A calm delight exulting in his eyes,
With gentlest voice, the monarch thus replies.
O brightest of thy sex, an angel's tongue
Alone can boast the sweetness of thy song.
Led by thy voice, my raptured mind would know
The mighty Power, from whom all blessings flow;
Would learn what holy seers his will explain,
What prayers delight him, and what offerings gain;
Safe in his smiles, beyond the grave respire;
Ex^lt over death, and flee from endless fire;
To whose immortal regions speed my flight,
And prove some humble seat, amid the sons of light.
But say, O fair, when formed the Power divine
The lamps that round yon sky forever shine?
Know thou the day when earth's wide realms were made,
The hills exalted, and the (...) spread?
Whose hand thine infant mind to reason wrought,
In virtue nursed thee, and in wisdom taught?
Though age my trembling brow has whitened over,
Strange unknown scenes thy curious thoughts explore.
Returned the lovely maid, Thy glad request
Wakes my fond hope, and warms my grateful breast --
Know, mighty prince, when Elam's deathful spear
Pierced the fell foe, and loosed my soul from fear,
From Israel's camp, through unknown paths, I strayed,
My lone steps wandering round the woodland shade.
'Twas there, the sacred truths the prophet sung,
And thus sweet music tuned his heavenly tongue.
From realms divine high-rais'd beyond all height,
The almighty Parent cast his piercing sight;
With boundless view, he saw the etherial vast
A clouded gloom, an undelightsome waste:
Around the extended wild, no sun's broad ray
Marked the clear splendour of immortal day;
No varying moon, ordained at eve to rise,
Led the full pomp of constellated skies;
No day in circling beauty learned to roll;
No fair spring smiled, nor frost congealed the pole;
Substantial darkness space unmeasured filled,
And nature's realms lay desolate and wild.
He spoke: at once, over earth's far distant bounds
The heavens wide-arching stretched their sapphire rounds
With hoary cli^^s the far-seen hills ascend;
Down sink the vales, and wide the plains extend;
Headlong from steep to steep the billows roar,
Fill the broad main, and toss against the shore.
He spoke: and beauty through all nature flowed;
With springing verdu^e earth's wide regions glowed;
Forth rush the slowery tribes, and trees on high
Shroud their tall summits in the ambient sky.
He spoke; the heavens with sudden glory shone;
In godlike pomp burst forth the golden sun;
Far through immensity his kindling ray
Shot life and joy, and poured the newborn day;
With milder luster rose the charms of even,
The moon's broad beam, and all the pride of heaven.
He spoke; and fishes filled the watery rounds,
Swarmed in the streams, and swam the Ocean's bounds*
The green sea sparkled with unnumber'd dies,
And varying beauty waved upon the skies;
Whales through the foaming billows proudly r^de,
And unknown monsters gambol'd over the flood.
From the deep wave, adorned with nobler grace,
In countless millions sprang the feathered race;
Through the far clouds the eagle cleft his way,
And soared and wantoned in the flames of day;
Full on the morn the peacock opened his beams,
And swans majesti^ rowed the expanded streams.
He spoke; and, wondering, from disparted plains
In throngs unnumber'd rose the bestial trains:
Their snowy robes the harmless flocks revealed;
Gay steeds exulting pranc'd the vernal field;
The lion glar'd, and mid the gazing throng
Shook his rough main, and grimly stalked along.
The wide earth finished, from his western throne,
In splendid beauty looked the gladsome sun;
Calm were the skies, the fields with luster crowned,
And nature's incense filled the etherial round.
Enshrined in sacred light, the Maker stood,
Complacent smiled, and owned the work was good.
Then from his hand in silent glory came
A nobler form, and Man his destined name;
Erect, and tall, in solemn pomp he stood,
And living virtue in his visage glowed.
Then too a fairer being showed her charms;
Young Beauty wantoned in her snowy arms;
The heavens around her bade their graces fly,
And Love sat blooming in her gentle eye.
O pair divine! superior to your kind;
To virtue fashioned, and for bliss designed!
He, born to rule, with calm, uplifted brow,
Looked down majestic on the world below;
To heaven, his mansion, turned his thought sublime;
Or roved far onward through the scenes of time;
Over nature's kingdom cast a searching eye,
And dared to trace the secrets of the sky;
On fancy's pinions scanned the bright abode,
And claimed his friend, an Angel, or a GOD*
Her he indu'd with nature more refined,
A lovelier image, and a softer mind.
To her he gave to kindle sweet desire,
To rouse great thoughts, and fan the heroic fire:
At pity's gentle call to bend his ear;
To prompt for woe the unaffected tear;
In scenes refined his softening soul improve,
And tune his wishes with the hand of love.
To her he gave with sweetness to obey,
Inspire the friend, and charm the lord away;
Each bleeding grief with balmy hand to heal,
And learn his rending sinews not to feel;
Each joy to improve, the pious with to raise,
And add new raptures to his languid praise.
To this loved pair a blessed retreat was given,
A seat for angels, and a humbler heaven;
Fair Eden named: in swift succession, there
Glad scenes of rapture led the vernal year;
Round the green garden living beauty played;
In gay profusion earth her treasures spread;
The air breathed fragrance; streams harmonious rung,
And love, and transport, tuned the aerial song.
With tranquil beams the seventh bright morn appeared
And thus, from firey clouds, a voice was heard.
This day, O Man, to sacred transports rise,
And pass the hours in converse with the skies:
To prayer, to praise, be all thy wishes given:
Soar from the world, and here begin thy heaven!
So shall thy sons pursue the virtuous road,
And, each returning sabbath, wake to GOD.
The sovereign voice the reverent pair obeyed;
A solemn beauty earth and heaven arrayed:
With joy the pinioned tribes, in every grove,
Hymn'd the blessed influence of immortal love:
Man joined the concert, and his raptured lays
Charmed the gay fields when angels ceased to praise.
Mid Eden's groves the tree of glory stood,
That taught the unaltered bounds of ill, and good:
Its fruit, all beauteous to the ravished eye,
Denied to man, and sacred to the sky:
Denied alone; a boundless store was given,
Food for bright angels, transcript fair of heaven.
And thus the law -- If vain desire to taste
Prompt thee, rebellious, to the dire repast;
Hear, hear, O man! on that tremendous day,
Thy life, thy bliss, thy virtue, pass away;
No more the heir of endless joys refined,
But guilty, wretched, to the dust consigned:
Toil here thy lot, thine end the dreary tomb,
And hopeless anguish thine eternal doom.
The sovereign voice the pair obsequious heard,
The injunction reverenced, and the danger feared:
'Till urged by impious lust, by hell ensnared,
They plucked the fruit; the guilt, and sentence shared,
For one poor banquet* one unreal joy,
Rebelled, and yielded bliss without alloy;
To how^ing deserts were from angels driven,
And lost the sweet society of heaven.
Then ills on ills unnumber'd rose forlorn;
No more the ^^ient beamed the angelic morn;
Fragrance (...) Beauty closed their blissful reign
Nor Sprin^ p^^ennial danced along the plain.
Cold Nig^^ her fearful clouds around them spread,
And gave new terrors to the howling shade.
Lost in the bosom of the ascending storm,
The sun's faint beam in winter ceased to warm;
Over plains, and hills, the chilling frost congealed;
The snow tempestuous sadden'd all the field;
On the wide wave the headlong whirlwind poured,
And all the thunders of the ocean roared.
Where late gay bloomed the harvest's waving pride,
And purpled fruits the bending branches died,
Impervious thorns, and clinging brambles spread,
And unblessed famine gloom'd the autumnal shade:
For blood, the raging wolf began to arm;
Fierce, hungry tigers rung the dread alarm;
The lion's sovereign voice, with thrilling sound,
Cleared the wide grove, and shook the hills around.
The sacred stamp the mind forever lost,
The skies' perfection and the angel's boast:
Else had our life rolled on, from sorrow clear,
A semblance bright of heaven's eternal year.
Now stained with guilt, the soul to hatred turned;
With pride was lifted, and with envy burned.
Fierce bickerings rose; with conquest noise was crowned,
And Reason's still, small voice in curses drowned:
In vain sweet Friendship charmed the stubborn ear;
She sung, and wondering found no heart to hear.
By hands, not wisdom, next the cause was tried,
And blows obtained what argument denied.
Revenge soon taught to point the murdering knife,
And secret ambush hedged the hated life.
The villain's gloomy path black night concealed,
And virtuous blood bedewed the lonely field.
Then rousing banners War with transport raised;
Forth flashed the steel; the far-seen signal blazed:
Over the scared hills the warning clarion rang,
And swift to combat startled nations sprang;
In floods of streaming gore the fields were drowned,
And slaughtered thousand^ heaped the embattled ground.
The regal dome, the tarret's golden gleam
Graced the sad triumphs of the imperious flame;
The Monarch spoke; and over the circling throng
Bright smiles broke forth, and pleased applauses rung;
A beauteous semblance of the fields around,
Starr'd with young flowers, and with gay verdure crown'^
Where airy songs, soft proof of raptured love,
Waved on the gale, and echoed through the grove;
While the clear sun, rejoicing still to rise*
In pomp rolled round immeasurable skies.
From wall to wall insulting engines frowned,
And all the pride of art fell crumbling to the ground.
To earth's wide realms, from scenes above the sky,
The Almighty Ruler turned his searching eye:
Deep sunk in boundless guilt the regions lay,
And vice exulting claimed a single sway.
Her countless millions, lured by Pleasure's charms,
Bask'd in her smiles, and sported in ^er arms;
The song the feast, inspired the jocund hours,
And Lewdness wantoned in luxurious bowers.
In vain from door to door the beggar strayed;
His portion hunger, and the frost his bed:
In vain sad Sickness raised her feeble cry;
No friendly hand appeared, nor melting eye:
Virtue, fair pilgrim, cast a wishful view,
And spread her wings, and sighed a last adieu.
He saw, while terror veiled his awful face,
And bade fierce ruin wrap the guilty race,
Borne by the vengeance of his lifted arm,
Far rolled the black immensity of storm;
From east to west were poured the glooms on high,
And cloudy curtains hung the unmeasured sky.
Shook by the voice that rends the immortal plain,
In one broad deluge sunk the etherial main*
Huge floods, imprisoned in the vaulted ground,
With wild commotion burst the crumbling bound;
Over earth's broad climes the surging billows driven
Climbed the tall mountains, and invaded heaven:
The pride of man, the pomp-embosom'd tower,
Towns wrapped in gold* and realms of mighty power,
All plunged at once beneath the unfathomed wave,
And nature perished in the boundless grave.
From realms* where su^s with milder glory shine,
His voice awaked the western wind divine.
At once the balmy wind obedient blew,
And springing beauty clothed the world anew;
In rosy youth her climes emergent smiled,
And flowery visitants rejoiced the wild.
How, doomed to pass beyond the liquid grave,
The ark's rich treasure triumphed over the wave;
How the blessed favorite, rising from the main,
Ruled orient lands, and peopled earth again,
Thou know. The wondrous tale, through every clime,
Tradition wafts along the stream of time.
With circling splendour, and etherial die,
The covenant bow spread sudden round the sky,
From those gay heavens, that arched with pomp divine,
Fair over the angelic world forever shine,
To earth removed, and fixed by GOD'S decree,
An endless barrier against the ambitious sea.
Safe in the sacred sign, ungrateful man
New s^enes of guilt with eager zeal began.
Again black Vice over nature stretched her sway,
And magic Pleasure charmed the foot astray.
No sacred anthems climbed the bright abode;
Nor Reason blushed to hail a golden god:
With rage, and conflict, earth was covered over;
Towns sunk in flames, and fields were drenched in gore.
With impious jests they mocked a future doom;
Sung over the shroud, and danced into the tomb.
From land to land the clouds of death unfurled,
And one wide lethargy benumbed the oblivious world.
Then too, proud Ashur, queen of realms, began
To forge her chains, and bind inglorious man.
Hence, tyrants sprang, and dared with impious claim,
Demand the honors of the sacred Name;
Hence stern Oppression raised his iron rod,
Hence crimson Slaughter wrapped the world in blood:
Through every clime the night of slavery spread,
And Heaven repenting grieved that man was made.
From this black mass, this min^led host of foes,
One sainted friend the Almighty Ruler chose;
For him, blessed champion of his yielding cause!
He changed the stable course of nature's laws;
(An hundred summers saw the circling morn,
Ere his first hope, the promised heir was born)
To him, to his he gave Canaan's shore,
'Till the bright evening gild the west no more.
To Idol guilt the world beside was given,
Their name, their memory blotted out of heaven.
When the dire famine over all nations spread,
His hand the favorite race to Egypt led.
As some fair tree, where fruitful streams are rolled,
Lifts spiry shoots, and bids its leaves unfold;
Over the green bank ambitious branches rise,
Enjoy the winds, and gain upon the skies;
While opening flowers around it gayly spring,
And birds with transport clap the painted wing*
So each fond sun, and each successive shade
Beheld with smiles the infant nation spread;
From field to field the rising boughs expand,
Share the glad smiles of heaven, and fill the jealous land.
Their sudden growth the envious tyrant viewed,
And impious hands in infant gore imbrued,
With bold oppres^ion bathed the streaming eye,
Racked the sad soul, and roused the suppliant cry.
Their bleeding wrongs the omniscient Mind surveyed,
And bade fierce Vengeance bare her flaming blade.
No (...) the limpid wave serenely flowed;
But through sad shores the river rolled in blood;
Unnumber'd reptiles climbed the stately dome,
Croak'd over the feast, and crawled the pillar'd room;
Insects in countless millions earth overspread;
The sickening murrain gloom'd the pastur'd shade;
From darkened skies the storm's red b^lts were hurled,
And hail, and lightening swept the wasted world;
Like cloudy curtains, locusts h^^g the day;
Pale death, and famine marked their baleful way:
Three days blank midnight wrapped the realm in gloom*
And all her firstborn sunk in one broad tomb.
Then, high in air his lucid banner spread,
To the bright sign collected Israel fled,
With transport traced the finger of the sky,
Winged their glad path, and hailed redemption nigh.
In vain its countless ills the waste disclosed;
In vain the sea their sacred path opposed;
Back rolled the instinctive main; and round their side
In crystal splendour stood the conscious tide.
In the bright front, a cloud his dark abode,
Throned on the rushing winds, an angel rode,
The spreading volumes marked their path by day,
And guiding flames illum'd their nightly way.
Behind, the tyrant, urged by Heaven's decree,
Drove his pale host, and trembled through the sea.
On the tall shore sublime the Prophet stood,
And stretched his hand above the eager flood;
Wide-circling all, far closed the billow'd womb,
And Egypt's glories found a watery tomb.
Through spacious climes of fierce and scorching day,
The cloud expanded led their lonely way,
'Till, white with cliffs, and crowned with many a shade,
In cloudy pride famed Sinai reared its head.
On this lone mount, the all-discerning Mind
To teach his name, to unfold his law, designed;
On earth to witness truth and power divine,
And bid over Jacob's sons his splendors shine:
Beneath its haughty brow the thousands lay,
And hoped the wonders of the expected day.
Fair rose the dawn: from heaven's sublime abode,
The almighty Power in boundless glory rode;
Long dusky folds a cloud around him spread,
His throne surrounding with impervious shade.
Its flame-bright s^irts with light excessive shone,
A noontide morn, that dimmed the rising sun.
Forth from its womb unusual lightnings fly,
And thunders, hurled on thunders, rock the sky:
To Sinai's top the wondrous scene descends;
Down plunge his cliffs; his tottering summit rends;
Over all the mountain burn devouring fires,
Wreathed in dread smoke, and crowned with lofty spires.
Loud as hoarse whirlwinds earth and heaven deform,
Loud as the thousand thunders of the storm.
With clear, dread voice, in pomp tremendous, roll
The trump's long-sounding terrors through the pole.
The Seer majestic climbs the towering height,
And, bosomed deep in glory, leaves the fight.
There, while the world was hushed in silent awe,
The Sovereign Mind disclosed the eternal Law;
And thus the dread commands. O Israel, know,
I am the LORD, who snatched thy sons from woe,
From Egypt's bondage traced thy various ways;
Nor shall base Idols share my sacred praise.
Let no vain words my fearful Name profane;
Nor toil, nor sports my holy sabbaths stain.
Thy parent's voice with reverent mind obey:
Thy hand from dire revenge, and murder stay:
Let not a thought thy neighbor's couch ascend;
And not a wish to others wealth extend:
Let truth thy converse, truth thy oaths confine:
And every passion to thy lot resign.
Unnumber'd statutes then his voice ordained,
The poor protected, and the rich restrained;
And taught, what manners prosperous rule assure,
Their foes to vanquish, and their peace secure.
Then through long, weary climes their course was turned,
Still moved the cloud, and still the glory burned.
With ceaseless care he filled their hearts with good;
The skies dissolving shower'd immortal food:
With wondering joy they saw the streamy rain
Pour from the rock, and spread along the plain;
And clouds of quails, from every region driven,
Blackened the fields, and filled the bounds of heaven.
