'Twas then, near Edom's realms the thousands lay,
And her proud prince denied the expected way.
Whatever their state, whatever their GOD concerned,
From their great Seer my curious parent learned;
Charmed with the scene, he left his native soil,
Shared all their wants, and bartered ease for toil.
Through long, lone paths we bent our circling course,
Untir'd by winter's rage, or summer's force;
Bright angels led the van; and round the road
Dread scenes of terror marked the present GOD.
Even now I see fierce Sihon's hostile train,
Sheathed in dire arms, and frowning over the plain.
In childhood then, around my sire I clung,
Danced in his arms, and in his bosom hung*
With nimble steps the sacred warriors sped,
Blew the shrill trump, and filled the field with dead.
Like drifts of rushing dust, that sweep the skies,
On fear's light pinions swift the remnant flies;
From town to town we wing our rapid way,
And the wide region sinks an easy prey.
Then giant Og his heroes drove to arms,
Whirled his proud car, and thundered hoarse alarms:
In distant fields I saw the storm ascend,
Its shades all darken, and its clouds extend;
Down the grim hills I heard the volumes roll,
And bursting terrors rend the s^uddering pole.
As snows, slight fabric* in warm s^ns decay,
The impious squadrons sudden melt away.
Now over the Seer had six-scor^ summers run,
And (...) locks around his temple^ (...)*
When sounds melodious, opening from the sky,
To the sad train declared his end was nigh.
His mind inspired with more than mortal sight,
Saw future scenes and ages rush to light;
And thus his voice. On Israel's chosen train,
Like vernal showers let endless blessings rain:
Each rising age, ascend thy glory higher,
With time roll on, and with the skies expire!
But oh, my sons, this voice attentive hear;
Let these last strains command the listening ear!
To unborn years I stretch my raptured eyes;
I see the promised seed in glory rise!
The etherial star triumphant mounts on high,
And fairer beams adorn the unmeasured sky:
All heaven impatient waits the sacred morn;
Jesus descends; the filial GOD is born:
Hosts of bright angels round the favorite shine,
And earth is ravished with their hymns divine.
It Is he, whose offering guilt shall wash away,
And raise Mankind to climes of ceaseless day;
The bliss of truth, and virtue, shall inspire,
And warm the bosom with seraphic fire.
Haste, haste, ye days of heaven! with rapid wing,
To this sad world the hope of nations bring!
Descend, O Prince of peac^! thy love bestow;
Cleanse the dark soul from seeds of endless woe;
With all earth's myriads Jacob's sons unite,
And bid immortal glory spring to light.
No more the gentile realms in dust shall mourn;
Nor evening altars to the in^ernals burn;
But waked, revived, by thy celestial name,
One cloud of incense, one unbounded flame,
To heaven ascend: the sun shall brighter rise,
And peace, and light, and glory gild the skies.
Thus the great Seer; and warmed with he^venly grace,
Besought all blessings for hi^ dar^ing race;
Then up famed Pisgah's side sere^^ly drew,
Where all Canaan met his rapturous view;
Thence his glad soul explored her native day,
And left, for bliss, the tenemental clay:
His soul, scarce lower than the angels made,
With glory mitred, and with truth arrayed.
As the bold eagle, borne from humble vales,
Lifts his strong wings, and up the expansion sails;
Over groves, over hills, over mountains, wins his way,
And climbs exulting in the noontide ray;
Now far beneath him sees each birdling fly;
Now clouds light-flo^ting skim the lower sky;
In prospect wide, with piercing ken, descries
Far, lessening towns, and spacious empires rise;
Here rivers wind, the lakes their borders spread;
And there the blue-seen ocean smooths his bed;
In pride sublime, he holds his upward way,
And bas^s, and triumphs, in the flame of day.
So, borne with angel-flight, his mighty mind,
Ascending, left the common wing behind;
Pull on the sun's great Source superior drew,
'Till truth's wide regions stretched in glorious view;
There fair Creation spread her boundless plan;
There opened, mysterious, all the world of man:
With every splendour bright Redemption shone;
And, one immense of beauty, God the Son.
Still up the heavens he winged his solar flight,
And soared, and mingled with unborrow'd light.
Far in a wild vale's solitary gloom,
Jehovah formed his favourite's lonely tomb;
For life distinguished, there his limbs refine,
'Till morn's last beams in purple glory shine;
Then, robbed in beauty, shall the Prophet rise,
And sail, the peer of angels, through the skies.
But, ere his spirit sought celestial day,
To Joshua's hand he gave the destined sway,
A Chief divine! with every virtue crowned,
In combat glorious, and in peace renowned,
To him the Almighty voice -- Thy chosen hand
Shall guide my sons, and rule the promised land.
That land, where peace, and every pleasure reigns,
Over hea^en-topp'd hills, and fair, extended plains;
Where countless nations build the lofty dome,
Nurse purpling vines, and teach the vales to bloom;
That land is thine. Wherever thy foot shall tread,
From the parched climes where Midian's thousands spread,
To realms, where Hazor, armed with potent sway,
Bids kingdoms bow, and conquered chiefs obey:
Or where Euphrates winds his gentle flight;
Or the broad ocean rolls in evening light;
All, all is thine. Who dare thy course withstand,
Shall feel the fury of the Eternal hand.
Lost in black crimes the torpid nations lie,
And claim fierce vengeance from an injured sky.
Rise, rise to arms! over Jordan's yielding flood
My guardian hand shall point the destined road.
Thus spoke the fair: and while the etherial strain
Breathed a soft music over the wondering train,
With anxious look the impatient monarch cried --
O best of maids, thy sex's noblest pride!
Far round the neighboring realms by fame is rung
The wondrous race, thy lovely voice has sung.
Often have I heard, how, armed with dreadful rod,
Before his votaries marched their mighty GOD:
How kings in vain their rapid course opposed,
Their hosts all vanquished, and their empire closed.
But still, misled by Rumour's dubious tongue,
In sad suspense my mind all-anxious hung.
Now with clear truth the scenes tremendous shine;
Of force convinced, I own the Power divine.
And must our race with one wide doom expire?
These turrets sink? these walls be wrapped in fire?
Must yon bright maid, whose so^t and lovely smile
Could murderers cha^m, and wolves of rage beguile;
These beauteous infants, scarce to reason born,
Sweeter than flowers perfume the vernal morn,
To war's unpitying fury yield their breath,
And helpless close their little eyes in death?
O thou great GOD, whose sway over heaven presides,
Whose searching eye the world's vast empire guides:
Stay, stay thine band; this guilty nation spare;
Let these sweet babes thy boundless pity share!
Unformed our infant prayer -- but cries sincere
And honest hear^s will find a bounteous ear.
He spoke; around, the melting voice of woe
Breathed (...) complaints, and tears began to flow;
When thus the Prince again -- O loveliest maid!
Where, where shall Gibeon find the needed aid?
Can no kind hand the friendly refuge give?
No pitying savior bid my children live?
Say, loveliest fair, can thou no succor lend?
Our teacher thou -- be thou our guardian friend.
Perchance thy bounteous Ruler, formed to bless,
Over suppliant realms may lift the branch of peace.
The maid returned -- perhaps a virgin's mind,
Though wisdom fail, the wished retreat may find.
To Israel's camp two trusty heroes send;
Let me, restored* their peaceful steps attend.
The maid, thou see by blessed adoption shares
Their mighty Leader's fond, parental cares.
Pleased with the offering, Joshua's hand may give
The palm of peace, and bid thy nation live.
Charmed with the thought, joy sparkling in his eyes,
With voice exulting, strait the king replies.
O fair divine! thy mind, with wisdom bright.
Even age out^soars* and climbs an angel's flight.
Let peace thy life surround. The task be mine
Soon to prepare, and end the blessed design.
Thy lovely voice must find a generous ear;
So sweet a strain even oaks would bow to near.
THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN: BOOK III.

ARGUMENT.
Characters of Hezron, Irad, and Selima. Morning. Irad and Selima walk out on
the plain, northward of the camp, and hold a conversation on the justice of the
War. As they are returning to the camp, they overhear two Israelites conversing
on a design of returning into Egypt. Irad communicates the discovery to Joshua.
The alarm is given, and an army percieved, coming from Ai to attack the camp.
Joshua goes out to the place of rendezvous, marshals a body of troops, and
sends them, under the command of Zimri, to meet the army of Ai. In the mean
time the camp is in a general uproar, and a large body of the Israelites
assembled, westward of the camp, for the purpose of returning into Egypt* After
the confusion is in a degree allayed, Tadmor harangues the insurgents, with a
list of grievances, and stimulates them to perseverance. Caleb who, with
Hezron, had been sent by Joshua, upon Irad's information, to watch the motions
of the camp, replies to him. Ardan answers him, with impudence, and Hezron him,
with severity. Insurgents march. As they are quitting the plain, Joshua comes
out, with a body of troops (...) attack them. The chieftains set their forces
in array. Joshua orders them to disperse. Ardan affronts him, and is killed.
The insurgents disperse, and the chiefs return to the camp. Irad goes out to
view the battle. Armies engage with violence, and equal success; until the
chiefs of Ai, influenced by superstitious fears, excited by the appearance of a
thunder storm, order a retreat. Zimri also retires. Scene of the beauties of an
evening after the storm concludes the book.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK III.
OF Judah's thousands Hezron held the sway;
And love, and reverence, bade them all obey.
The chief, of simple manners, knew no art;
Truth was his language; honesty his heart:
To bless mankind his life's unvaried end;
His guest the stranger, and the poor his friend.
So fair his strong, and stubborn virtue shone,
Heaven crowned his wishes with a lovely son.
To mould young Irad was his darling care;
To form for peace, to animate for war;
His limbs to innerve; his vices to control,
And lead to wisdom's fount his thirsty soul.
In earliest years, the favorite Youth began
To show those charms, which rarely grace the man.
To rashness brave, his bosom burned for fame;
Yet knew a milder, and a nobler flame:
Love's gentle fire his passions could control,
And pure Religion warmed his manly soul.
Not that, which broods upon the surly brow.
Or walks on frozen joints, demure, and slow;
At truth, and virtue, points the fatal wound,
Swells on the tongue, and vanishes in sound:
But that, whose influence fires the angelic band;
Smooths the rough bosom; op^s the narrow hand;
Serenely brightens in the cheerful face;
Casts round each act unutterable grace;
With rising morning, bows the secret knee,
And wafts, great God! the humble soul to thee.
His raptured father wished no second son;
But found both parents' charms combined in one:
His own strong sense, and daring thought, refined
By the soft graces of a mother's mind.
His lively duty cheered the waning year;
With hand all gentle wiped the aged tear;
Explored each wish, prevented each request,
And thought it heaven to make a parent blessed.
Nature's politeness, unaffected ease,
Moved in his limbs, and framed his soul to please;
To worth complacent gave the just reward,
And notic'd humble life, with kind regard.
Nature can form the soul, or rough, or fine;
But all her clouded beauties faintly shine:
Religion bids a new creation rise,
Fragrant as spring, and fair as spangled skies.
Thus, on the canvas, West, with raptured view,
See^ newborn worlds his magic hand pursue;
The impassioned forms dissolve in soft desire,
Or glow, and tremble, with seraphic fire;
They breathe, they speak, they move, the field around*
And the ea^ listens for the expected sound.
But these must fade: while Virtue's strokes shall live,
Transcend earth's sky-built tomb, and with the heaven^ revive.
Beyond his peers, by nature, Irad shone;
By virtue, ripened to the duteous son;
By virtue, aimed at life's sublimest end,
Rose to the saint, and softened to the friend:
Pleased his fond nation saw his glories rise,
And a new Joshua charmed their raptured eyes.
The virgins viewed, how could they not approve?
Esteem's the silent harbinger of love.
The kind e^e, glistening with a frequent tear,
The conscious blush, that saw discovery near,
T^' unbidden sigh, that swelled the beating breast,
And the fixed gaze, that scarce could be repressed,
The soft emotions to his eye revealed,
And new, strange tremors through his bosom thrilled.
But far over all Selima's charms prevailed,
When his pleased heart her piercing eyes assailed.
His youngest birth, blessed Caleb owned the fair,
His life's chief solace, and his favorite care.
Not nature's hand her beauty could improve;
Her voice was melody; her mind was love;
Her stature tall; her air intrancing ease;
Her skin the lilly, opening to the breeze;
Her cheek was health's inimitable die,
And the bright soul sat sparkling in her eye.
No vile cosmetic stained her lovely face;
No affectation murdered real grace:
Her robes all neatness, told the world how fine,
How pure, the angelic habitant within.
Sweetness etherial majesty controlled,
And formed an Irad of a softer mould.
Such was her soul, as when, of darkness born,
Over young creation rose beginning morn,
Fair, in her front, a blushing Virtue stood,
Just sprung to life, and e^'d the forming God;
From grace to grace with glowing wisdom grew.
And smiled, and triumphed, in the rapturous view.
Now twice nine years had over the fair-one rolled,
Illum'd her eyes, and bade her charms unfold;
When her quick fancy, self-inspir'd to rove,
Attun'd her feelings to romantic love.
Often on the youth she fixed a secret gaze,
And often, with transport, listened to his praise.
The charms of (...), the beauty of desert,
Stole soft, and silent, through her yielding heart*
Esteem, which hermits scarce could disapprove,
Bloomed in his smiles, and opened into love.
Nor shone her glances on his breast in vain:
The gaze, that gave, returned the pleasing pain.
Judgment, in both, the spotless flame improved;
They liked from fancy, but from reason loved.
Often would each sire his tender wish declare,
To see one band unite the lovely pair
Often sighed the youth to unfold his anxious mind;
But still a modest fear his lips confined:
In pleased attention on her charms he hung,
And half-heard wishes trembled over his tongue.
At length, kind Heaven, propitious to the pair,
Led his fond steps, where love had led the fair.
In a lone walk, far-distant on the plain,
Surpised, his tongue unbidden told his pain.
The beauteous maid, of frank and gentle mind,
Smiled in his hopes, and blessed with love refined,
In truth's mild beam the spotless union grew,
And gave such joy, as youthful angels knew.
Now waked the dim-seen dawn. Over hills afar
Rose in gay triumph morn's refulgent star;
Up the gay skies fore-running beauty spread;
The gray mist sailed along the mountain's head;
In clouds the embosom'd lark her matin sings,
And from his couch impatient Irad springs,
To morn's unnumber'd sweets invites the fair,
G^y prospects, magic songs, and fragrant air.
Rapt with the charms, which nature gives to view
The great, the high, the beauteous, and the new,
To her s^ft power the^ (...) the yielding mind,
Warmed (...) as they gazed refined;
In flowery tribes, where (...) splendors play;
When (...) prospect holds the lingering da^;
When brightened Evening spread^ (...) train,
And (...) young Hes^e^ to his (...);
In cloudy wilds, where gloomy thunder lies,
The pale moon mourns, and mountains prop the skies.
Over northern plains serene the lovers stray,
And various converse charms their easy way --
How sweet, O fair -- the Youth with rapture cries --
Earth's beauteous scenes, and wonders of the skies!
The folding clouds! the gates of morn unbarr'd!
The dewy plains, with slowery gems instarr'd!
The cliff-topp'd mountain! the deep-waving grove?
The air all odor! and the world all love!
Thrice fair are nature's works -- the maid replied,
And her face bloomed in beauty's living pride --
When round her fields my thoughts untroubled roll,
An easy joy steals softly on my soul:
Fired as I gaze, my breast with rapture warms,
Her glories ravish, and her music charms.
But oh the fate of Ai's unhappy field,
That every joy, and every hope, dispelled!
Fled are the charms, that nature once attired
And lost the sweets, that either once inspired.
As now through well-known paths, retired I stray,
And seek accustomed beauties round my way;
At every turn, the seeming trump alarms,
Pale corses rise, and groans, and clashing arms;
From my pained bosom heaves the unbidden sigh;
The still tear trembles in my laboring eye;
Lo^^, but to grief, my feet bewildered love,
And my heart deadens to the self, and love.
O fatal hapless combat! cause unjust!
That blends the noblest heroes with the dust;
From sad Canaan's sons their wealth demands,
The docks they tended, and their cultured lands;
Bids over their peaceful domes destruction flame,
And blots with deep dishonor Israel's name.
The Prince rejoined, By all-creating Heaven,
To Abraham's sons th^se fruitful fields were given.
Whatever he made, the Maker claims his own;
Gives, and resumes, advised and ruled by none.
By him bestowed, a righteous sword demands
These flocks, these cities, and these promised lands,
Yet not 'till crimes, beyond long-suffering great,
Had filled their cup, and fixed their changeless state,
Would Heaven permit our race its gift to claim,
Or seal the glory of the almighty Name.
In vain mild Mercy hoped their hearts to gain,
And Patience looked for Penitence, in vain.
As rolling streams one course eternal keep,
All rush impetuous down the guilty steep.
The maid returned, The nations' foul disgrace,
Stained with black guilt, I grant Canaan's race.
But not alike are all from virtue driven;
Some, more than others, claim the sword of Heaven:
Yet undistinguished falls the general doom,
The best, the worst, we destine to the tomb.
Where Hazor's hundred towers majestic rise,
Frown over her plains, and dare avenging skies;
In all that elegance of artless charms,
Which prompts mild love, and rival hate alarms;
In that sweet union of serene desires,
Which blows with fragrant breath unmingled fires;
Young, beauteous fair-ones, through her regions known,
Out^ie the maid, thou love to call thy own.
To these bright virgins chosen ^rads bow;
Less wise, less virtuous, and less fair than thou;
But crowned with many a grace; of thoughts resin'd,
Of pleasing person, and of dauntless mind,
Shall this blessed train, so young, so fair, so brave,
Fall, with black wretches, in a ^irey grave?
Or round wild regions must they hapless roam,
Exiled from joy, and forced from cheerful home?
To hunger, thirst, and sorrow, sink and pray,
And breathe, with lingering death, their lives away*
Should thou, when war to Salem drives her course,
Seize the keen steel, and join the conquering force,
W^ile thy bold breast with glory's warmth beats high,
And wreaths well-twin'd approach thy ravished eye,
To some love hamlet loos^ly wandering come,
Where simple swains had built their peaceful home,
Where care in silence smoothly passed away,
And homebred happiness deceived the day;
Should there sweet, helpless children meet thy view,
Fair as young rosebuds look through early dew,
With infant wonder, on thine armor gaze.
And point, with artless hands, the steely blaze:
Say could thy heart one angry purpose know,
Or doom such cherubs to a single woe?
Charmed by soft smiles, I see thy heart retire,
And mild compassion breath^ a gentler fire;
Thy love parental over them kindly yearn,
Prompt pleasing hope, and all their wishes learn;
Thy bounteous hand each needed bliss bestow,
And in the angel lose the intended foe.
Yet should dread war over these fair regions fly,
Unnumber'd virgins, bright as those must die;
To flames unnumber'd babes resign their breath,
And ere life blossoms, meet untimely death.
To thee* O prince! without a blush, I own
Such woes tremendous freeze my heart to stone.
^re Irad's arm such precious lives destroy,
Let me, far guiltier, cease from every joy;
Quick to the dreary grave my form descend,
Our love all vanish, and our union end.
The Prince rep^ied, Blessed gentleness of mind!
The grace, the glory of a ^eart refined!
When newborn, helples^ beings meet our eyes,
In noble minds, such thoughts resistless rise:
Even brutes, when young, our tender wishes try,
And love forbids the infant whelp to die
Yet often this kindest impulse of the soul
Bids wild desire in murmuring tumults roll,
And blames the Power, whose love alone, to earth.
And all earth's drear and dark events, gave birth.
In thy pure bosom, angels must approve.
For sad Canaan's youth, this generous love.
But once as fair, as young, as soft as they,
As white with innocence, with smiles as gay,
Were those black throngs, whose crimes as mountains rise*
And wipe out pity from the all bounteous skies.
As eggs innoxious, often in meadows strewn,
Break into asps, and pour the viper's brood;
Nursed in rank soils, to strength the reptiles grow,
Resound the hiss, the sting of vengeance throw,
Uprear the crest, inroll the snaky spire,
Light the keen eye-ball with terrific fire;
From fields, and forests, death, and poison gain,
And scatter wide destruction round the plain:
So, harmless once, by vile affections lured,
In guilt, and years, those babes alike matur'd;
A thirst for sin, all patterns left behind,
The form all putrid, poisoned all the mind,
To every crime, to every madness, driven,
Cursed the sad world, and hissed the name of Heaven.
There the sot reels, the murderer prowls for blood;
There the starved orphan sues in vain for food;
For man man burns, with Sodom's tainted flame,
And the world sickens with incestuous shame.
Even nature's ties their bosoms bind no more,
Wives wade in nuptial, fires in filial gore;
To howling Molock blooming babes expire,
And mothers round them dance, and light the funeral fire,
Should then those infants to dread manhood rise*
What unheard crimes would smoke through earth and ^kies!
What hosts of demons sin's dark realm would gain!
How hell gape hideous round Cana^n's plain!
This sea of guilt unmeasured to prevent,
Our chosen race eternal Justice sent,
At once the bright possession to reclaim,
And against its victims point the vengeful flame*
Thus crimes their d^e and dire reward shall know;
Thus God be witnessed sin's unchanging foe;
From land to land Jehovah's glory shine,
And fear, and homage, wait the Name divine.
But, O unrivaled maid! the kindest doom
These babes may destine to an early tomb.
To manhood risen their guilt, beyond control,
Would blot their names from life's c^lestial roll.
Now, in fair climes, their souls, forever blessed,
May bloom in youth, and share immortal rest;
And hail the boundless grace, that snatched its foes
From sins unnumber'd, and from lasting woes.
And, O bright maid! whatever high Heaven designed
Is just, is glorious to the omniscient Mind.
When Heaven commands, the virtuous ask no more:
His will i^ justice, as his arm is power:
Led by his voice, our cause divine we know;
We tempt no evil, and we fear no foe.
All gentle Youth! Selima soft replied --
How well thy words from falsehood truth divide!
Wi^h what sweet tenderness, thy voice displays
The truth, the luster, of the Eternal ways.
But say, blessed Prince! will Heaven our race succeed?
Shall we victorious gain the darling meed?
So often our h^st rebellion blackens over,
I fear, lest triumph crown our arms no more.
When will the friendly cloud again return?
When over yon dome the nightly glory burn?
Rejoined the smiling Prince; too anxious maid,
Let faithless terror never thy ^eart invade,
To Abraham s^al'd the sacred covenant stands --
Thy countles^ sons shall rule Canaan's lands. --
Guilt's impious train these tumults shall destroy;
Too vile, too base, to share the promised joy.
And he, whose soul, a plant for earth too fair,
Has grown, and ripened for a kinder air.
Full soon may feel the hand of blasting time,
By Heaven transplanted to a nobler clime,
Pass the cold winter of the frozen tomb,
And rise, and flourish in eternal bloom.
But to glad fields, beyond those hills that lie,
And drink mild influence from the western sky,
The rest triumphant soon shall wing their way,
Seize their vast towns, and reign from sea to sea.
Then joined in love, in hands connubial joined,
Each passion calmed, and every taste refined.
Our fears shall end, unclouded hope begin,
Peace' gentle morning over Canaan shine*
In soft beatitude the seasons roll,
And growing union mix the kindred soul.
The maid returned -- O day supremely fair!
Not blooming Eden owned a happier pair.
But, Youth beloved! my bosom, racked with pain,
Tells me, sad tale! the darling wish is vain.
Tells me that chosen morn will never come,
Nor bli^s be finished, but beyond the tomb.
For earth too bright were these love-lighted fires!
Too blessed the indulgence of such pure desires!
He^e unallay'd, no lot, no joy appears;
Grief poisons hope, and pleasures mix with tears.
Ah fairest, wisest, loveliest of thy kind!
Of ^orm all finish'd' and of matchless mind!
Sweet smiling visitant from yonder s^y!
Too bright to live, and O too dear to die!
Why, hapless Mina! why from friends, and home,
Did thou, unguided, in the wi^d wood roam?
Perhaps the hungry wolf around thy way
Lurked with grim rage, and seized his helpless pr^y.
Perhaps, O lot of anguish! brutal men
Thy path unguarded, with fell eyes, have seen.
Or do thou pale, unseen, unburied, lie.
Sad sorrow's victim, in the inclement sky?
How soon is thy fair course of glory run!
Thy hopes all ended! all thy duties done!
Sleep, lovely maid! in hollowed silence rest,
Let fragrant gales thy form with leaves invest;
There with new sweets, the lovely wild-rose bloom,
And pitying strangers raise thy verdant tomb.
Ah hapless maid! the tender prince rejoined --
How thy rich graces charmed each generous mind!
Even Joshua's love how nobly did thou claim,
Thy wishes virtue, and thy actions fame!
When his toils rose, when dangers dire oppressed,
And Israel's griefs hung heavy on his breast,
Thy gentle mind, a soul-supporting stay,
Seren'd those toils, and charmed those griefs away;
A calm retreat from fear, and doubt, and strife,
And all the hidden pangs of sceptered life.
Rest in mild slumbers, lovely maiden! rest;
Thy life be copied, and thy memory blessed!
Each soft-eyed virgin bid thy fame revive,
Attune her lyre, and in her actions live;
So, joined with thee, in beauty's distant clime,
Her praise shall triumph over the death of time.
As thus the converse passed, with many a tear,
To the still camp approached the sadden'd pair.
In the utmost skirt, a tent at distance stood;
Whence mingling voices, scarce-distinguish'd, flowed.
Heard'st thou -- a warrior low his zeal expressed --
When generous Hanniel Jacob's sons addressed?
How on his words the thousands listening hung!
How sweet persuasion charmed us from his tongue!
From pride, from pomp, from love of titles free,
He loves the poor; he feels for thee and me.
Oh, could our tribes by sad experience learn
Wha^ children tell, and what the blind discern,
Him for their leader would they raptured claim,
And fly from endless toil, and endless shame.
From hideous war my wearied soul recoils;
I ask no treasures raised from battle's spoils.
To painful arms let sons of slaughter run;
By them be glory's painted bubble won:
To peace, of aims far different, would I fly,
In peace inglorious live, inglorious die:
While peace, while plenty, much-loved Egypt knows,
^ears no shrill trump, and dreads no banded foes,
These boasted flocks, and towns, and promised fields,
To them my first, last with delighted yields.
With earnest voice, his fellow pleased replies --
Since toil and pain have taught thee to be wise,
Know, my brave friend, a secret, faithful band
Soon point their course to Egypt's darling land.
When first to combat Joshua bends his way.
To guard the camp these bold associates stay;
With one firm heart, our path we then begin,
And noble Hanniel leads the blessed design.
But hushed in silence must these counsels rest,
Scarce even to tried, and faithful friends confessed;
Lest the dread Chief's all-watchful, piercing eye,
With sun-like ken, the hated plot descry.
Thou know what ills a plot disclosed attend;
Our names must perish, and our lives must end.
His friend returned -- The loved, the bold design
My glad soul welcomes, and my hand shall join.
Hail happy tidings! hail auspicious fields!
Where genial nature every pleasure yields --
Too blessed, to that sweet native land I fly,
That cot, that heri^age, that friendly sky --
Dear scenes of yout^* where peace and pleasure mild,
With cheerful health, and ceaseless plenty smiled --
Might these, O envied lot! again be given,
'Twe^e bliss too great: I claim no higher heaven*
This heard, Selima to her tent withdrew;
While strait to Joshua ardent Irad flew,
To him, apart, the dangerous plot disclosed,
And what the tribe, and where the tent, exposed.
As some fond parent eyes his darling child,
Pleased, the great Hero on the favorite smiled,
His zeal, his prudence praised, and on his head
Besought the Heavens their choicest bliss to shed.
Mean time from distant guards a cry ascends,
And round the camp the dinning voice extends;
The alarming trump resounds; the martial train
Pour from the tents, and crowd the accustomed plain,
In mazy wanderings, thickening, darkening, roll,
Fill all the field, and shade the boundless pole.
As where proud Erie winds her narrowing shores,
And over huge hills a boiling ocean pours,
The long white-sheeted foam, with fury hurled,
Down the cliffs thundering, shakes the stable world,
In solemn grandeur clouds of mist arise,
Top the tall pines, and heavy, seek the skies:
So spread the volumes of the dust afar;
So roar the clamors of commencing war.
Anxious, and active, there the Leader strode.
Nerv'd every heart, and steeled for death and blood;
From rank to rank, he hushed the tumult's sound,
And spread deep silence over the attentive ground:
Then while the chiefs combined the dread array,
Towered a high rock he bent his rapid way;
From the tall height, to Ai he cast his eyes,
And saw, in southern fields, her squadrons rise;
A cloud, far-spreading, over the plain impelled,
Rolled up the expanse, and wrapped the gloomy field:
Approaching, widening, slow the darkness came,
Emblaz'd with gleams of intermitted flame.
So, long and black, like skirts of rising even,
Thick clouds, now gathering, filled the northern heaven;
Borne on slow winds, that ceaseless changed its form,
Over the dark mountains sailed the expanding storm;
In rising grandeur far-off thunders roll,
Dim lightnings flash, and gild the clouded pole;
More wide, more vast, the solemn gloom ascends,
And frowning, deepening, round the horizon bends.
At once the Hero gave the loud command;
In awful silence moved the chosen band;
Compact, to Ai they cours'd their dreadful way,
And generous Zimri ruled the long array,
Mean time new scenes around the camp began,
The tribes all motion, man conf^s'd with man;
From tent to tent swift-hastening feet appeared:
Low-murmuring voices, mingling sounds were heard;
Loud, and more loud, the earnest clamors grow,
Hum through the tents, and all the camp overflow.
To Egypt's realms -- resounds the general cry --
From these sad scenes, with prosperous feet, we fly,
These hosts of foes, these fields of ceaseless fight,
This sway of bondage, and this war of flight.
Haste, freedom's sons, and seize her happy shores,
For all her peace, and wealth, and joy, are yours.
Thus round the host the mingled clamor slew,
And loud, and fierce, debates tumultuous grew;
They urged, persuaded, threatened, flattered, cried,
With love conjured, with stubborn breast denied;
Friends left their friends, with answering look severe,
Sighed sad departure, dropped the expressive tear;
From parents children headlong burst away,
While groans recalled them from the dire affray;
To brothers brothers gave the parting ^and,
And Virtue eyed, with tears, the swerving band.
All dressed in arms, and clothed in rich array,
Forth from the camp the warriors bent their way:
Their hands their gold, and favorite treasures bore,
And each fond bosom hailed the Egyptian shore.
Over the broad circuit of the western plain,
From all sides gathering, moved the numerous train,
This way, and that, in thousand paths impelled,
Immingling, rushing, darkening, hid the field,
To one great central phalanx swiftly driven,
Gloom'd the sad ground, and cast a shade on heaven,
Frowning, and fierce, expanded over the plain,
And, proud of numbers, deemed resistance vain.
Of name obscure, before the increasing throng
Two haughty chieftains proudly stalked along;
Felt all the joys, which little minds o'errun,
From sway first tried, and influence scarce begun;
Looked wise, inportant hurried over the field;
Commanded, questioned, with loud threats compelled;
Spoke with stern voice; advising, wavering stood,
And scarce the ground was printed, where they trod.
Far round the plain the mingled tumult ran,
Chief answered chief, and man rehearsed to man.
Through each small circle loud the murmur spread,
Of spoils ungiven, virtues unrepaid,
Woes unextinguish'd, labors never to end,
The starving household, and the naked friend --
Where now is the heart, that blessed the prophet's sway,
That soothed the tribes, and bade the soul obey,
Swept Bashan's fields, o'erthrew proud Sihon's throne,
And to poor warriors left the spoils they won?
But now new chiefs, in wiles and learning trained,
Wield a dread scepter, with an iron hand;
All, all but Hanniel; Hanniel singly glows
With Israel's good, and weeps for Israel's woes.
Hail then, oh hail the blessed, auspicious day,
That opes to brighter realms our happy way!
The chief^, we chose, the glorious path shall guide*
Un^ur^'d with learning, and unstained with pride.
Thus round the plain the tumult shrill resounds;
Of different note, immix unnumber'd sounds;
High tossed in either helms confusedly fly,
And clashing shields to clashing shields reply:
Loud, hoarse, and rough, wide jars discordant noise,
And raging passions swell the clamorous voice.
So, where on ocean's brim the long beach winds*
Breaks his proud waves, and all his fury binds,
Unnumber'd fowls, of various wing, arise,
And toss in wild gyrations to the skies;
From each harsh throat hard strains of discord roar,
Break with dire din, and grate along the shore;
Loud, and more loud, the nations heaven deform,
Or gloom the strand, and croak the coming storm.
As round the plain the mingled tumult ran,
Tadmor, the elder chieftain, thus began --
Hail, sons of freedom! Jacob's fairest boast!
Heirs of the sky, and virtue's genuine host!
Well did brave Hanniel teach, in words divine*
How fast our tribes, with toils, and griefs, decline;
Full well he marked what deep designs are laid
By chiefs, of man, nor truth, nor Heaven, afraid;
That, swelled with pride, and trained in artful lore,
O'erleap all right, and crush the hapless poor.
To us no leader tells the deep design,
What hosts oppose us, and what lands combine*
What towns are next besieged; what dangers tried;
What spoils are won, and who those spoils divide.
In Egypt's realm the long-wish'd rule to gain,
They found each art, and each bold effort, vain:
Thence through the waste they urged our fatal way,
And hoped, in this dire land, untroubled sway;
Yet there the poor a lot far happier found,
With fasts unburden'd, and with rites unbound:
Our tributes paid, at plenteous feasts we sat,
Stretched in soft ease, and every dainty ate.
Oh, why from those fair regions did we come?
Why, blind and headlong, leave our darling home?
Here our own leaders Egypt's kings outdo*
And change of lords is all the good we know.
Haste then, from these dread fields of misery fly;
With chiefs you chose again to Egypt hie;
Where ease, and wines, and feasts, and soft delight,
Earth ever fruitful, skies forever bright,
Awake sweet pleasure, raptured love revive,
And teach poor mortals what it is to live:
Now seize the hour, by Joshua's folly given,
Or opened for Israel by a pitying Heaven.
Ai's gallant sons will sweep his host away,
Worn by long labors, and to fasts a prey;
Or, escaped the field, their weary feet must fail;
Or, joined in fight, our arms will soon prevail;
This day beyond pursuit our course removes,
And leaves the tyrant to the slaves he loves.
He spoke; at once, from all the impatient train,
A bust of triumph shook the sounding plain;
Thence rose the shout; as often the heavens replied;
And, borne through fields, and woods, the far-off murmur died.
Thus, when the vernal storm forbears to rave,
And the wild river swells his torrent wave,
Huge isles of ice, along the clifted shore.
Float slow, and cumbrous; solemn thunders roar,

And now, when Tadmor ceased* the shouts decayed,
With sweet, mild accent, thus grave Caleb said --
How slight the toil, mistaking chief, to prove
It Is wisdom's voice directs the path, we love!
Though thorns, though serpents hedge the fatal way,
The fond heart bids, and answering feet obey.
Each truth, each argument, thy voice runs over,

The waste's dire ills thy plaintive words resound,
Yet through that waste the darling realm is found;
Again those countless woes our race must try;
Again with toil, and thirst, and famine* die.
Or shall we free, by Hazor's bands compelled,

Will hosts that tremble, where Ai^s sons appear,

But to what end* against unnumber'd ^oes,

Shame, vice, idolatry, and bondage, joined,

If war is destined Israel's fearful doom,
With war, let freedom, wealth, and glory come:
Let peace, let realms, let empire crown the toil;
The world applaud us, and the Eternal smile
In this fair land, shall each poor warrior reign
Lord of himself, and monarch of the plain.
His house, his herd, his harvest all his own,
And changeless law transmit them to his son.
But Egypt's wealth her king alone commands,
Her sons, her gold, her products, and her lands.
For him our hands, in slavish woe, must toil,
And pamper splendour on the beggar's spoil,
Poor beyond thought, suspended on a breath,
Our life a sufferance, and a nod our death.
But Israel's chiefs are trained in dangerous lore,
And hence regardless of the humble poor.
Say, Tadmor, say, the wiles of art to shun,
To Egypt's realms impatient do thou run?
To courts, to lords, with smooth deceit o'erhung,
Where art first budded, and where learning sprung?
Truth, conscience, Heaven, thine idle dreams deny;
Repent, return; nor, snared by treasons, die.
The hero spoke. From all the angry train
A rising murmur waved along the plain:
As 'twixt tall hills, where rushing torrents roll,
A slow, and lingering groan ascends the pole;
Through gloomy caverns hums the solemn sound,

Imperious prince, I know thy heart of s^eel

The hero spoke. A rising hiss began,

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK IV.

ARGUMENT.
Morning. Tribes assemble. Story of Achan. Embassy from Gibeon. Story of Mina,
Joshua gives her to Elam, prince of Gibeon, in marriage, and makes (...) with
the Gibeonites. Feast. Joshua's prayer. Cloud descends on the tabernacle. Elam

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK IV.
NOW the third sun illum'd the azure main,
And Israel anxious gathered on the plain.
In every face suspense and grief appeared,
Each son was doubted, and each parent feared.
Brothers on brothers cast a side-long eye,
And trembling fair-ones pressed the rising sigh.
Mid the wide concourse great Eleazar shone,
The sacred minister of Heaven's high throne.
White were his aged lo^ks, and round his face
Calm contemplation cast a solemn grace;
Over his pure vesture shining unguent spread,
And breathed the fragrance of the Arabian shade:
Full on his breast the star-bright Urim glowed,
And over his brow beamed HOLINESS TO GOD.
The sacred rites performed, he bent his way
To the bright (...) that mocked the rising day.
The train with reverence bowed. Around his head
Red spires of lambent flame serenely played;
On the clear splendors gazed the crowd around,
And deep attention hush^d the shady ground.
Now in the sacred place the Priest adored,
And thus his voice Jehovah's smiles implored.
O thou, whose wisdom built the bright abodes,
Great KING of KINGS, and sovereign GOD of GODS,
Almighty Father hear! Let grace divine
Shower on our host, and cleanse from every sin!
Thou see, Omniscient Mind! what guilt unknown
Pollutes our race, and dares insult thy throne:
Thou see; and oh may thy all-gracious voice
That guilt declare, and bid thy sons rejoice!
He spoke. A rushing sound of winds began,
Sung in the vail, and through the temple ran;
A sapphire flame, unutterably bright,
Shot from the gloom, and wrapped the walls in light;
The dome all trembled; earth beneath it shook;
And over the ark a voice in thunder spoke --
To Israel's thousands, from the Eternal throne,
This mighty mandate by thy voice be known.
Of Judah's race, a wretch, by madness driven,
With impious hand, has dared the wrath of Heaven:
S^ones shall his house destroy, and flames devour;
I AM commands; let all my sons adore. --
Nor more; an awful darkness round him spread,
Still as the gloomy mansions of the dead.
All sad, all slow, returned the mournful priest,
And strong impatience every eye expressed --
What the decree of Heaven! the Leader cried --
With solemn voice, the sacred Seer replied --
Of Jud^h's race, a wretch, by madness driven.
With impious hand, has dared the wrath of Heaven:
Stones shall his house destroy, and f^ames devour;
I AM commands; let all his sons adore.
He spoke; and ^^rrow gloom'd the plain: in ^aste,
So Joshua's voice decreed, the lots were cast;
The wretch, so long concealed, arose to view,
And Achan's name to fear^^l vengeance drew.
Forth from the crowd, with languid steps, and ^low,
The victim s^rode, and looked un^tter'd woe*
His useless (...) hung feebly by his s^de;
His tottering knees their w^^ted and denied;
His front was clouded with a wild dismay;
For hastening ruin darkened over his way.
And thus the youth forlorn -- My hated name
Sinks in the misery of undying shame,
Passed is the day of grace; my dimmed light
Fades in the skirt of everlasting night.
From the rich spoils my ^and a store conveyed,
Helped by the night, and safe in covert laid.
Beneath my tent the mischi^f may be found,
Where spreads the flooring over the secret ground.
Why did my heart resist that lovely fair,
Who sweetly warned me of the tempting snare?
Hear, all ye warriors! fly the fatal r^ad,
And learn, that vengeance waits the foes of GOD!
Great Joshua heard; and though his feeling mind
To crimes was gentle, and to misery kind;
Fierce on the youth he cast a dreadful eye,
That withered all his strength, and bade him die.
And could no honor, and no law, control
The grovelling wishes of thy gloomy soul?
How durst thou, impious, face the Almighty rod,
Put forth rebellious hands and steal from GOD?
Did thou not know, weak man! the avenging Sky
Traced thy dark footsteps with all-searching eye?
Did thou not fear, amidst the gloomy deed,
Its vengeance bursting on thy guilty head?
Did thou not fear the stings of conscious shame?
The thunder's terror; or the lightning's flame?
Go, raise to Heaven the sad, repenting eye,
A Heaven that hears, when Misery li^ts her cry!
Perhaps so^t Mercy yet may lend an ear,
While thy (...) glimmers in his last career*
Not pity'^ ^i^h, but ^o^ly's, hides from view
The wretch* whom Justice awful feet pursue.
Go then, unpardoned, sink in shame forlorn,
Of Heaven the victim, and of earth the scorn;
A warning lamp, over guilt's benighted way,
To light bewildered error back to day.
He spoke. The victim, with dread horror pale,
Walked trembling onward to a distant vale;
His look of anguish asked a hand to save,
And Pity's eye pursued him to the grave.
Mean time around their Chief the princes stood,
And kind compassion in their bosoms glowed:
When robbed in fair attire, two strangers came,
And bowed respectful, at the Hero's name.
One, passed his strength, was graced with manly scars,
Crown of the brave, and palm of glorious wars;
Tall was his frame, his countenance roughly kind,
And his calm front with honest boldness shined.
Dressed in light robes, as flowers adorn the wild,
In nature's prime his young companion smiled
Sweetness ineffable. Devoid of art,
His eye, soft-glowing, looked the friendly heart.
Hail strangers, hail! the mighty Hero cried,
Whose port bespeaks a nation's fairest pride.
Bring your kind hands the peaceful branch from far?
Or pant your bosoms for the fate of war?
The elder chief replied -- From Gibeon's king
Our friendly hands no hostile message bring.
Though once in sight renowned, now silver age
Serenes his brow, and cools ambition's rage.
It Is his first glory, Gibeon's weal to increase,
To soothe sad woe, and widen human bliss.
Passed are five morns, since round the extended plain,
With fond impatience, rushed a chosen tr^in,
Over rocks, and streams, the nimble deer pursued,
Traced the wild marsh, and scoured the devious wood.
From the lone mansions of the unpierc'd shade,
At once deep cries our wondering ears invade.
Led by the unknown voice, we nimbly hied
Through the thick grove, and strait the scene descried
'Twixt two rough savages, whose hungry eyes
Lowered death, and ruin, over their helpless prize,
Fair as the star of morn, a lovely maid.
In pangs of terror, called in vain for aid.
Her robes embroidered loosely met the view;
Her hair, unbound, in wild disorder fl^w;
All pale she stood, and to the pitying sky
Stretched her white hands, and raised a piercing cry.
In vain, on terror's wings, the caitiffs flew;
His eager sword this generous hero drew;
Their heads in twain the steely vengeance clave,
And hungry vultures yield the horrid grave.
To Gibeon's domes we led the beauteous fair,
Repo^'d on down, and nursed with tenderest care.
Pleased with our pains, her sweet, angelic tongue
Strange truths divine, with heavenly music, sung.
Of nature's Sovereign Lord, the tale began,
How earth was formed, and how created man;
How the tall mo^ntains heaved their cloud-wrapp'd spires,
And heaven was starr'd with thousand thousand fires.
Then too she told how, roused to fearful ire,
JEHOVAH bade the deluged world expire;
Thy nation's rise; the rod of Sovereign power,
That shook proud Egypt's realms from shore to shore,
The cleaving main; the wonders of the wild*
Where hard rocks slow'd, and sands with ve^dure smiled;
^ood, shower'd from heaven, perfumed the morning blast,
And quails in millions peopled all the waste.
I^ these dread scenes, Aradon's mighty mind
The clear displays of boundless power divined;
Scenes nobler far than ancient sages knew,
Than age ever taught, or airy fancy drew.
At once, inspired with eager zeal to learn
What wondrous truths the glorious scheme concern*
This prince, his only hope, the monarch cho^e.
And joined with me, his pleasure to disclose.
Sweet peace by us his friendly heart demands;
His gold he proffers, and his warlike bands;
At thy request, to arms the thousands fly,
With thee we conquer, or with thee we die.
Should thou consent, some bright, and generous Sage,
Famed for pure manners, and grown wise with age,
Skilled with unseen, yet all-persuasive art,
TO inform the mind, and softly win the heart,
Whose tenets, nobly raised over pride, and strife,
Grace the fair conduct of a virtuous life,
He asks, to spread Religion's sacred sway,
To lure his sons to heaven, and point the way.
And O what price immense can thou demand!
What golden hoards? or boundless breadth of land?
One precious prize our grateful hands restore,
Unbought by gems, or loads of shining ore,
In thy own tent, behold thy favorite fair,
Child of thy choice, and darling of thy care!
Thus spoke Hareshah. While glad smiles expressed
The Leader's joy, he thus his chiefs addressed.
Even now, propitious, on our lengthened toils
Behold the all-watchful Eye complacent smiles!
In other realms our growing fame is heard,
Our triumphs numbered and our Guardian feared.
But say, brave princes, shall these bands be tied?
And Gibeon's sons to Jacob's heirs allied?
Shall some blessed sage her thousands teach to rise
To peace, to tru^^, to virtue, and the skies?
Your choice I (...) -- he said. Quick Hanniel rose,
Whose life was contest, and whose joy to oppose.
To save the suppliant race his wish inclined,
For Heaven had formed him with a feeling mind:
But well he knew how fair his matchless art
Could gild the latent mischiefs of his heart;
How thousands on his words enchanted (...),
Touched by the magic of his wi^y tongue.
All paths with him were smooth, that showed a name,
Though slaughtered nations paved the road to fame.
Thrice rose the chief to thwart the Leader's cho^^e,
And thrice strange faltering seized his opening voice;
^ar round he casts his keen, experienced view,
And peace, the wish of every bosom, knew;
With shame his dauntless front was covered over,
And the cheek blushed, that never blushed before.
Pleased the great Leader saw his failing eye,
And voice, in vain, attempt a base reply,
Then smiling thus -- Untaught the wiles of art
I see mild aspects speak the friendly heart.
Yes let fair Peace, over Gibeon's happy land,
Raise her sweet voice, and lift her sacred wand.
Against hostile realms alone our falchions rise,
Foes to high Heaven, and victims of the Skies.
But far removed from Israel, very far,
Be every wish to extend the waste of war:
To sooth vain pride with conquest's dreadful name;
To pamper avarice with the spoils of shame;
To take one hour from man's too hasty doom,
Or force one widow to a husband's tomb.
From death's sad scenes, and battle's horrid toils,
The real hero's generous mind recoils:
When swords alone can plead the righteous cause,
The crimson steel his hand reluctant draws;
Grief walks his partner to the dreadful plain,
And glory's mansions prove the haunts of pain.
It Is Israel's boast, the human weal to inc^ease;
To stretch the reign, and nurse the arts of peace;
The fierce, the wild, to tame; the weak defend;
Late to begin, and soon the strife to end;
To teach vain man the bliss to virtue given,
And with new saints to enlarge the bounds of heaven.
But now, brave chiefs, to Joshua's tent repa^r --
My fond heart pants to find the lovely fair --
Her sire, in Edom's realm, our nation joined,
Urged by the dictates of a virtuous mind:
Her, a sweet babe, his hand indulgent bore,
To virtue formed, and nursed in sacred lore.
As some bright lilly, daughter of the morn,
Swells its young leaves, and bids its splendors burn;
Fair, and more fair, the expanding beauties glow,
Dance in the sun, and shame the driving snow;
So, born for heaven, still brightening to the view,
From truth to truth, from charm to charm, she grew;
Soft was her temper; all her thoughts refined;
Beauty her form, and virtue was her mind.
Now at the tent arrived, the fair they found;
With many a lovely maid encompassed round;
With smiles of joy, their friend the virgins hailed,
And gentle tears on every cheek prevailed.
When first her Sire appeared, around his form
She cast, with sweetest grace, each snowy arm;
Pleased the great Hero eyed his lovely child,
And gave the fond embrace, and over the charmer smiled.
Sweet maid! he cried, where rov'dst thou from the plain?
With tears we sought thee, but we sought in vain.
Far in the wood, replied the fair, I strayed,
No care disturbed me, and no fear dismayed;
Charmed with the flowers, that, undistinguished, smiled
With solitary beauty round the wild.
A plumed musician, on her verdant throne,
Hymn'd, with soft transport, to the falling sun.
Slow I approached; the bird before me flew;
I heard the sound; how could I not pursue?
So long I wandered, day forsook the sky;
I gazed, and gazed; but found not where to fly.
In different paths, I roam'd the woody plain;
But faint, and trembling, still returned again.

The wolf began to howl; and all around,
The hungry panther shook the shuddering ground;
Lou^ ^^ar'd the approaching lion's dread alarms,
And death rushed by me, in a thousand forms.
The long, long, dismal night at length was gone;
And cheerful day with pleasing beauty shone.
Hushed was the world, save where, along the wood,
Rung the soft current of a silver flood.
Down verdant banks, with trembling steps I strayed;
Each breeze alarmed me, and each leaf dismayed;
Till, near the confines of the lonely stream,
Rose two barbarians, as the tiger grim.
My hated garb displeased their savage eyes,
And female weakness bade their lust arise.
O why was strength to miscreant villains given?
Why lovely virtue left unarmed by heaven?
Why must the helpless fair-one's glory stand
A prey, for every monster's brutal hand?
Thus mourned my heart; when Elam rushed to sight,
Clave the dire foes, and calmed my wild affright*
At once low-whisper'd Virtue's heavenly friend --
Weak are the fair, that heroes may defend.
She spoke. The blush that gives the brightest charm,
Glowed in her face, and told her heart's alarm.
Skilled in the science of the human soul,
The experienced Chief beheld her passions roll,
Smiled at the expressive language of her eye,
The dancing bosom. and the deep drawn sigh*
On Elam's face he turned a searching view,
Traced his young flame, and all his wishes knew.
Often on the virgin glanced his earnest gaze;
She glance for glance, and blush for blush, repays;
Their eyes prove faithful to the melting heart.

No pride of beauty waked his young desires;
Nor eye that sparkles, fraught with ^ambent fires;
Nor cheek, that gaily shines with morning glow;
Nor downy bosom, dipped in spotless snow.
He sighed for charms of nature more refined,
The Maker's image, in the fair one's mind;
Such charms, as found in heaven, delight improve,
And placed in angels prompt an angel's love.
Thus while they paused; with sweet, and modest grace,
^ear in his eye, and blushes over his face,
The trembling youth began -- O Chief divine!
My parent's voice thou heard'st, disdain not mine.
To this bright maid my wishes would aspire --
O blame not! frown not on the spotless fire! --
Thou know the joy her virtues yield to thee;
Then think her hand were paradise to me*
Pleased the Chief saw his eyes with fondness shine,
And mien all modest, merit's faithful sign,
And thus -- O fair! it is thine alone to choose.
Say, must this heart so soon its darling lose?
Can thou to Elam yield a willing hand,
And seek a guardian in a distant land?
With voice sincere, unused her thoughts to hide.
And bosom frank, the virgin's lasting pride,
The guise, low-creeping Cunning must approve,
Fair mark of worth, and friend to virtuous love,
The maid replied -- O sire! it is bliss to me,
To be by him beloved, approved by thee.
The Chief returned; Blessed heir of spotless fame!
Thy choice and wisdom ever ask the same.
Receive, brave Elam! Joshua's favorite care,
As angels virtuous, and as Eden fair.
Her hand, her heart shall heal thy bleeding mind,
Warmed with pure love, and graced with truth refined,
Thy fainting strength, thy languid eye inspire,
Improve thy joys, and wake the hero's are,
Charm, with soft tenderness, thy griefs away,
And gild alike the darkness and the day.
And thou, brave Elam! still, as morn returns,
While early transport in thy bosom burns,
On firm foundations let thy fondness rest,
Nor cold indifference canker in thy breast.
Know, all the vows by heedless lovers given,
Though often on earth forgot, are sealed in heaven:
Then let thy fond connubial actions show
Truth was the language of the lover's vow.
And thou, my child, to Heaven thyself approve*
Act all the soft commands of duteous love:
So shall your lives serenely dance away,
And bliss unclouded light the setting day.
But now, brave friends, let pleasure round us roll;
Enjoy the genial feast, and share the bowl;
Three days, with me, and every pleasure, stay*
The fourth glad morn shall gild your homeward way*
Thus he. The feelings of each grateful breast
With manly dignity the chiefs confessed.
In converse mild they sat. With busy care,
The attendant train the cheerful feast prepare;
With kindly warmth the smoking ^auldrons glow,
And sweet through either rising odours low.
So vast, so various, was the Leader's mind,
It roved through every region, unco^fin'd;
From scenes sublime, with soft transition, ran
Through all the duties, all the weal, of man;
At once his friends, his race, his Maker, served;
At once his own domestic bliss preserved;
In nice dependence ranged the servant train,
And over his house bade beauteous order ^eign.
Through all their minds Religion's influe^^^ ran --
Men, true to Heaven, he knew were true to (...) --
Her sons be chose; and with all-(...) sway,
Rewarded, ruled, and led in virtue's way:
Hence, rich return of all his watchful toil,
No murmur pained him, and no household broil.
Peace round his mansion shed her influence mild,
And cheerful, friendly, each domestic smiled.
Now the loved maid had 'scap'd from savage bands,
With twofold pleasure, wrought their active hands.
So just, so gentle was her angel mind,
To want so bounteous, and to all so kind,
Her, as the Leader, each alert obeyed,
And thought it bliss to please the heavenly maid,
Mean time, selected for the genial feast,
To Joshua's tent came many a princely guest;
Their courteous hearts the noble strangers greet,
And hail the fair with gratulation sweet.
Over a vast board a wide pavilion spread,
With grandeur shined, and cast a pleasing shade.
There sat the guests; there cates delight the soul;
There wines inspiring tinge the spacious bowl:
They taste, enjoy, and, with light converse gay,
In calm oblivion roll their cares away.
Over all great Joshua shone, with aspect mild,
Cheered every guest, and with soft splendour smiled:
Touched by his eyes, each heart with rapture glowed,
And sweet complacence every face o'erflow'd.
So round the immense the sun's broad glories stream,
Spread boundless life, and pour the etherial flame;
Warmed with pure light, the golden planets roll,
And smile soft-beaming joy from pole to pole,
In endless pride, at beauteous distance, rise,
Swell the great pomp, and glad the earth, and skies.
There, like the day star, beauteous Irad shone,
His splendors lessening in the nearer sun;
Full on the Chief a sparkling eye he turned,
And as he gazed, with bright ambition burned,
Marked all the glories of his awful f^ce,
His solemn grandeur, and his matchless grace;
While hoary Hezron watched with boding eyes,
And saw, well-pleased, the future hero rise.
There too, in transport brightened Caleb's pride,
With tears, embracing Elam's lovely bride;
Yet felt soft pain, to see her favourite's charms,
The destined treasure of a stranger's arms,
To see her days at distance doomed to roll,
And mingling friendship soothe no more the soul.
In easy converse passed the hours away;
Each face shone cheerful, and each heart was gay;
In glad succession went the goblets round,
And blended voices gave a jocund sound.
Mean time thronged numerous round the Leader's door,
The stranger, orphan, widow, and the poor;
Called from each tribe, by Joshua's kind command,
A rare-felt joy inspired the friendless band;
They feasted, sang, and in the dance combined,
Poured forth the raptures of the oblivious mind:
Then, moving various, over the camp they spread,
Each bliss imploring on the Leader's head.
When now the feast was over, the sun drew nigh
The gilded borders of the western sky:
Forth to the temple marched the illustrious train,
The thousands gathering over the extended plain.
From a tall rock, amid the silent crowd,
The suppliant Hero raised his voice aloud --
O thou, whose hand illum'd yon rolling fire,
Stretched the wide plains, and bade the hills aspire,
Ruled by whose power, the stars unnumber'd rise,
And swift-wing'd lightnings flame athwart the skies,
Storms ride majestic over the etherial plain,
And wake the sleeping thunders of the main!
Empires, at thy command, arise, and fall;
And flight and triumph hasten at thy call!
Disclose, O Power Divine! thy sovereign voice --
Does combat please thee? combat is our choice --
Do^s peace delight thee? peace alone we prize,
Led by thy will, and guided by thine eyes.
By thee this land to Abraham's race was given,
'Till suns withdraw, and stars are lost in heaven:
If now the bright possession GOD ordain,
And crowns await us, on the crimson plain,
By some great sign the eternal smiles display,
And point our footsteps to the fierce affray!
At once a hollow wind began to roll,
As distant thunders rumble round the pole;
The fields grew black, the forests felt the alarm,
And swift through either rushed a cloudy storm,
High heaven all trembled with the dreadful sound,
And peals on peals, convulsive, shook the ground.
Far round the sacred dome the darkness spread;
The sun's clear splendour vanished in the shade:
Red flames burst forth; the conscious mountains nod,
And the world smokes beneath the approaching GOD.
In silent awe, the camp astonished stood;
And each burned fiercely for the day of blood.
Fixed in still wonder, gazed the stranger pair,
And marked, with anxious mind, the darkening air,
The dome, invelop'd in the sable shroud,
And peals deep-murmuring in the hollow cloud:
With solemn look, each frequent eyed his friend,
And felt, instinctive, half-form'd prayers ascend.
Mean time the Leader every chief commands --
Two days, let peace refresh the fainting bands;
The third glad sun, awaked by trumpet's sound,
Shall light our falchions to the deathful ground.
Sleep, hapless Ai! thy last returning day
Soon gilds thy turrets with a pitying ray.
And let the chief, the important charge who owns,
Of all our wealth, our wives, and blooming sons,
Bid a long trench wind through the tented ground,
And guardian walls the spacious camp surround.
He spoke. With joy the attendant chiefs obeyed,
And round the camp the glad commands conveyed.
The squadrons ardent wait the appointed morn,
Cleanse their blue shields, and polished coats adorn.
So Joshua willed; for well the Hero knew
How glittering s^eel allured the ravished view;
Thence praised the chief, in shining neatness armed,
Averse from toys, but with true beauty charmed;
And thence in glorious panoply he blazed,
A great example, acting all he praised.
Now round the world pale Eve her sadness threw;
Still, solemn darkness cloudy curtains drew:
Through the wide camp the Leader traced his way,
To learn what wishes marked the busy day.
Ai, full in view, each heart to combat fired,
And with gay prospects every breast inspired.
No thought of Egypt boding minds embraced;
No childish fear even vulgar souls disgraced*
In deep oblivion sunk the painful wound,
And fierce impatience hoped the embattled ground*
Pleased the great Hero heard the exulting strain,
And wandering, listening, sought his tent again.
When now the morn looked mildly from the east,
To Joshua Elam thus his voice addressed --
O Chief of Israel, crowned with grace divine!
Let health's green garland round thy temples twine.
To bless mankind be still thy loved employ;
To serve thy Maker still thy sacred joy;
No hour of thine to wasting grief be given;
Let each more brightly roll, and antedate thy heaven!
But now, his years impatient of delay,
My hoary father hopes our homeward way.
Indulge, great prince, our eager wish to bear
The rapturous tidings to his longing ear.
His soul rejoiced will ^mile at nature's pains,
And life flow swifter through his icy veins.
Bid us with speed our destined path resume,
And bless a parent, sinking in the tomb.
He spoke. Hareshah joined the youth's request:
Even Mina's eyes a secret wish confessed.
In love's kind heat, like ice in summer's ray,
All former ties, dissolving, pass away;
To newfound friends the soul oblivious flies,
New objects charm us, and new passions rise.
The Hero saw, and kindly bade depart
The loved, the long-lost favorite of his heart;
With arms impassioned clasped the bright-eyed fair,
Kissed with fond look, and dropped a tender tear.
On gay-dress'd camels, toward the setting day,
With converse sweet, the lovers bent their way;
Like two fair stars, that shed a lonely light,
And sink in clouds, above the mountain's height.
Two seers their steps attend, to point the way,
That ends in ma^^ons of unchanging day.
And now, over all the camp, the raptured throng
Crowd the wide plain, and wake the enlivening song.
Here cheerful thousands bid the walls ascend;
And broad, and deep, the lengthening trenches bend.
Here the strong arm the falchion learns to wield,
Or hurls the javelin over the measured field.
With shouts of praise the conquerors often are crowned,
And clanging bucklers swell the applauding sound.
Part, joined in crowds, in mimic fight engage,
Range their small hosts, and sport with seeming rage;
From force unequal here the vanquished fly;
There, with deep groans, dissembling victims die.
Mean time all-watchful. Hanniel, round the plain,

Me, cried the hero, Israel's thousands know
A fair unchanging friend, or open foe.
To generous war since Israel's voice is given,
To war I fly, and hope the smiles of Heaven.
Rouse then to arms; for glorious fight prepare;
Each thought of peace, each terror vile forbear:
Let glory's fire each warrior's breast inflame,
And deathless deeds shall brighten Jacob's name.
Thus he. The wile the thoughtless thousands drew,
Snared by soft words, and caught by gilding show;
For war, invigor'd, glowed the undaunted mind,
And kindling eye balls with new luster shined.
No walls they need, to stay the impending foe;
Yet, with light labor, swift the barriers grow;
Hope high in view displayed unmeasured spoil,
Soothed every pain, and lessened every toil.
As thus serenely passed the cheerful day,
And care* and grief, oblivious rolled away,

The cry of foes, and growing sound of war.
The sporting warriors, prompt at dread alarms,
Ceased from each game, and braced for fight their arms;
Over eastern fields, with rapid steps they hied.
And bands conjoining swelled the embattled pride.
From the wood hastening, flew, with wild surprise,
Two timorous youths, and raised lamenting cries,
With trembling voice, they said -- Of naught afraid,
Through yonder grove, with easy course, we strayed;
A savage band, by twining shrubs concealed,

It Is my own son; the spring of all my joys --

Nor yield your faithful Shallum childless to the grave.
The train, inspired, with nimbler footsteps flew;
Each pressed his shield, and each his falchion drew;
The youths, before them, showed the sadden'd way,
Where the fell heathens bore their hapless prey;
Where the close thicket wrapped the ambush'd force,
And bending shrubs, and footsteps marked their course.
Thence the glad train, with ease, the foe pursued,
And hoping, hastening, scower'd the devious wood.

Their hapless prize the heathens swift conveyed;

The heroes rushed: his friends glad Helon knew;
Loud rose his voice; the warriors eager flew;
While the bold heathens stayed their useless flight,
New-brac'd their shields, and strung their nerves for fight.
Shrill through the woods the clash of arms arose;
These, fix^d to hold, and fierce to rescue, those;
The forest shook. In front, confessed to view,
Full on the heathens raging Shallum flew.
One with his lance, and one with griding steel,
He slew: the victims gave a hideous yell.
To his son's voice he winged his furious course;
Naught stayed his speed, and naught withstood his force.
Where two huge heathens struggling Helon led,
He wildly sprang; one flew; the other bled:
With frantic joy he seized his raptured hand,
And urged him trembling toward the friendly band.
There scarce arrived, a javelin pierced his side;
He groaned, he sunk, grew pale, and fainting died.
Aghast, his darling's fate the fire beheld,
Then rushed delirious round the woody field;
On the fled heathens stretched his raging course,
O'ertook, and singly drove the gathered force:
Three fierce he flew; the rest, in devious ways,
Fled over the field, and 'scap'd the hero's chase.
At length returned, with a deep, bursting groan,
In strong embrace he clasped his hapless son.
Pressed to his bosom, bore him over the plain,
And* mid the weeping warriors, sought the camp again*
