THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK V.
NOW sunk the sun beneath the western main,
And deepening twilight shaded every plain:
To the known tent untroubled Irad sped,
And forth, with proffered hand, Selima led.
Through southern fields they traced their easy way,
And love, and rapture, changed the night to day.
The western beam decayed: the expanding sky
Spread clear, and boundless, to the attentive eye:
Scarce filled, the moon ascends the vaulted even,
And slow behind rolls on the pride of heaven;
With joy, the unenvious planet^ round her play,
^oin their glad beams, and swell the mimic day;
From star to star the mingling luster flies;
Unmeasured beauty clothes the lucid skies;
Hushed in calm silence sleeps the world serene,
And floating splendour gilds the shadowy scene.
Round the mixed glories of the spacious sky
The pair instinctive turned a raptured eye,
From scene to sc^ne with rising wonder ran,
And mild, with accent sweet, the maid began --
In yon broad field what scenes of glory shine!
The bright effusions of a source divine!
Great as the hand, that formed yon lucid way!
Fair as the morn, that opened immortal day!
In earliest youth, when first my feeble mind
In nature's works celestial power divined,
To those gay regions fancy stretched her flight,
And roved, and sported, mid the gems of light.
For whom, I cried, ascend yo^ glowing fires?
What favorite firstborn of the ange^ic choirs?
Those azure curtains? that sublime abode?
A tent of glory for some darling God!
Say, loveliest Prince! for thy superior mind
Walks, with sure step, in wisdom's path refined,
Why rove so far the unnumber'd flames on high?
Why cast their endless beauty through the sky?
Is yon blue frame, hat limits morn and even,
The sapphire pavem^nt of some nobler heaven?
Are stars but gems of unborn light, that spread
With dust of gold the streets where angels tread?
Or if for man these works of glory shine,
For earth-born reptiles furniture (...);
Say why so s^range the acts of H^aven appear,
There such bright pomp^ such wondrous meanness here.
The Youth returned -- Fair as those lucid eyes,
All lovely maid! thy bright ideas rise.
In vain proud man, wi^h self-applause runs over
His arts of Egypt, and his Ea^^ern lore,
Thy soul, on nature's pinion^, takes her flight,
And* self-instructed, gains a nobler height.
When from the deep* ascended earth, and heaven,
To man, sole heir, the mighty (...) was given.
Un^i^e his sons, no guilt his mind (...);
His life, his lim^^^* no fierce diseases stormed;
Nor d^ath's cold poison pal^d his growing bloom,
Nor knew his feet the journey to the (...).
(...) beauty's purple splendour round him played;
Immortal Health his vi^^^ous limi^^ arrayed;
(...) of the (...),
(...), and b^ossom'^ in his eye.
Arrayed in endless light, his infant mind
S^one with fair truth, and glowed with grace refined;
Her robe sky-tinctur'd, Virtue round him threw;
Unchanging jubilee his passions knew;
Heaven's living* lamp, with clear, and constant shine,
Sunn'd the pure regions of the world within.
Far other glories then arose to view;
Parts answering parts, and beauties ever new.
With strong, bright charms the heaven angelic shined;
The varying prospect charmed the enchanted mind;
Soft strains of rapture bade all either ring;
The gales, all fragrance, shed the light of spring;
From stars, from moonbeams, life's sweet influence flew,
Inspired the streams, and glowed in fostering dew;
Bade with strong life the purpling fruits refine,
And warmed the bosom with a youth divine.
Then reigned fair Love, the immortal bliss of heaven;
Then social angels came on clouds of even:
Here traced new wonders of the omniscient Mind,
Strange to their world, and first on earth designed;
In countless forms, where love and beauty glowed,
And stamped a rival of the bright abode.
His hand such nature to the man assigned,
His form so tempered, and so wrought his mind.
All gave delight; where spring displayed her prime;
Or where blank winter froze the desert clime:
The vale's soft pride; the flower's etherial form;
The mountain's grandeur, and the solemn storm.
But when foul guilt debased the beauteous mind,
The skies grew dim, and sickening nature pined.
With converse sweet* no more kind angels came:
No (...) morning shed the eternal beam;
No more from starry realms life's influence fell,
And pe^ce, and Eden bade the world farewell.
Yet still with clear, though faded luster, glowed
The love, the greatness, of a bounteous GOD.
What though cold east winds withered all the plain;
Though blasts, and mildews shrunk the golden grain;
Pale evening's, skirts the frost, and damp o'erhung;
Air bred disease, and worms the fruitage stung:
Still over the mountains stars serenely rise;
Still the soft moonbeam trembles from the skies;
The sun, fair image of unborrow'd day,
Lights heaven, and earth, and cheers the boundless sea;
Reviving seasons, crowned with luster, roll,
And plains of plenty glad the expecting soul.
These splendid scenes surprise thy curious mind;
For worms too noble, and for foes too kind.
But not too noble, or too kind, they shine,
The works of wisdom, power, and love, divine.
From morn's gay bounds, to skirts of distant even,
They teach the hand, and spread the name, of Heaven;
In beauty, grandeur, make JEHOVAH known,
But mark, with faded charms, a world undone.
Yet these, could man the common bliss pursue,
Would gentle peace, and smiling joy, renew,
Light, with soft-beaming hope, the cheerful day,
And drive grim war, and cankering hate, away.
Thus spoke the Prince. The tender maid replies,
While her sad bosom heaves unbidden sighs.
Fair scenes of bliss thy living words disclose,
Realms of gay youth, and times of sweet repose.
Oh had our fire! but hence, ye wishes vain!
No fancied joy shall edge returning pain --
Yet too, too blissful is the fond employ,
To nurse gay hope, and dream unreal joy;
Abroad in fields of airy light to roam,
And fly the envenomed grief, that lurks at home.
Ah, had the fatal fruit, untasted hung,
What bliss had brightened! and what glory sprung!
In gentlest union these blessed hands had joined,
One wish informed us, and one soul intwin'd;
On some lone hill our envied mansion stood,
There rich perfumes in morning breezes flowed;
Sweet Peace around it waved her balmy wing,
And Youth unchanging dressed eternal spring.
There, O blessed lot! each innocent employ
Had formed, and cherished mild, domestic joy:
The walk all-pleasing, virtuous love refined;
Our flocks, our prospects, soothed the improving mind;
For me, the garden opened its spicy bloom;
For thee, soft vesture whitened over the loom;
Our growing bliss the sun delayed to see,
And the poor heathen been as blessed as we.
Ah dire reverse! while round this field of gore,
War's hoarse rough-grating clangors ceaseless roar;
While sons, and fathers, in one hour are slain,
And each bright youth must tempt the fatal plain;
While the sad virgin sees, with wearied eye,
No hope remains her, but to weep, and die;
While pain, and grief, and half-form'd joy invade,
And suns gay-rising set in mournful shade.
Kind, tender maid! the smiling Prince returned --
The hapless fall how sweetly have thou mourned!
Thy voice, all music, wins the raptured ear;
Yet more persuasive drops that melting tear.
But, O bright maid! by strong affections driven,
Let no fond wish oppose the choice of Heaven.
To man's first guilt ten thousand ills adjoin'd,
Writhe the torn limbs, and agonize the mind:
Pain, famine, toil, the sword, the ruthless wave,
Care, envy, broken faith, sad sorrow, and the grave.
Yet GOD's high acts unerring wisdom guides,
And boundless love his every choice decides.
Hence all events, and hence all beings right,
Best in their places, to best ends unite.
Hence from small ills unmeasured good shall flow;
Hence joys unnumber'd spring from every woe:
Through the vast whole the eternal glories shine,
One great I AM, all-beauteous, all divine.
Thus the great Prophet sung; and often my sire
With these blessed truths my tender heart would fire,
When, won to virtue, on his lips I hung,
And learned pure wisdom from his friendly tongue.
Heaven's high behest, had faithless man obeyed,
A peaceful earth his eye had still surveyed;
Mild hours and seasons soft over nature run;
His sons, in millions, spread to lands unknown;
To Eden's bowers the filial nations come,
Hailed their great fire, and owned their happier home.
While from his throne, supreme of all below,
He saw well-pleased, his mighty kingdom grow;
His subjects children, love his potent sway,
And one vast household spread to every sea.
But, sprung from earth, and still to earth confined,
No fairer bliss had flowed for poor mankind:
No law had given the high, stupendous claim,
To soar, and brighten in the immortal flame.
Now to those climes where, 'twixt delight and pain,
Expands, untravers'd, night's eternal main,
Worms, born of dust, may point their lofty way,
And seize the bliss of ever-rising day.
Often on the flower, embosom'd in perfume,
Thou see gay butterflies in beauty bloom;
With curious eve, the wondrous insect scan,
By Heaven ordained a threesold type of man.
First from the dunghill sprang the shining form,
And crawled to view, a hideous, loathsome worm;
To creep, with toil, his inch-long journeys, cursed;
The ground his mansion, and his food the dust:
To the next plant, his moment over, he drew,
And built his tomb, and turned to earth anew.
Often, from the leaf depending, have thou seen
Their tombs, with gold bedropp'd, and clothed in green;
There slept the expectant, 'till the plastic beam
Purged his vile dross, and bade his splendors flame.
Then burst the bonds: at once in glory rise
His form etherial, and his changing dies,
Full on the lucid morn his wings unfold,
Starr'd with strong light, and gay in living gold;
Through fields of air at large the wonder flies,
Wafts on the beams, and mounts the expanded skies,
Over flowery beauties plumes of triumph waves;
Imbibes their fragrance, and their charms out-braves;
The birds his kindred, heaven his mansion, claims,
And shines, and wantons, in the noonday flames.
So man, poor worm! the nursling of a day!
Springs from the dust, and dwells in humble clay;
Around his little molehill doomed to creep,
To drag life's load, and end his toil with sleep.
In silence to the grave his form descends,
And waits the trump, that time and nature ends,
There strength imbibes, the beam of heaven to bear:
There learns, refined, to breathe its fragrant air;
Of life the bloom, of youth the splendour, gains,
And, clothed in beauty, hopes empyreal plains.
Then, winged with light, the deathless man shall rise,
Sail through yon stars, and soar from skies to skies;
See heavens, over heavens, beneath him lessening roll,
And feel the Godhead warm his changing soul;
From beauty's fount inhale the immortal ray,
And grow from light to light, in cloudless day;
Mid morn's fair legions, crowned with grace, be known,
The peer of angels, and of GOD the son.
But O what scenes in that far region glow!
What crowns of patience! what rewards of woe!
From yon tall hill, when morn's inviting air
To woodland wandering lured thy chosen fair,
Thou know how sweet gay prospects to descry,
And catch new Edens with the ravished eye.
In living green, the lawns at distance lay,
Where snowy flocks moved round in vernal play:
High tower'd the nodding groves; the cliffs sublime
Left the low world, and dared the assaults of time;
Huge domes heaved haughty to the morning fires,
And the sun trembled round a thousand spires:
All heaven was mild; and borne from subject vales,
A cloud of fragrance cheered the enchanting gales.
Such pleasing scenes if this drear earth supply,
What scenes, what glories bloom beyond the sky!
There with strong life the plains immortal glow;
There Beauty bids her streams of rapture flow:
There changing, brightening, reigns the extatic power;
Smiles in each fruit, and burns in every flower;
In solemn domes, with growing pride, aspires;
Gems with fair stars, and robes in living fires;
Round the trees wantons; on the mountains blooms;
Charms in new songs, and melts in strange perfumes.
And O, of liquid light what seas extend!
What skies impurple! and what stars ascend!
But cease, my tongue! nor headlong rush too near
The sun, that kindles heaven's eternal year.
When great Messiah shall those gates unbar,
Where grief r^cedes, and pain, and death, and war;
Then freed from dross, from every stain refined,
And dressed in all the elegance of mind,
To her own mans^on shall thy Soul aspire,
And add new raptures to the sainted choir.
With love divine thy heart has learned to glow;
Smiled at each joy, and wept at every woe;
In each soft station amiably stood,
And sh^wn the bright amb^tion to be good;
The best, the loveliest daughter, sister, friend;
Thy life all virtue, and the heavens thine end.
Scarce, of thy years, can blooming cherubs claim
A purer conscience, or a fairer name.
Pleased as he spoke, an aged Form drew near,
The moon-beams whitening over his silver hair.
His quivering limbs a tattered garb arrayed;
A staff his slow, and faltering footsteps stayed --
Oh youth! he said, in wealth thy lot is cast;
Let humble Poverty thy bounty taste.
Large as thy treasure be thy heart to give;
Thy bread impart, and bid my children live.
Sire! cried the Youth, to Hezron's tent repair;
The poor, unfriended, never entered there.
To share his wealth the Heaven-sent strangers come;
There orphans, beggars, find a constant home.
His pious acts in sweet memorial rise,
And prayers of thousands bless him from the skies.
Returned the sage. To life's far distant end,
On thee may Judah's envied bliss descend!
From Asher's race I spring, nor of thy sire,
Nor thee, fair Prince! or clothes, or food require.
My highest wish the gifts of Heaven exceed;
Though small my portion, yet far less my need.
But O loved Youth! my faithful counsels hear;
Let hoary Age command thy listening ear.
Thy growth, thy beauty, nobler than thy peers,
Mine eyes attentive marked from earliest years:
I saw thy limbs in fair proportion rise,
And thy face smile the image of the skies.
Thy mind all-lovely, every voice proclaimed;
For sense distinguished, and for virtue famed;
Bounteous and brave thy heart; thy tongue discreet;
Thy manners courteous, and thy temper sweet.
Often on these plains when gathering armies spread,
The long van darkened, and proud ensigns played;
Absorbed, I saw thee war's gay splendors view,
Trace the deep files, and moving chiefs pursue;
I saw the martial flame instinctive rise,
And growing lightnings tremble in thine eyes;
I saw, and smiled; and Israel's voice approved,
That destined empire to thine arm beloved.
But still, impelled by strong desire to find
If Fame well sung the beauties of thy mind,
I watched thy steps, when evening hid the main,
Assumed these rags and sought thee on the plain.
For know, fair Prince! in Truth's unbiased state,
The proud are little, and the lowly great,
From man, man claims, of high, or low degree,
The courteous manners, I have found in thee.
Now over thy head have twice ten summers ^un;
The Youth is ripened, and the man begun:
Thy shapely limbs are sinew'd into force,
To hurl the dart; to speed the nimble course:
Yet on what plain in triumph have thou stood?
When, bold and active, dared the strife of blood?
No scar of thine attests the patriot wound;
Thine arm inglorious, and thy wreaths unbound:
Should thou, when Joshua sleeps, the scepter bear;
How shall thy untaught mind conduct to war?
How know what counsels wisdom bids embrace?
What strength to arm? the ambush where to place?
Where on the field to stretch the dreadful wing?
Or with what words of fire the languid arm to string?
Rise then, brave Youth! from ease unhonour'd rise!
Let sun-bright glory tempt thine eager eyes!
When next approaching combat threats the field
Seize the strong lance, and grasp the sheltering shield;
If Hezron grant, the van's bright station claim,
And leave the foremost in the chase of fame!
Ill fits vile ease a Prince of worth divine,
Whose countless graces fair as angels shine;
At home, unnotic'd, stretched in sloth, to lie,
While friends, while fathers toil, and bleed, and die;
Line 323. Twice ten summers) This is a mistake of the Sage.
To share the spoils distained with others' gore,
A mean, false plunderer, when the battle's over.
Then while to war thy bold companions run,
While deeds of glory, wreaths of life, are won;
On the dread sword while Israel's cause suspends;
While empire victory, ruin flight attends;
While in full view the field of promise lies,
And the brave arm shall win the unmeasured prize;
Demand thy share, thy share of danger claim;
The toils of danger give the crown of fame.
To thee, through tribes, through nations yet to come,
Let grateful Israel owe her prosperous doom;
Her endless rule; her land in beauty dressed;
Her stream of glory, and her ages ble^^'d.
Thus, in far distant times, when Joshua's name
Shall pass, all-fragrant, down the tide of fame;
When future heroes to their sons s^all tell
How Hezron triumphed, and how Sihon fell;
Combined with theirs, thy deeds shall waft along,
Swell the glad theme, and mingle in the song.
No shameful sloth, no dread of manly toil,
No mean, false wish to share in others' spoil,
No love of ease, the generous Youth replied --
To tents confine me, and to Hezron's side.
Far other wish my glowing mind inspires;
Fame wings my thoughts, and war my bosom fires.
When Glory's sons assembling hosts array,
The extatic view bears all my soul away.
My pulse beats high; my bristling hair ascends;
My heaving heart a thrilling anguish rends:
Sighs, prayers, and tears confess the growing pain;
But sighs, and prayers, and melting tears are vain.
By love, beyond my highest claim impelled,
My sire constrains me from the embattled field.
Youth, frowns the chief, to ruin heedless flies;
From arms refrain, 'till years shall make thee wise.
Go tell thy sire, the kindling sage returned,
Thy hated absence Israel long have mourned.
In forceful language, ask their wondering eyes,
Why sunk in sloth, their darling Irad lies,
Their voice demands thee to the important plain,
To generous toils, and glories bought with pain:
They pant, they burn, to see thy splendors shine,
Thy falchion triumph, and thy garlands twine.
Not fame alone, but duty points the way,
And truth and virtue chide the dull delay.
This said, the Ancient over the plain withdrew,
And, fading from the moon-beam, left the view.
As lost in silence stood the wondering pair,
Or mazed, bewildered, roved they knew not where,
A cloud ascending eastern skies overspread,
Involved the moon, and wrapped the world in shade:
A dim-seen luster clothed all heaven around,
And long, black shadows floated over the ground.
As deep and solemn the far whirlwind roars,
Or waves run rumbling under cavern'd shores,
With murmuring noise, over western mountains came
A broad, and dark-red meteor's awful flame:
Far over the woods, and plains, its sanguine hair
Streamed wild, and dreadful, on the burdened air.
As eastern groves its lessening light absorb,
Like thousand thunders, bursts the rending orb;
Wide-shooting flames the glimmering sky surround;
A gloomy glory spreads the twilight ground;
Loud over the world a long, hoarse echo roars,
And sad Canaan groans through all her shores.
Quick to the camp returned the astonished pair,
And half, in broken slumbers, lost their care.
Over anxious Irad hovering visions played,
Called up ^air scenes, or dismal terrors shed;
Often from his co^ch, in act to smite, he sprang,
And often his voice in shouts imperfect rang.
When first through broken clouds the morning shined,
In purpose firm he fixed his doubtful mind;
At Hezron's feet, with graceful reverence stood,
And claimed the blessing, ever with joy bestowed.
With dawning smiles, he blessed his lovely son,
And sweet complacence round his aspect shone.
Will Hezron bend his ear? the favorite cries --
Speak, my beloved -- the indulgent sire replies.
Thou know my bosom feels the warrior's flame,
Sighs for gay arms, and pants for generous fame;
For Israel weeps, to aid her cause aspires,
And burns tumultuous with resistless fires.
When next our host the shining falchion wield,
Blessed sire! command me to the embattled field.
Youths, over whose heads a few more months have run
In sport, the peers, the rivals of thy son,
In glory's bright career with heroes join,
And their fair names even now begin to shine.
Grant, best of parents! grant one blissful day,
And threefold duty shall thy love repay.
Why do thou bring -- the anxious sire replied --
The dread request, my love has often denied?
Why must thy sire his favorite treasure lose?
Why will thy heart the path of danger choose?
That path, concealed where various evil lies,
And the brave perish, while the dastard flies.
More circling summers have those youths beheld;
Th'accustom'd age commands them to the field.
Scarce nineteen suns thine infant eyes have seen;
Secure from shame, enjoy thy hours serene.
Let truth, let wisdom be thy virtuous care;
And the sweet converse of thy darling fair.
Still with thy partners draw the mimic field;
The javelin hurl, the heavy falchion wield:
So taught their use, shall thou, when battles join.
With fairer names, with veteran heroes shine*
In marshaled hosts a nobler office claim,
And stride more swiftly in the chase of fame.
Returned the favorite -- To thy faithful son
Whenever thy choice, indulgent sire, was known,
No counter choice unduteous words confessed,
But my sole answer was obedience blessed.
When last mild evening closed the cheerful day,
Over southern plains I traced my careless way;
There as I gazed the works of Heaven around,
A chief, of Asher's race, my footsteps found --
Youth, cried the hoary sage, the changing sun
Beholds, well pleased, thy riper years begun.
The scenes of dangerous war thy breast demand,
And thy loved nation asks thine aiding hand;
Their eyes require thee on the hostile plain,
Nor let a nation claim thy aid in vain.
Go tell thy sire, while friends, while brothers die,
It Is shame, it is guilt, in torpid ease to lie.
His duty bids him dress thy limbs in arms,
And thine strait summons to the trump's alarms.
Haste, virtuous Youth! thy nation's voice obey,
And fly, where Glory points her envied way.
Ah sire beloved! to shame, to fatal shame
Yield not thy darling Irad's opening name.
Think, best of parents! with what stings of gall,
Contempt and scorn a generous mind appall.
Save me from piercing scorn; from ruin save;
From dastards snatch me; rank me with the brave;
Thy nation's call, more loud than thunders hear;
Though Irad fail, let Israel gain thine ear.
With anxious look, the unwilling sire replies,
The tears fast-streaming from his reverend eyes --
O son beloved! beyond expression dear!
The ground of every joy! and every fear!
This painful tale disparts my troubled soul,
And bids my tears in large effusion roll.
How can my heart to savage war resign
My wealth, my boast, my glory, all that is mine;
The child, the joy, the image, of my mind;
The best, the only trace, I leave behind;
To prayers long-tried, all-fervent, kindly given;
The richest bounty of indulgent Heaven?
From infant years thy lovely form to raise,
To lure thy mind to all that merits praise;
Against fatal snares thy youthful heart to arm,
With truth illumine, and with virtue warm,
Ten thousand sighs I breathed, ten th^^^^nd prayers,
Watched countless nights, and felt ^^^umber'd cares.
Each opening wish, each rising th^^ght, I scann^^;
Each newborn virtue nursed with fostering hand:
The flower etherial saw, with rapture, bloom,
Glow with strong light, and charm with choice perfume*
And each glad morn beheld my praises rise,
A grateful tribute to the bounteous skies.
As, touched with joy, thy beauties I behold,
Thy limbs invigorate, and thy thoughts unfold;
Thy pure complacence eye the all-lovely Mind;
Thy love, thy goodness flow to all mankind;
Thy aims expand beyond the flight of youth;
Thy tongue unvarying yield the voice of truth;
Thy cheerful bounty make the poor thy care;
Thy spotless mind affect so bright a fair;
Thy sweet obedience every wish forerun,
And my bliss double in my darling son;
Too blessed, I wish, my pains, my toils reviewed,
Each pain repeated, and each toil renewed.
But chief, when that bright fair, who gave thee breath,
Sunk, pale and hapless, in the arms of death,
Thy hand so gently soothed her long decay;
So sweetly guileful lured her pains away;
Whole nights, whole days, sustained her drooping head;
Dried her sad tears, and watched her weary bed;
Like some mild angel, sent from pitying skies,
Shed dew^ slumbers on her languid eyes;
Illum'd the grave, seren'd the rugged way,
And cheered each fainting Hope of future day:
Me from myself thy matchless duty stole,
And chained thee lovely to my inmost soul.
Now to far regions is that parent gone,
And, but for thee, thy sire were left alone:
From thee removed, no second self I know;
And, O blessed favorite! solitude is woe.
When winged, my sweet companion traced her flight,
A wildering gloom obscured the cheerful light;
Each joy was banished from my hapless doom,
And not a wish remained me, but the tomb.
Her tent, forsaken, seemed in shades to mourn;
Her empty seat implored her blessed return:
Friends graced my board; her vacant place I viewed;
Down rushed the tear, and every pang renewed.
Through distant fields I roam'd; the fields were sad;
No more her presence bade the flowers be glad:
A solemn twilight round all nature spread,
Drear as dun caves, that house the silent dead.
Alone in crowds I stood, in fields alone;
My hope, my friend, my lovely solace gone.
But thou wast left. In thy angelic face
Smiled her loved image, glowed her matchless grace:
To thee I flew; and, in thy duty, viewed
Her power to charm, her wish to bless, renewed.
That peace, the world beside could never give,
I found in thee, and loved again to live.
Too rich, too great, I own my Heaven-lent store;
On earth, if thou may live, I ask no more.
Shall then thy sire that dread persuasion hear?
Or feel the urgence of that forceful tear?
Ah! how can Hezron thy loved life destroy,
And yield the insatiate grave my only joy?
For, O fond Irad! all the pride of state,
Fair dreams, and painted bubbles, of the great,
No real joy, no gentle peace, contain,
But gay deceit, and undiscovered pain.
Whatever in Wisdom prompts a wish to live,
Soft, calm domestic scenes alone can give.
Should thou be slain, even these must ceaseless mourn;
No joy betides me, and no hopes return;
A poor, despairing stranger, here I stay,
'Till Death's loud voice shall summon me away.
But ah! to combat Israel Irad calls --
The piercing sound my struggling heart appalls --
Was all my bliss for Israel's weal bestowed?
And is a nation's voice the voice of GOD?
Go then, my son, may he thy bosom guard,
To triumph lead thee, and with fame reward;
Bright, and more bright, extend thy prosperous doom,
Or speed my footsteps to an early tomb.
Thus the great chief; and rising as he spoke*
In his right hand a sword suspended took;
Forth from the sheath the blade refulgent drew,
And his sad eye balls kindled at the view.
Behold, brave youth -- with earnest voice he cried --
Thine is the sword, thy sire's, thy grandsire's pride;
By death of kings, and generous chiefs, renowned,
With wreaths ennobled, and with triumphs crowned.
When Egypt's sons, on proud Sabea's plains,
By Moses guided, poured their countless trains;
High in his haughty car a chieftain rode,
Bore down whole troops, and rolled through brooks of blood;
Deep in his breast, while thousands round him fell,
Thy generous Grandsire lodged this shining steel*
Then ceased the fight; Sabea's millions fled,
And the earth groaned beneath the piles of dead.

To Jahaz' deathless field when Sihon drew,
When combat thickened, and when dangers grew,
This arm, this falchion clave the monarch's side,
And low on earth abas'd his impious pride.
From Hezron's hand the honored gift embrace,
Dread of thy foes, and glory of thy race;
And while thy arm their weapon learns to wield,
Let the same spirit prompt thee to the field.
Each wild excess, each useless danger shun;
But first in virtue's course auspicious run:
Outstrip thy peers; To Joshua's height aspire;
Let real glory all thy wishes sire:
Let mine, my sire's, my tribe's, my nation's fame
Imbibe new splendors from thy added name.
Yet not one fear my b^ding mind alarms,
Lest Irad's deeds distain his parent's arms;
I know thy generous mind; and, fore'd to yield,
Assured, behold thee grace the embattled field.
And oh ^ will thou, whose hand from every foe
My life preserved, and soothed in every woe,
My darling son defend! from thee he came;
Scarce born, I gave him to the eternal Name;
Thine are his virtues; round his youthful head
A guardian shield may thy good angel spread.
Thus spoke the chief. In Irad's feeling soul
A strange, tumultuous joy began to roll:
As often to unfold his grateful heart he tried,
The suffocated sounds in silence died.
Down dropped the sword; and strait, with homage due,
The Youth enkindling from the tent withdrew;
Quick to the lovely fair-one traced his way,
And strove the tumult of his thoughts to allay.
Her in the tent, with maidens compassed round,
Select companions of her sports, he found.
There, sweetly welcomed with instinctive smiles,
He smoothed his face with new, but harmless wiles,
And, while soft art her tender mind prepared,
His own design, his (...) consent declared.
With guarded lips he spoke; but dire surprise
Pierced her sad heart, and gloom'd her starry eyes;
With one deep sigh, she felt her strength decay,
Slid to the ground, and breathed her life away.
Quick to her aid the Youth impassioned flew:
And, with the virgins, bade her life renew;
Again revived the splendour of her eye,
And tinged her cheek with health's ^ranscendent die.
O best beloved! with tender voice he said --
Let not such anguish wound my beauteous maid!
Let cheerful hope thy timorous thoughts inspire,
And thine eye languish with a brighter sire!
When over my head a few short days shall roll,
My hastening feet must reach the appointed goal*
To manhood grown, the law, from heaven revealed,
Resistl^ss calls me to the imbattled field.
If Israel's sons my falchion earlier claim,
And kindly summon to the path of fame,
Why should thou mourn? it is duty points the way;
When duty calls us, safety bids obey.
Thou know when evening last the skies attired,
The sage, reproving, generous thoughts inspired;
First from his mouth my nation's choice I knew;
And swift to war my soul obsequious flew:
No place, no hope, to vile delay was given;
The call of nations is the call of Heaven.
Led by his voice, I trust his guardian care;
With equal ease he saves in peace, and war.
The same good hand, that through the woodland shade,
To friends, to safety, loveliest Mina led,
Though thousands fall* may Irad's bosom shield,
And wing the averted javelin through the field.
Thus he, with softest voice, and fondest eye --
Then stopped; and anxious, hoped the maid's reply.
She, plunged in grief, and lost in dread amaze,
Sat silent, solemn, fixed in mournful gaze:
With tenderest action on her looks he hung,
And thus vain solace tunes again his tongue*
But, doomed to fall, should Heaven my life demand,
And death betide me from a heathen's hand,
I fall in virtue's cause. Far happier doom,
In that blessed path, to find a speedy tomb!
Than, lost in sports, or sunk in shameful ease,
To drag a worthless life, and swell in glorious days.
And O bright maid! without one guilty fear,
My thoughts can view resistless death draw near.
In that far clime, where joy extends her reign,
My pinioned soul shall spring to life again;
Strong with empyreal youth, shall trace her way,
And join the nations of immortal day.
Thence, when thy form is summoned to the tomb,
Perchance my spirit, winged with light, shall come,
Hail thy release from toil, and grief, and pain,
And raptured guide thee over the trackless main;
In bonds etherial there our souls be joined,
And prove the extatic nuptials of the mind.
With silent, sad, and discontented air,
And face averted, sat the listening fair.
While the deep woe her feeling bosom mourned,
With a long, heavy sigh, she thus returned.
With boding heart I heard the sage's tale,
But felt fond hopes the dire design would fail;
That Hezron's choice, so often tried in vain,
Would still confine thee from the fatal plain.
Yet thy loved maid, with gentle words, designed
To change thy wish, and sooth thy eager mind*
But my soul trembled at the dreadful light,
And every sense was lost in wild affright.
Now to dire fate my fondest hope must yield,
While empty fame allures thee to the field.
But O blessed youth! by soft entreaties won,
Where duty calls not, hideous danger shun.
Let not thy ardour fame's high impulse feel,
Tempt nearer fight, and try the deathful steel.
The fatal front to veteran warriors give;
Be thy rich boon, thy blessed reward, to live.
I know thy bosom burns with glory's fire;
I know what visions war's bright beams inspire.
I fear, would Heaven the cause were less to fear,
Lest thy bold footsteps headlong rush too near;
Lest, winged with zeal, on instant death thou fly,
And leave thy hapless maid to weep, and die.
For ah! on Irad all my joys suspend;
Grow with thy bliss, and with thy life extend.
Should then dread war compel thee to the grave,
The sad, untimely portion of the brave,
Whither, ah whither can Selima fly?
Where find a friend, to bid her early die?
Robbed of thy face, the world's a desert drear;
The house of pain, and grief, and cankering care;
Forlorn, and friendless, life's lone path I tread,
And ask no lot, but with the silent dead.
Nor all those joys, thou know to paint so fair,
Can sooth sad woe, or lighten dark despair.
With thee conjoined, ^ claim my only doom,
Alike well-pleased, or here, or in the tomb.
Scarce would my soul, without thee heaven explore,
Where the first joy shall be to part no more.
Oh, would the Chief thy anguish'd maiden hear,
And mark thy station in the humbler rear!
There no fell heathen would thy life annoy;
Nor fatal danger threat Selima's joy:
'Till age, 'till art, from sure experience won,
Had taught thee caution's every wile to shun.
And then, ah then might peace our days serene;
War cease to rage, and foes no more be seen;
Bliss, in glad streams, around our land extend,
And every sigh, and grief, and terror end.
Thus spoke the saddening maid. With pleasing guile,
The tender Prince recalled her vanished smile;
With tales amusive lured her grief away,
And cheered her soul with hope's inlivening ray.
Meantime, through Israel spread the rumor far,
That matchless Irad joined the coming war.
Charmed with the tale, a bold and generous train
Of youths, his rivals, thronged the vacant plain;
And there, with one glad voice, the hero chose
To guide their footsteps against the expected foes.
To him too youths the flattering message bare;
With modest grace, and sweet, becoming air,
Surpised he heard, and while their hearts he won,
Assumed the trust, and owned the honor done.
And now decayed the sounds of busy day;
The sun descending beamed his final ray;
In starry grandeur rose the boundless night,
And tempered either with a milder light.
As through the host a general silence flowed,
To Zimri's tent the watchful Leader strode,
And thus -- Brave chief, to Ai direct thy course;
Thy sole command awaits a chosen force:
Through the deep forest steer thy southward way,
Where stately portals hail the setting day.
When first the clarion's voice to conf^ict calls,
Forsake thy ambush, and ascend her walls;
Over all her domes let sudden flames aspire,
And her proud turrets sink in hostile fire:
Then through her northern gates direct thy way,
And lead thy squadrons to the fierce affray.
I go -- the chief replied. The moon's broad round
Looked in full luster on the tented ground;
Fair over the shadowy hills she gently rose,
And showed a path for A^'s exulting foes.
In glimmering steel, a long, refulgent train,
Stretched in just ^iles, and dazzled all the plain.
Slow to the wood their fading steps they pressed,
The Chief, in silent joy, retired to rest.
THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK VI.

ARGUMENT.
Morning. Army assembles under the command of Hezron, and Joshua. Irad
sollicits, and obtains a post, in the front of the western division. Orders.
Israelites march. Army of Ai. Characters of Oran, and Carmi. Battle. Feigned
retreat of the Israelites. Hanniel's disobedience, and overthrow. Joshua
rescues him. Signal of return to battle. Joshua's address. Battle renewed.
Joshua retires, and gives Caleb the command. Exploits of Irad. Exploits of
Hezron, and of Caleb, Death of Ludon. Oran. Death of Hezron. Exploits of Carmi.
His death* Irad rallies Judah. Joshua descends to battle, kills Oran, and puts
the Heathens to flight. Zimri, having set Ai on fire, comes out upon the rear
of the enemy. Final rout, and overthrow of the Heathens. Irad's distress at the
fate of his father. Interview of Irad and Selima. Evening.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK VI.
NOW dawning light concealed the worlds on high,
And morn in beauty clothed the cloudless sky:
Loud over the field the trump's shrill sound began,
And swift to arms the startled thousands ran;
From all the camp burst forth the numerous throng,
Shook their tall spears, and waked the martial song;
Wide waved their plumes, refulgent flashed their shields,
And spiry banners trembled over the fields,
South of the camp, in two deep squares they stood,
And fierce for combat, faced the plain of blood.
Before the western band great Hezron rose,
Joy of his race, and terror of his foes:
Averse from pomp, in useful steel arrayed,
Pleased, his just ranks the mighty chief surveyed;
Pleased to the well-known field of combat drew;
When duty called, his soul no terror knew
Of equal strength battalions eastward stood,
And high in front exalted Joshua strode.
By nature fashioned millions to control,
In peace, in war, the great all-moving soul,
His mind expanded looked existence through;
His heart undaunted danger never knew;
With calm endurance, toils and fears engaged,
Climbed as they rose, and triumphed as they raged;
Patient, serene, as ills and injuries tried,
Meek without meanness, noble without pride,
Frank yet impovious, manly yet refined,
As the sun watchful, and as angels kind.
His Maker first, his conscience next he feared,
All rights kept sacred, and all laws revered;
Each wandering friend, with faithful friendship blamed;
Each foe applauded, as fair merit claimed:
Alike his kingdom wealth and want approved;
The noble reverenced, and the peasant loved.
His form majestic, seemed by GOD designed
The glorious mansion of so vast a mind:
An awful grandeur in his countenance sat;
Calm wisdom round him cast a solemn state:
His deathful arm no single force withstood;
His speed, his skill, no vigor could elude;
His piercing eye his mighty soul displayed;
His lofty limbs resplendent arms arrayed;
With varied lightenings his broad falchion shone,
And his clear buckler mocked the rising sun.
Fair in the front of Judah's manly train,
A young gay band, adorned the gladsome plain.
Bright was their steely mail; their polished shields
With dazzling splendour streaked the smiling fields;
Soft breezes sported through their plumy pride;
Their trembling falchions glittered by their side;
(...) their bravery, and their thirst of fame;
(...) age, their stature, and their arms the same.
(...) fair front, more beauteous than the morn,
(...) ^loudless splendors orient skies adorn,
First of his race exalted Irad stood,
His soul inflamed, his falchion drawn for blood;
On Joshua's step^ his ceaseless eye was turned,
Charmed with the pomp, that round the hero burned;
Tall was his stature, lofty was his mien,
His eye refulgent, and his brow serene:
Topp'd with two snowy plumes, that played in air,
A silver morion crowned his auburn hair:
Far over the train his form sublimely shone,
By nobler arms, and manlier beauty, known.
So, when calm spring invests the sparkling plain,
And night sails silent up the etherial main,
Round the broad azure stars unnumber'd glow,
And shadowy luster robes the world below:
Throned in the western heaven young Hesper shines;
His silver car to nether realms declines;
Over the gay mountains smiles his living eye,
And sinks, in splendour, down the gladsome sky.
From host to host the Chief majestic strode,
Inspired their hopes, and steeled their souls for blood;
Their perfect ranks his skilful eyes divide,
And his great bosom swells with manly pride.
As towered the western square he bent his way,
Where hoary Hezron led the long array;
To meet his steps impatient Irad ran,
And bent one knee before the godlike man;
And thus -- To youth will Joshua lend an ear? --
-- Speak my brave son; thine every wish declare --
-- With gentle voice, he said -- The Youth replied --
See that fair train, inwrapp'd in steely pride!
In war, though young, our bosoms pant to shine,
And feel the wish, that brighter glows in thine.
Give, best of men! this brave, though youthful band
In the bright front of charging fight to stand.
Oh, by my father's toils in fields of blood,
Whose love this freedom, and this sword bestowed,
Indulge this ardent wish! nor let thy frown
Quench a young flame, that emulates thy own.
I grant thy wish -- the mighty Chief replied,
Smiling superior over his manly pride, --
Go, first of youths, defend thy Maker's laws,
And lift the falchion, in thy country's cause.
May GOD's good hand thy tender footsteps guard,
With caution bless thee, and with fame reward!
He spoke, and kindly raptured Irad reared;
His swimming eye the grateful mind declared;
Swift he returned, on high his shield displayed,
Shook his blue sword, and thought the fight delayed.
Near the blessed scene enraptured Hezron stood,
And life ran nimbler through his languid blood;
Charmed with the kind regard, to Irad given,
He kneeled to earth, and blessed all-bounteous Heaven,
That Heaven which gave, his every wish to crown,
The Chief to Israel, and to him the son.
Now, ranged for combat, wait the warrior bands,
And his brave leaders Joshua thus commands --
'Till this right hand exalt the javelin bright,
Let every rank conduct a mimic flight:
Slow, firm, and close, be moved the fair retreat;
Nor winged with ruin wild, and foul defeat:
Meantime a missive death let arrows rain,
And slings unnumber'd tempest all the plain.
But when the javelin's beams in either burn,
Swift to the fight let every rank return;
Each vigorous arm the sword's broad terrors rear,
Or hurl the vengeance of the slaughtering spear;
Brace firm the spacious shield; disdain to fly;
Rush to glad conquest, or with glory die.
He spoke: over southern plains, in long array,
To Ai's high walls the squadrons bent their way.
Undaunted Ai, the approaching storm beheld,
And roused her heroes to the darkening field;
Her chiefs command, her northern gates unsold,
Bright arms burst forth, and hosts to fight are rolled;
Like gloomy clouds, the blackening thousands rise*
And shrill-voic'd clarions thunder in the skies.
Two warlike chiefs the embattled heathens guide,
Their forms majestic clothed in golden pride.
Wrapped in blue mail, insufferably vain,
With cruel front, that frowned a stern disdain,
Around, dark Oran cast a sanguine eye,
Waved his broad shield, and dared the avenging sky.
Grim in the van, with lofty stalk, he strode,
And shook his spear distained with drops of blood,
Blood, by his hand, in ancient battles shed,
In wasted realms, and fields bestrew'd with dead.
Sheathed, in his hall the crimsoned weapon lay,
Lest cankering time should cleanse the stain away;
There, often retired, he turned it over, and over,
And with fierce transport viewed the purple gore,
There called to mind the orphans of his spear,
Smiled horrid over the scene, nor knew to drop a tear.
Behind him darkly rolled a cloudy band,
Roused to the war from many a distant land,
With various arms in one great host combined,
And various banners streaming on the wind.
Against Joshua's host the chief imperious strode,
And with fond prescience hailed the scenes of blood;
A gloomy smile arrayed his shaggy brow,
And thus his horrid joy began to flow.
Blessed be the Gods, who gave this rapturous hour!
For this their fires shall many a youth devour;
While their gored children bleeding parents view,
And tears in vain their lifeless forms bedew.
Warriors rejoice; yon troop forgets the day,
When Ai's brave squadrons swept their host away;
Soon shall our spears be bathed in brooks of blood,
And fields grow fruitful with a genial flood.
Against Judah's hosts, enclosed in burnished arms,
With matchless bravery and unrivaled charms.
Ai's dauntless sons to fight young Carmi led,
And now the helm first sparkled on his head.
Moved by his ceaseless sighs for martial fame,
His royal fire the parent's fears o'ercame.
Reluctant sent him to the deathful plain,
And fondly hoped his lovely steps again.
There pleased with fame's imaginary charms,
He clasped the phantom in his eager arms,
On the bright glories turned a raptured eye,
And gazed, and gazed, and fancied bliss was nigh.
Now, mid the grandeur of the deep array,
A dreadful space in gloom tremendous lay:
No banners wave in air, nor trumpet's sound;
But silent terror saddens all the ground.
Loud burst the clarion's voice, and trembling far,
Shoot the broad ensigns over the frowning war;
As thousand stars through kindling either stream,
Bright showers of arrows cast a transient gleam:
From slings tempestuous countless pebbles rain,
Whizz through the skies, and whiten all the plain;
The shrill helms clatter, death pursues the wound,
And prostrate heroes clothed the sprinkled ground.
So, when red summer burns the sultry pole,
Over darkening hills a cloud's black volumes roll;
Hoarse rush the winds; hoarse drives the rattling hail,
Batters the craggs, and tempests all the vale;
Deep groan the forests, torn their branches fall,
And one tumultuous ruin buries all.
Ere the loose combat long suspense had hung.
"Retire," the great command around them rung;
Then, closely wedged, recedes the yielding fight,
And well-feign'd terror clothes the mimic flight.
Swift towered their yielding foes the heathens spring,
Their bucklers blaze, their flashing lances sing:
Often they rush forward, often the bands retreat;
For Israel's host disdains a base defeat;
From ranks behind unnumber'd arrows shower,
And stones unnumber'd down the concave pour;
Thick fall the foremost, clanging arms resound,
And streams of crimson die the embattled ground.
Meantime, fierce Hanniel, burning still for fame,
And sickening still at Joshua's envied name,
Deemed this the destined hour, to pluck the crown
From the Chief's head, and plant it on his own.
Oh heaven, he cried, shall Israel ever flee,
The dupe of cunning, and the coward's prey*
Must these pained eyes again our ruin view,
Curse our wild counsels, and our follies rue?
Come every generous chief, whose bosom brave
To foul disgrace prefers a hero's grave,
Join Hanniel's path; and soon proud Ai shall see
A few, bold warriors yet disdain to flee.
Whatever my voice commands, my hand shall dare,
My deeds unspotted, as my dictates fair; --
Far nobler doom, to fall in manly fight,
Than share, with titled names in splendid flight. --
This said, his course the hero forward bends,
No chief applauds him, and no chief attends:
Two vulgar warriors, sad rebuff to pride!
Alone rush on, and clamor at his side.
Their dauntless course their raptured foes descry,
And well-aim'd lances glitter through the sky;
Thick round the warriors, sinks the hissing steel,
And death's cold hand the brave attendants feel;
In Hanniel's thigh expands a painful wound,
And the stunned hero raging bites the ground.
Swift to his aid, impassioned, Joshua flew,
Though well proud Hanniel's dark designs he knew,
Heard all the vaunts, the close injustice saw,
And felt the infractions of his prudent law:
Yet now the chief lay weltering in his gore;
Foes in distress to him were foes no more --
Over the pale form he threw his guardian shield,
And bore him languid through the dreadful field:
Thick shower the stones, the flashing javelins sing;
And his bright arms with ceaseless murmurs ring.
Borne by four warriors over the distant plain,
Reluctant Hanniel sought the camp again:
There friendly plants his dying strength renew,
And sleep's soft influence aids the balmy dew.
While Joshua thus -- Hence taught, ye warriors, know,
Wild, headstorng wishes guide to certain woe,
In peace, laws only claim a righteous sway;
In war, one voice commands, the rest obey.
Proud disobedience Heaven consigns to shame;
The path of duty leads alone to fame.
He spoke -- With awe the silent squadrons heard,
The precept reverenced, and the teacher feared;
Each saw, abashed, the terrors of his frown,
And pleased, condemned rebellion, not his own.
Meantime, brave Irad, on the western plain,
With pangs retired from Ai's contemptuous train.
As often the imperious taunt his rage inspires,
And his scorched bosom flames with eager fires,
Their utmost strength his vengeance promps to try,
He longs, he pants, to bid the insulters fly:
Often toward the host his course instinctive turns;
His drawn sword trembles, and his buckler burns;
But still his soul, in childhood taught to obey,
Restrains the wish, and backw^^^ ^urns his way.
Now with pure splendour glowed meridian light,
And Ai triumphant chased (...) imagined flight,
When gay in dazzling arms, great Joshua turned:
His eyeballs sparkled, and his bosom burned:
The glittering lance his mighty hand uprear'd;
Loud rose his voice, and distant squadrons feared.
Behold, he cried, yon sheets of smoke ascend!
What heavy volumes round the skies extend!
Brave Zimri's conquering arm, while Heaven inspires*
Bursts Ai's proud portals, and her turrets fires;
Now wheel your course; to active vengeance spring:
Brace the strong hand: the bloody falchion wing;
See, Heaven's propitious finger points the way!
Fear chains their limbs, and terror yields the prey;
Over our glad course commencing glory smiles,
And boundless treasures crown triumphant toils.
He spoke; the warriors eyed the exalted sign;
And thrilling bosoms owned the voice divine;
Swift wheeled, the ranks to combat vigorous rise;
Red lances shower, and shouts convulse the skies.
An equal ardour Ai undaunted brings,
Fronts the dire foe, and fierce to danger springs --
As, borne by warring winds, through either roll
Two rising storms, and cloud the northern pole;
Over some dark mountain's head their volumes driven
With floods of livid lightening deluge heaven;
Peal following peal, careering thunders fly,
Burst over the world, and rend the shuddering sky.
With equal noise the storms of war resound;
The blackening volumes cloud the hostile ground;
Through the shocked air in mingled tumult rise,
The conqueror's triumphs, and the victim's cries.
And now the chief to prudent Caleb's charge,
While the cloud thickens, and the sounds enlarge,
Commends the host that own his mighty sway,
And bends to distant rocks his backward way.
Here high in air he lifts the lance's beam,
And power divine supplies a ceaseless stream;
With pointed circles glows the weapon bright,
And casts the effulgence of excessive light.
Long over the plain, impatient to pursue,
Had panting Irad fixed an anxious view,
Sighed the great Leader's ^^^rning voice to hear,
Or catch the radiance of the expected spear:
The ready sword his hand all eager pressed;
The well-brac'd buckler gli^^er'd over his breast:
In the utmost western ranks he silent stood
And looked far onward through the field of blood:
Pained, lest the destined sign, forgot should fail,
Or (...) base dart the Leader's life assail.
But when the sun-bright point insta^^'d the air,
The blooming hero ken^'d the b^am afar;
To his brave peers, with ardent joy he cries,
And all the warrior sparkled in his eyes.
Lo, generous youths, on yon delightsome plain
Shines the fair javelin, wished so long in vain!
Now spurn the hated flight; to combat spring;
Let virtue rouse you, and let glory wing.
Now shall our sires, and now the Leader, know
What flames heroic in our bosoms glow;
Ai now shall learn, ^^taught our strength to slight
Not fear, but wisdom planned our seeming flight,
On their own heads redoubled vengeance feel,
Or fly inglorious from the conquering steel.
Rise then, brave youths, their impious scoffs repay;
My arm to triumph leads the envied way.
He spoke; the voice each active hero warms;
With dreadful din they clash their glittering arms,
Full on their dauntless foes impetuous fall,
And break resistless over the embodied wall.
As winter's shrilling blast begins to roar,
And drives, in gloomy rage, along the shore;
Torn, in it is path, the trees confusedly lie:
The white waves roll, the boughs tumultuous fly.
Not with less force, over piles of warriors slain,
Pours the bold band across the bloody plain;
Death leads their way: with youthful vigor light,
They deal swift vengeance through the dusty fight,
Regardless of the storm, that round them flies,
Of dying murmurs, and of conquering cries.
High in the van exalted Irad strode,
And now commenced the toils of death and blood.
When first his arm, immingling in the strife,
Drew the red stream, and spilt a human life,
(A lovely youth opposed his hapless head,
And with pure crimson died the infant blade)
Through his chilled veins a new, strange horror ran,
And half-form'd tears in either eye began;
In his young heart, unused to create woe,
Instinctive sympathy began to glow;
The dreadful scene he gazed, and shook to hear
The hollow groan and see pale death so near*
But soon fresh transports in his bosom rise,
Roused by shrill arms, and fired by barbarous cries:
Again his spirit claims the imbattled foe,
And bids two heroes to his falchion bow;
Through cleaving ranks he wings a dreadful way,
And clouds of rolling dust obscure the day.
Meantime in Judah's van great Hezron sped,
His voice arous'd them and his footsteps led;
With fixed firm course, the hoary hero strode*
His brown arms purpled with the bursting blood;
Ranks after ranks against his falchion rise,
And chief on chief in swift succession dies.
For now each breast such active vengeance warms;
They spurn the trifling toil of missive arms;
Each braces firm the shield, and joys to wheel
The surer vengeance of the griding steel.
Full on great Hezron's course the heathens rushed,
And the first chiefs by following chiefs were crushed:
In solemn pomp, against the growing storm
The mighty hero reared his moveless form.
In vain bright swords around him ceaceless hung,
Troops pressed in vain, and clattering armor rung.
So, on some hill, while angry tempests lower,
In stately grandeur, stands the moss-grown tower;
Loud roar the winds; impetuous drives the rain,
And all the fury of the etherial main;
Still, reared to heaven, it frowns with pride sublime,
Spurns the fierce storm, and mocks the waste of time.
Far distant, Caleb swept the crimson plain,
Guided the fight, and pil'd the numerous slain;
Round his great arm the cloudy squadrons hung;
Clash'd on his buckler countless weapons rung;
Chiefs after chiefs opposed his wasting course,
Met his broad steel, and felt its fatal force.
Ludon, the Hivites' prince, his arm defied
All rough with gold, and gay in barbarous pride;
With giant strength the heathen hurled his spear,
Its terrors quivering through the parted air;
Loud over brave Caleb's shoulders sung the steel,
And pierced a warrior's breast; the warrior fell;
His blue mail clang'd; to rise he tried in vain,
But writhed in dying anguish on the plain.
The mighty leader raised his sword on high,
Its transient lightnings circling in the sky,
Full on the Heathen's neck a griding wound
Sunk; the loosed head fell spouting to the ground,
Amazed, the Hivites saw their monarch lost.
And deathlike murmurs groaned around the host.
Near the bold leader Oran reared his steel,
Where the storm thickened, and the fiercest fell*
Imperious taunts provoke the rage of war,
Loud threats insult, and tumult sounds afar;
Wedged in a moveless throng, the battle grows,
Cries deeper roar, and shriller ring the blows.
With joy, unfeeling Oran strides the slain,
And hails the ruins of the accustomed plain;
No anguish melts, no wound his pity charms,
No fate impassions, and no groan alarms*
Through the red scenes he hews a raptured way,
And mingling darkness intercepts the day.
Meantime fierce Irad over the field is driven,
And boasts the assistance of a ^avou^ing heaven
Though new to war, with war his bosom glows,
And knows no transport, but the flight of foes.
In scenes of distant death bold Hezron stands,
Dies his blue arms, and pains his aged hands;
Full many a chief his veteran falchion crowns,
Thick flit the shades, and blood the verdure drowns.
Impetuous Carmi springs the chief to meet,
Conscious of youth, and light with nimble feet;
His arm all active strews the sanguine ground,
Wakes the deep groan, and deals the frequent wound:
Full on his angry sword the warriors rush,
Impel the upright, the falling heedless crush:
No chief the fury of his arm withstands,
And ruin widens over bold Hezron's bands.
Amazed, the hero saw the deluge spread,
And wide, and wider rise the piles of dead,
Flight first commence in hosts that own his sway,
And proud Ai hail a second conquering day:
From his sad bosom heaved a heavy groan;
Round the whole war he missed his favorite son:
Untaught to droop, he hopes congenial fire
May yet ward shame, and yet the troops inspire. --
Where now, he cries, are fled the boasts of morn?
The towering stalk? the brow of lifted scorn?
Then Judah's warriors promised deeds of fame,
Hissed impious flight, and spurned the dastard's shame.
Far other scenes now rend these hapless eyes;
The foe advances, and the boaster flies;
Broke but by fear, ye wing inglorious flight,
Giants in words, and maidens in the sight;
Oh had kind Heaven dispensed a speedier doom,
And this frail form in Bashan found a tomb!
Then had these palsied limbs, in peace reposed;
Unpain'd with shame, these eyes in triumph closed;
Pleased to the last, surveyed my favorite race,
Viewed no base flight, and bled for no disgrace. --
Hence, hence, ye timorous souls, to Joshua fly,
And tell the Chief, ye saw your leader die.
The hero spoke; and urged by passion's force,
On furious Carmi bent his aged course;
Awful in gleam of arms, the chiefs appear,
Here the bold youth, the white-hair'd hero there:
But ere his sword great Hezron could extend,
Or circling bands their ancient chief defend,
A long, bright lance his wary foe beheld,
And snatched it glittering on the bloody field;
Swift through the hero's side he forced the steel;
Pierced to the heart, the aged warrior fell;
There lay, a corse, bespread with purple stains,
The form, that triumphed on a hundred plains.
On Ridgefield's hills, to shame to virtue dead,
Thus dastard bands the foe inglorious fled;
When Wooster singly braved the deathful ground,
Fired hosts in vain, and met the fatal wound.
In dangers born, to arms in childhood trained,
From Gallia's heroes many a palm he gained;
With freedom's sacred flame serenely glowed
For justice armed, and sought the field for GOD;
With steady zeal his nation's interest loved;
(No terror touched him, and no injury moved)
Far in the front, with dauntless bosom bled,
And crowned the honors of his hoary head.
Bent over his foe, the lovely Carmi stood,
And viewed, with tears of grief, his bursting blood;
And thus -- Unhappy sire, he sadly cried --
Perhaps thy monarch's joy, thy nation's pride. --
How like my father's bend^ thy hoary brow^?
His limbs, his countenance, and his lo^ks of snow,
All in thy venerable face I (...) --
Perhaps the parent of a son like me --
He spoke; and fiercely wheeled his bloody sword,
Spr^ng to the ^^ght* and many a (...) go^'d;
His voice, his eyes the joyful host inspire,
And through the sweetness flames a dreadful fire.
Active as light, over trembling ranks he hung;
Shouts shook the plains, the frighted forests rung:
Unnumber'd sullen groans were heard around;
Unnumber'd corses clothed the purple ground:
From post to post retired pale Judah's train,
And chief on chief increased the piles of slain.
Dark as an evening cloud, bold Ai was driven,
Gloom'd all the fields, and cast a shade on heaven;
Wide rolled the storm; wide drove the dust along,
And ruin hovered over the flying throng.
Meantime, brave Irad turned his sparkling eyes,
And saw in distant fields the clouds arise;
Sad flight and terror filled the backward plain,
And the foe shouted over his kindred slain.
As, when autumnal clouds the skies deform,
Bursts the wild whirlwind from the gloomy storm;
Hoarse crash the pines; oaks stiffly stubborn fall,
And sudden thunders listening swains appall:
So, winged by Heaven, impetuous Irad flew;
As swift their darling chief the youths pursue;
Whelmed in their path, the falling bands expire,
And crowds of warriors from their steps retire.
Now, where brave Carmi swept the purple ground,
Terrific Irad shook his buckler's round;
Alike in years they seemed, alike in arms,
Of equal stature, and of rival charms:
Nor this, nor that, the dangerous sight can yield;
But each demands the empire of the field.
From the fierce chiefs the wondering bands retreat;
Blows following blows their sounding shields repeat;
Uncleft, each faithful orb the stroke rebounds,
Blunts the keen blade, and intercepts the wounds:
'Till Irad's nimble arm, with sudden wheel,
Through Carmi's side impels the fatal steel,
Pure streams of crimson stain the subject ground,
And the freed soul pervades the gaping wound.
Not that fair pride, that soul-supporting flame,
That lights the splendors of the immortal name;
Not all the bravery nature can impart,
Nor the fond wishes of a virgin's heart,
Nor parents' vows, nor nations' prayers could save,
The young, bright hero from an early grave.
He fell, with beauty's fairest beams adorned,
While foes admired him, and while Irad mourned.
Ah youth, too soon allotted to the tomb;
Oh had kind Heaven dispensed a softer doom,
On thy fair deeds a sweet reward bestowed,
And opened the mansions of the blessed abode!
Thus, where sad Charlestown lifts her hills on high,
Where once gay structures charmed the morning sky,
Ere Howe's barbarian hand in savage fire
Wrapped the tall dome, and whelmed the sacred spire,
In life's fair prime, and new to war's alarms,
Brave Warren sunk, in all the pride of arms.
With me, each generous mind the hour recall,
When pale Columbia mourned her favourite's fall;
Mourned the bright statesman, hero, patriot, fled,
The friend extinguished, and the genius dead;
While he, the darling of the wise, and good,
Sealed his firm truth, and built his name in blood.
Loud as the rushing storm, the din of war
Burst over the plain, and shook the fields afar;
Pierce Irad raised a loud, distinguished cry --
Here see, my friends, their gasping leader lie. --
Through Ai's wide host my sword shall hew your way;
Shall Judah's sons alone desert the day?
Shall Joshua know you fled? to glory rise;
Lift all your arms, and pierce the knave that flies.
The hero spoke* abashed the warriors heard,
Rung their blue arms, and high the standard reared;
Aloft in air a Lion's gloomy form
Lowered, like the darkness of a sullen storm;
Around his head his shaggy terrors frowned,
And his red eyeballs gleam'd destruction round.
Swift from the bearer's hand fierce Irad drew
The banner'd staff, and mid the heathens threw;
With joy they sprang to seize the glittering prize,
And smiles of triumph sparkled in their eyes.
Shame flushed the cheeks of Judah's glowing train;
Their bosoms heaved; their faces flashed disdain;
To seize the shining spoil each warrior sprang;
The combat thickened; and all either rang;
Far rolled the darkness of the dusty cloud;
Loud rose their cries, and armor clash'd aloud.
The blackening tempest Ai undaunted kenn'd,
Pleased to procure, and stubborn to defend;
Scarce Irad's arm could cleave the firm-wedg'd train,
As fierce he strove the standard to regain;
Through ranks on ranks he forced a sanguine way,
Ere his red falchion won the splendid prey;
With smiles, he saw the crimson tumult grow,
And hailed the vengeance gathering over the foe.
From the tall rock great Joshua cast his eyes,
And saw the varying scenes of combat rise.
To Carmi's force beheld pale Judah yield,
And rose to save the triumphs of the field;
But soon new shouts ascend the clouded sky,
His friends now triu^ph, and the Heathens fly.
Now nearer scenes his searching view demand,
Where mighty Caleb rules the warrior band;
Fierce Oran's sword begins inglorious flight,
And his loud clamours animate the fight:
Scarce Caleb's arm the conflict can sustain,
His voice arouse, or deeds inspire, the train;
So fierce the heathens throng the embattled ground,
So thick the warriors fall, the groans resound.
The Hero viewed, and towered the fainting throng,
Swift as a rapid whirlwind, rushed along;
As against a mound, when tempests ride the gale,
The raging river foams along the vale;
Down the wall crumbles, and with dreadful reign
Sweeps a wild deluge on the wasted plain.
Bursting upon the dark embodied throng
Thus the wide ruin Joshua drove along;
Around his course increased the piles of dead,
The brave sunk fighting, and the coward fled.
Now, where unfeeling Oran crushed the slain,
All grim with dust, and red with many a stain,
While smiles of transport gathered on his brow,
His fierce eye sparkling over the bleeding foe,
While high for death he reared his sanguine arm,
And a brave warrior bowed to shun the storm,
Great Joshua's full-orb'd buckler caught the wound,
And lightnings darted from the moony round,
Then, by his hand with rushing thunder thrown,
On Oran's helmet burst a mighty stone,
That, bounding onward against a warrior's side,
Crushed his strong ribs, and shed a plenteous tide.
Stunned by the staggering blow, the leader fell,
Writhed with the pain, and gave a hideous yell;
Furious he lay, with heaving, panting breath,
Rolled up his whitening eyes, and frowned in death;
Cursing the shield, which seized his nimble dart,
And stopped its passage to the warrior's heart:
Swift on his throat descends the indignant blade,
Bursts the black gore, and leaps the grisly head.
Loud over the tumult rose the Hero's cry;
The host all quakes, the distant groves reply --
Rush on, bold heroes, conquest crowns the day;
Now spring to fight, and seize the trembling prey.
This arm on Oran drove the final wound;
Let shouts of triumph shake the hostile ground:
Wealth, and fair peace, the generous contest yields,
And wreaths of glory bloom in bloody fields. --
As in the enkindled wood fierce winds arise,
And storms of fire are blown across the skies;
In blazing trains, the towering pines descend,
And rushing thunders all the forest rend:
So, loud and furious, Israel thronged the fight,
And their blue armor flashed a dreadful light;
Over the pale rear tremendous Joshua hung;
Their gloomy knell his voice terrific rung;
From glowing eyeballs flashed his wrath severe,
Grim Death before him hurled his murdering spear;
Heads, severed from their necks, bestrew'd his way,
And gushing bodies round his footsteps lay.
Meantime Ai's sounding portals wide unfold,
And fierce to combat bursting bands are rolled;
In dreadful pomp ascends the widening train;
Battalions on battalions cloud the plain:
There glowing Zimri wings his rapid force,
And eager thousands darken round his course,
Ai's ghastly sons the smoking walls surveyed,
And wild amaze each pallid front arrayed;
Here lay in gore their brethren, and their sires;
There sunk their mansions in terrific fires;
Before, behind, their foes increased alarms;
They raised one shriek, and dropped their useless arms:
Wherever an opening rank received the day,
Or dust obscure disclosed a glimmering ray,
Borne by light fear, they left the lingering wind,
They fled, they flew, nor cast a look behind;
Often on the spear's protended point they ran;
While thronged resistless, meeting man with man,
Steel stretched to steel, and shield to shield opposed;
On every side the power of Israel closed.
So thick they thronged, no spear could miss its course;
In vain no ^alchion spent its ardent force:
Less heard and less, resounded piercing cries,
And dust besprinkled ceased to fill the skies.
So, when tall navies lift imperial sails,
And hope the indulgence of propitious gales,
When the cold north's fierce wind the main deform,
And, filled with thunders, rolls the raging storm,
Heaved from the bottom, foaming billows rise,
And climb, and climb, and roar against the skies;
Over shivered masts unroll the surging waves,
And the pale sailors plunge in watery graves.
Swift as a whirlwind, over the southern plain,
Impetuous Zimri drove the Hivite train:
With prosperous course, they sped their hasty flight,
Sunk in the wood, and vanished from the sight.
And now, obedient to the Chief's command,
Round the tall standard thronged each wearied band;
A smile of transport every face adorned,
Their wounds unheeded, and the dead scarce mourned.
Nor knew fair Irad how his parent lay,
But, fired with glory, steered his careless way;
Near the great Chief he moved with conscious grace,
And conscious blushes crimsoned over his face;
When, pale and ghastly, on the bloody ground,
Stained with black dust, and pierced with many a wound,
Stiff gore besprinkling all his locks of snow,
And a cold cloud around his reverend brow,
Hezron appeared: at once his nerves congealed;
His frozen lips a dumb, dead silence sealed;
A moveless statue, over the sire he hung,
Nor streaming tears released his marbled tongue.
Then round the corse impassioned arms he threw,
And washed the clotted gore in filial dew;
Glu'd to the form with strong embraces lay,
And kissed, with quivering lips, the senseless clay.
At length the Chief, soft pity in his eyes,
Reached his kind hand, and forced the Youth to rise:
Four mournful warriors Hezron's body bore,
And their eyes glisten'd with a tender shower.
The sun declined; besmeared with dust, and blood,
Slow over the plain the wearied squadrons trod;
When, fair as Phosphor leads the morning train,
Dressed in new beams, and beauteous from the main;
Crowned with white flowers, that breathed a rich perfume
And clothed in loveliness, of gayest bloom,
Rose in soft splendour Caleb's youngest pride,
A thousand maidens following at her side.
In snow-white robes of flowing silk arrayed,
First of the virgins walked the blushing maid;
Her long, dark hair loose-floated in the wind;
Her glowing eyes confessed the etherial mind;
A wreath of olive flourished in her hand;
A silver lyre obeyed her soft command;
With sounds harmonious rang the warbled strings,
And thus the maids, and thus Selima sings.
Who comes from Ai, adorned with gay attire,
Bright as the splendour of the morning fire?
Fair as the spring, ascends the lovely form,
And dreadful as the blaze, that lights the storm!
Ye maids, with flowerets strew the conqueror's way,
Strike the loud harp, and sing the dreadful day!
To Irad's steps the matchless fair-one came,
Her breast quick-panting, and her cheeks on flame;
Her beauteous hand the verdant crown displayed;
Graceful he bowed, and placed it on his head.
Slow to her train the trembling fair withdrew,
The charmed youths following with a moveless view,
So, winged with light, and dressed in strange array,
The mantling glory of the rising day,
With sweet complacence, such as angels show
To souls unprison'd from this world of woe,
Parted soft-smiling from our general sire
Some bright-ey'd Virtue, of the heavenly choir,
Far in the solar walk, with wanderous flight,
The form celestial lessened on his fight.
Again the youth his wonted life regained;
A transient sparkle in his eye obtained;
A rising glow his tender thoughts confessed,
And the soft motions of his melting breast.
But soon dark glooms the feeble smiles overspread;
Like morn's gay hues, the fading splendour fled;
Returning anguish froze his feeling soul,
Deep sighs burst forth, and tears began to roll.
THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK VII.

ARGUMENT.
Evening described. Irad's dream. He goes out to the walls of Ai. His
lamentation for his father. Reflections on the fate of Ai. Appearance of an
army. Irad returns in h^ste, and alarms the Camp. Joshua, at his request,
allots him a body of forces, with whom he goes out to attack the Heathens.
Battle by the burning of Ai, between Hazor, etc. and Israel. Irad's exploits.
He kills Adnor, and pursues Samlah to the eastern part of the host. Uzal.
Shelumiel. Jabin's character, and exploits. He kills Shammah, and Seraiah.
Jobab. Confusion of the Israelites. Irad returns, and rallies them. He attacks
Jobab, with success. Kindling of the neighboring forest separates the
combatants.

The scene of this battle is partly on the plain east of Ai, partly in the
forest still eastward, and partly northward of the forest.

THE CONQUEST OF CANAAN. BOOK VII.
OVER the wide world immeasurably spread,
Night, still and gloomy, cast a solemn shade.
In heavens half-clouded stars unfrequent hung;
Scarce heard, the blast with mournful murmurs rung;
Above tall, eastern hills, the moon's pale eye
Looked sad, and dreadful, from the cheerless sky:
Her cold, wan face, half-hid behind a cloud,
That wrapped the mountains in a sable shroud,
With feeble luster streaked the shadowy plains,
And edged her vapoury robes with dismal stains.
All, but the savage race, to sleep retired,
And the last gleams of western skies expired.
Stretched in his tent, unhappy Irad lay,
And sad oblivion bore his toils away.
In that still hour, when rapt on eagle-wings,
To distant climes bewildered fancy springs,
A death-like slumber sealed his tearful eyes,
And thus unreal scenes in vision rise.
Through lonely fields, in russet gloom arrayed,
Lost in mute grief, with weary steps he strayed.
A shadowy light, like evening's dusky ray,
Spread over the world, and formed a twilight day.
Before his wandering path, a northern grove
Shed midnight round, and pierced the clouds above:
Slow waved the tall, dark pines: a hollow sound
Rolled through the wood, and shook the autumnal ground.
Dull-murmuring fell the sullen, swelling streams,
Lulling to sleep, and blue in glimmering beams.
With broad, black horrors over its bosom spread,
An eastern mountain reared its shaggy head;
High hung the hoary cliff; the cedars height,
Less seen, and less, withdrew beyond the sight.
Strange unknown scenes the regions wild display,
And solitary music slowly die^ away.
From the thick grove, in dark-brown robes revealed,
A form stalked solemn over the shuddering field;
Of other worlds he seemed; nor cast an eye
On the brown plain, or on the gloomy sky.
Regardless of the scenes that round him mourned,
On Irad's path his sad, slow steps he turned;
Pale stood the Youth; the stately shape drew nigh;
Gash'd was his cheek, and fixed his lofty eye;
Like a light flame, low hung his beard of snow,
And death's cold terrors hovered on his brow.
'Twas Hezron's self. With weak, but solemn sound,
As sullen graves beneath the foot resound,
His voice began -- On fate's dark verge I stand,
Whence thickening dangers roll across thy land.
Night wraps the world; approaching storms arise,
Hang over thy race, and cloud the southern skies.
My mouldering bones a colder night detains,
Closed in the tomb, and bound in icy chains:
But the winged spirit fairer climes surround,
And heaven un^ol^^ng bids her songs resound.
Faintly he spoke. By strange, immortal spell,
His wounds grew smooth, his sightless garments fell:
His pallid face a sudden beauty fired,
And with strong life his changing eye inspired;
Over his white robes a purple splendour ray'd;
Long glittering pinions loosely round him played;
In dreadful pomp, sublime the Vision stood,
And living fragrance breathed along the wood.
At once the hero, startled, raised his head;
Still was his tent, and all the tumult fl^d;
Again to sleep he closed his wearied eyes,
And broken slumbers over his toils arise.
Sudden, his name re-echoing from the walls*
A wild, and visionary murmur calls --
Irad awake; my voice thine ear invades,
From the dark mansions of imprisoned shades;
In southern plains the clarion's thunders rise,
And shouts of triumph fill the rending skies.
Swift from his couch the Youth astonished rose,
(While every vein the dreadful murmur froze)
With active hand his arms around him braced;
With nimble feet the glimmering champain passed,
And towered Ai's flames, that raged with awful force,
Suspense, but fearless, steered his lonely course.
Still over his head the airy phantom hung;
Irad awake -- the voice unreal rung:
Sad grief, and anxious doubt his thoughts oppressed,
But love's soft whispers still disturbed his breast.
Now solemn silenee sailed along the air;
No bird complained; no ^choing voice was near;
Save the slow murmur of the passing gale,
That swept the plain, and sounded through the vale.
The flames dark-glimmer'd on the hero's shield,
And cast long shadows over the pallid field:
Round the dread scenes he turned regardless eyes,
And thus began, with intermingled sighs --
And art thou fled forever? this thine end,
Thou best of parents, and thou surest friend?
And could thou fall, a prey to murdering war?
What cruel demon drove my feet so far?
Was no kind angel hovering over the throng?
Where looked the Power, thy virtue served so long?
Thy soul so pure -- thy life so firmly just --
Scarce Heaven's own law could more demand from dust.
Why, O thou righteous Mind? but cease my tongue,
Nor blame the dread decree, that cannot wrong.
Mine the sole fault -- and mine the single blame --
Wild with the magic of that phantom, fame.
Did thou for this the guilty shield bestow,
To leave thee naked to the fatal blow?
Did thou for this the sword accursed impart,
That should have plunged beneath the murderer's heart?
Far other love, far other faithful cares
Nursed my young limbs, and watched my rising years;
My early steps, from pleasure's slippery road,
Lured with soft smiles, and led them up to GOD;
Thy own bright actions prompting to pursue,
To virtue charmed me, and to glory drew;
With Joshua's self my wishes forced to vie.
Boast of mankind, and chosen of the Sky.
Pale, in the visions of the guilty bed,
Thy form affrights me, and thine eyes upbraid.
There scenes of dire distress thy words unroll,
Doomed for my life, and opening on my soul.
Or does thy mind its loved employ pursue,
To guard from ill, and hidden dangers show?
Perhaps thy thoughts, beyond the silent tomb,
Watch, as in life, thy nation's secret doom;
Some rushing fate unknown discern afar,
Some threatening ambush, or some wasting war.
Perhaps the first of maids thy care demands,
And claims her safety from aerial hands.
Ah! knew the fair what crimes to me belong,
Her lovely voice had spared the applauding song;
A breast more pure her melting arms embraced,
And the bright garland worthier temples graced.
Thus spoke the chief, when now his steps were nigh
Ai's awful flames, that waved across the sky;
All pale, and gloomy, climbed the dreadful blaze,
And smoky volumes curled above the rays;
A dreary gleam enrolled the shady ground,
And the brown land-scape faintly rose around.
Touched by the solemn scene, the hero cried --
Where hapless Ai! is now thy towery pride?
Where now thy manly sons, whose sinewy arms
Rose, a strong bulwark against impending harms
Where now the heaven-topp'd spire? the gilded wall?
Thy kings, thy heroes? whelmed in ruin all --
Destruction's clouds sailed blackening over thy light,
And wide oblivion's never-ending night.
Where yon tall dome shoots forth the greedy flame,
Perhaps some hero hoped a deathless name.
Often when returned from war, his tender race
Climbed his fond knee and asked the sweet embrace:
Often, with a parent's glistening eye, he viewed
His face, his virtues in their forms renewed.
Perhaps some daughter, darling of his care,
Beamed, like Selima fairest of the fair:
